For the Morning Prayer, one should wash his face, hands, and feet, and only afterwards, may he pray. If he is far from water, he may clean his hands only and then pray.
All the ritually impure need only wash their hands in order to pray, just like those not in such a state. Even if they are able to immerse themselves [in a <i>mikveh</i>] and ascend from their impure state, this immersion is not required [for prayer].<br>We have already explained that Ezra decreed that only one who has had a seminal emission is prohibited from Torah study until he has immersed himself. The Rabbinical Court [that existed afterwards] decreed that [this applies] even to prayer, i.e., such a person alone should not pray until he immerses himself.<br>These decrees were not put into effect because of questions of ritual purity and impurity, but rather to ensure that the Torah scholars would not overindulge in marital intimacy with their wives. Therefore, they instituted ritual immersion only for one who has a seminal emission, thereby excepting him from the other ritually impure.
Therefore, at the time of this decree, it was said that even a זב who had a seminal emission, a menstruating woman who emitted semen, and a woman who saw traces of menstrual blood after relations, required immersion to recite the <i>Shema</i> and to pray because of the seminal emission, despite [the fact that they remained] ritually impure.<br>This is reasonable, since this immersion was not a matter of purity, but a result of the decree so that they would not constantly be with their wives.<br>This decree regarding prayer was also abolished, since it was not universally accepted by the Jewish people, and the community at large was unable to observe it.
It is widespread custom in Shin'ar and Spain that one who has had a seminal emission does not pray until he has washed his whole body in water, [based on the command]: "Prepare to meet your God, Israel" (Amos 4:12 .<br>To whom does this refer? To a healthy person or to a sick person who engaged in intimacy, but a sick person who has had an accidental emission is exempt from bathing and there is no such custom regarding this matter. Similarly, there is no such custom regarding a זב who has a seminal emission and a menstruating woman who emits semen. Rather, they should clean themselves, wash their hands, and pray.
The proper covering of one's nakedness: What is implied?<br>Even if one covers his genitalia in the fashion necessary for the recital of the <i>Shema</i>, he may not pray until he covers his heart. If one did not - or was unable to - cover his heart, as long as he covered his nakedness when he prayed, he has fulfilled his obligation. However, <i>a priori,</i> he should not do so.
The purity of the place of prayer: What is implied?<br>One should not pray in a place of filth, a bathhouse, a latrine or garbage heap. [Similarly, one should not pray] in a place that is not presumed to be clean until he checks it.<br>The general rule is that one should not pray in any place in which one would not recite the <i>Shema</i>. [Thus,] just as one separates oneself from excreta, urine, a foul odor, a corpse and the sight of nakedness for <i>Kri'at Shema</i>, so too, he should separate himself for <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.
[The following rules apply] when one finds excreta in his place [of prayer.] Since he transgressed by not checking the place before he began to pray, he must pray again in a clean place.<br>[The following rules apply] when one finds excreta while he is in the midst of prayer: If he can walk forwards such that [the feces] will be left four cubits behind him, he should do so. If not, he should move to the side. If he is unable [to do the latter], he should stop praying.<br>The great Sages would refrain from praying in a house in which there was beer or brine at the time of its foaming because of the foul odor, even though it was a clean place.
Things that might bother and distract him: What is implied?<br>One who must relieve himself should not pray. Whenever anyone who must relieve himself prays, his prayer is an abomination and he must pray again after he relieves himself.<br>If a person can restrain himself for the length of time that it takes to walk a <i>parsah</i>, his prayer is considered prayer.<br>Nevertheless, <i>a priori</i>, one should not pray until he has checked himself very well, checked his intestines, rid himself of phlegm and mucus and any [other] bothersome thing. [Only] afterwards should he pray.
[The following rules apply to] one who burps, yawns, or sneezes during prayer: Should a person do so voluntarily, it is deprecating. [However,] if the person checked himself before he prayed and did so against his will, it is of no consequence.<br>If saliva comes up during prayer, one should cause it to be absorbed into his tallit or clothes. If he is bothered by this, he may throw it behind him with his hand in order that he not be bothered during his prayer and be distracted.<br>If one passes gas unwittingly during prayer, he should wait until the gas subsides and return to his prayer.
One who desires to pass gas from below and is bothered exceedingly, to the extent that he cannot restrain himself, should walk back four cubits, [expel the gas], and then wait until the gas subsides. He should say:<br>Master of all the world, You created us with many orifices and ducts. Our shame and disgrace is apparent and known before You. Shame and disgrace during our life, worm-eaten and decaying in our death.<br>He then returns to his place and prays.
When urine flows on a person's legs during <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he should wait until the flow ceases, and return to the place at which he stopped. If he waited the time necessary to complete his <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he should return to the beginning.
Similarly, one who urinates should wait the length of time it takes to walk four cubits, and then pray. After one has prayed, he should wait this length of time before urinating, in order to make a distinction following the words of prayer.
Proper intention: What is implied?<br>Any prayer that is not [recited] with proper intention is not prayer. If one prays without proper intention, he must repeat his prayers with proper intention.<br>One who is in a confused or troubled state may not pray until he composes himself. Therefore, one who comes in from a journey and is tired or irritated is forbidden to pray until he composes himself. Our Sages taught that one should wait three days until he is rested and his mind is settled, and then he may pray.
What is meant by [proper] intention?<br>One should clear his mind from all thoughts and envision himself as standing before the Divine Presence. Therefore, one must sit a short while before praying in order to focus his attention and then pray in a pleasant and supplicatory fashion.<br>One should not pray as one carrying a burden who throws it off and walks away. Therefore, one must sit a short while after praying, and then withdraw.<br>The pious ones of the previous generations would wait an hour before praying and an hour after praying. They would [also] extend their prayers for an hour.
A person who is drunk should not pray, because he cannot have proper intention. If he does pray, his prayer is an abomination. Therefore, he must pray again when he is clear of his drunkenness. One who is slightly inebriated should not pray, [but] if he prays, his prayer is prayer.<br>When is a person considered as drunk? When he is unable to speak before a king. [In contrast,] a person who is slightly inebriated is able to speak before a king without becoming confused. Nevertheless, since he drank a <i>revi'it</i> of wine, he should not pray until his wine has passed from him.
Similarly, one should not stand to pray in the midst of laughter or irreverent behavior, nor in the midst of a conversation, argument or anger, but rather in the midst of words of Torah.<br>[However, one should not stand to pray] in the midst of a judgment or a [difficult] halachic issue, even though these are words of Torah, lest one's mind be distracted by the halachah in question. Rather, [one should pray] in the midst of words of Torah that do not require deep concentration, e.g., laws that have already been accepted.
Before reciting occasional prayers, e.g., the <i>Musaf</i> Prayer of Rosh Chodesh or the prayers of the festivals, one must review his prayers lest he make mistakes in them.<br>A person walking in a dangerous place, e.g., a place frequented by wild animals or bandits, when the time of the <i>Amidah</i> arrives, should recite [only] a single blessing:<br>The needs of Your people, Israel, are great and their knowledge is limited. May it be Your will, O God, our Lord, that You will provide each and every one with a sufficient livelihood, and give to each individual all that he lacks. And that which is good in Your eyes, You should do. Blessed are You, God, the One who hears prayer.<br>He may recite it as he walks on the road. [However,] if he is able to stop [to recite it], he should. When he arrives at a settlement and his mind is composed, he should recite a proper <i>Amidah</i> of 19 blessings.

Chapter 5

A person who prays must be careful to tend to [the following] eight matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The specific laws regarding these eight categories are discussed by the Rambam in the ensuing halachot of this chapter.</i> [However,] if he is pressured,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">and unable to carry out any or all of these prerequisites.</i> confronted by circumstances beyond his control,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., ill, as explained by the Rambam in Halachah 2.</i> or transgresses<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even if one transgresses intentionally.</i> and does not attend to one them, they are not<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2.</i> of absolute necessity.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., one is not required to repeat the prayer or compensate for its recitation.<br>In the previous chapter, the Rambam listed five categories which are of absolute necessity for prayer. A person who fails to fulfill any of those requirements must repeat his prayers. These eight categories are לכתחילה (at the outset) necessary for the <i>Amidah</i>, but, do not disqualify the prayer if they were not tended to.<br>The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> points out that even Chapter 4 refers to certain actions which were only problematic לכתחילה, such as burping and sneezing. Nevertheless, they are included in that chapter because, from a topical perspective, it is more appropriate to discuss them within the context of the general categories mentioned there.</i> They are:<br> 1) standing;<br>2) facing the Temple;<br>3)<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3.</i> preparation of his body;<br>4)<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4.</i> proper clothing;<br>5)<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5.</i> proper place;<br>6)<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6-8.</i> control of his voice;<br>7)<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 9.</i> bowing;<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 10-12.</i> and<br>8) prostration.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 13-15.</i>
Standing: What is implied?<br>[Generally,] one should pray only while standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 1, prayer is called עבודה - service. Thus, it can be compared to the sacrifices in the Temple which are also referred to by that term. In regard to both these services, Deuteronomy 10:8 states: "...To stand before God and serve Him" (<i>Berachot</i> 30a, <i>Tur, Orach Chayim</i> 98).<br>Indeed, the term <i>Amidah</i> - (lit. "standing") is used to refer to the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> because of this requirement. (See <i>Soferim</i> 16:12.)</i> [Thus,] a person sitting in a boat or in a carriage, if able to stand, should do so;<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">However, unlike one riding an animal, he is not obligated to sit if he is able to stand (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i> if not, he may sit in his place and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30a relates a difference of opinion among the Sages whether it is preferable to recite one's prayers early, before the desired time so that one can stand during <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> or whether one should set out on a journey in a boat or carriage and pray at the appropriate time even though one will not be able to stand.<br>From this discussion, we may conclude that, under these circumstances, it is acceptable for one to remain seated during the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> if doing so will contribute to one's ability to concentrate.</i><br>A person who is ill<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">and would have to strain to stand</i> may pray even while lying on his side,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 2:2 which relates that one may recite the <i>Shema</i> lying on one's side, but not lying on one's back.</i> provided he is able to have the proper intention.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., One who is ill should pray only when he is able to concentrate. However, if he is unable to concentrate, it is better that he should not pray (Rabbenu Manoach).<br><i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> relates that Rashi was accustomed not to pray when he was ill.</i> Similarly, one who is thirsty or hungry<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">to the extent that he cannot concentrate</i> is considered as one who is ill.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">and is not required to pray. The commentaries point to the statement of the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 5:1) that one in discomfort should not pray as the source for this halachah.</i> [Therefore,] if he is able to concentrate properly he should pray. If not, he should not pray until he has eaten or drunk.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It is questionable whether this halachah is accepted at present, when, after the fact, prayer with a lesser level of concentration is accepted (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 101) and <i>siddurim</i> are commonly available (See <i>ibid.</i> 9:3).</i><br>One riding an animal should not descend [from the animal]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">even if he has someone to hold his animal, - <i>Berachot</i> 30a mentions opinions which require one to descend in this instance, however, the accepted view is...</i> - even if he has someone to hold his animal. Rather, he should sit in his place and pray so his mind will be settled.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Even if someone were holding his animal, the person would be preoccupied with worries about his animal and, therefore, would be unable to concentrate on his <i>Amidah</i>.</i>
Facing the Temple: What is implied?<br>A person standing in the Diaspora should face <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30a explains that this is derived from King Solomon's prayer at the dedication of the Temple (I Kings 8:48 : "...and they will pray to You towards their land."</i><br>One standing in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> should face Jerusalem.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as implied by I Kings 8:44: "...and they will pray to God towards the city that You have chosen" (<i>Berachot</i>, <i>ibid.</i>).</i><br>One standing in Jerusalem should face the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as implied by II Chronicles 6:24 (which gives a slightly different account of Solomon's prayer): "...and they will pray towards this House" (<i>ibid.</i>).<br>The Talmud (<i>ibid.</i>) also relates:<br>Rav Avin... said: What is implied by the verse: "Your neck is like the tower of David built with turrets (תלפיות) (Song of Songs 4:4 ."? A hill (תל) to which all mouths (פיות) turn."<br>Thus, the Temple is called תל פיות, the hill to which all mouths turn in prayer.</i><br>One standing in the Temple should face the Holy of Holies.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as implied by I Kings 8:35: "...and they will pray towards this place." <i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) concludes: "Thus, all of Israel direct their hearts towards one place." (See also the commentary to Chapter 1, Halachah 3.)</i><br>A blind person, one who is<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">in a new place and is...</i> unable to determine direction, or one travelling in a boat<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">who cannot face whatever direction he desires lest he fall</i> should direct his heart towards the Divine Presence<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as implied by I Kings 8:44: "...and they will pray to God" (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 4:5), the Rambam explains that one should imagine himself facing the Holy of Holies in the Temple.</i>
The preparation of one's body: What is implied?<br>When one stands in prayer, he should place his feet together side by side.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 10b quotes Ezekiel 1:7 which describes the angels as standing in the following manner: "And their feet are a straight foot." Therefore, when praying, we place our feet together in order to appear as angels - those expert in praising God.</i> He should set his eyes downwards as if he is looking at the ground, and his heart upwards as if he is standing in Heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 105b relates:<br>Rav Chiya and Rabbi Shimon bar Rebbe were sitting together. One of them opened the conversation and said: One praying should set his eyes downwards, as [I Kings 9:3] states: "And my eyes and my heart were there all the days" (i.e., towards the land of Israel and set downwards because of the presence of the <i>Shechinah</i>).The other said that one's eyes should be pointed upwards, as [Eichah 3:41] states: "Let us lift up our hearts with our hands [towards God in Heaven]."Rabbi Yishmael, the son of Rabbi Yossi, came and asked them what they were doing. They answered that they were discussing the <i>Amidah</i>. He [Rabbi Yishmael] said: My father said: One praying should set his eyes downwards and his heart upwards in order to fulfill both of those verses.<br>His hands should be resting on his heart - <i>Shabbat</i> 10a relates: "Ravvah threw off his cloak, clasped his hands together and prayed like a servant before his master."</i><br>His hands should be resting on his heart, with the right hand clasped over the left hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The right side always represents the notion of lovingkindness; the left side, stern justice. Therefore, we hold our hands such that the right overpowers the left: i.e., lovingkindness overpowers stern judgement.<br>The right side is given prominence over the left in many other contexts because of this idea. (See the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 2:4.)</i> He should stand like a servant before his master,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Maharal (<i>Netiv Ha'avodah</i>, Chapter 6), in his commentary on <i>Berachot</i> 10b quoted above, explains that placing our hands and feet together is a statement of absolute dependence on God. We demonstrate that we are neither able to move to our desired place, nor to act with our hands as we wish. In this way, we show how we are totally given over to His service, as a servant who has no independent existence of his own.</i> in fear, awe, and dread.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30b bases this on Psalms 2:11: "And you shall serve God in fear."</i> He should not rest his hand on his hips [during the <i>Amidah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This appears to be based on Rav Yitzchak Alfasi's interpretation of <i>Berachot</i> 24b. The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> explains that such a position conveys an attitude of irreverance. For similar reasons, it is forbidden to lean on anything during the <i>Amidah</i>.</i>
Proper clothing: What is implied?<br>One should adjust his clothing and make himself neat and presentable before [praying], as [implied by Psalms 29:2]: "They bow to God in resplendent holiness."<br>One<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30b relates that, on the basis of this verse, Rav Yehudah would adjust his clothes before praying.</i> should not pray wearing [only] his undershirt,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of אפונדתו is based on the Rambam's commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Berachot</i> 9:5, which defines that term as the undergarment worn to collect perspiration, in order that one's outer garments remain fresh. Others explain that it refers to a money belt.</i> bareheaded,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soferim</i> 14:15 states that one may not utter God's name with his head uncovered. (See also <i>Shabbat</i> 155b.) "One praying must stand with his feet together and cover his head as one standing before the king" (<i>Zohar, Va'etchanan</i> 260b).</i> or barefoot<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 10a relates: "Ravvah bar Rav Huna put on fine shoes and then prayed. He said: 'Prepare to meet your God, Israel' (Amos 4:12 ."<br>The Rambam does not mention the source of these halachot or the verse from Amos. Rather, he cites the verse in Psalms, which may be understood as producing a halachah that is more subjective in nature, dependent on the commonly accepted rules of etiquette.</i> - if it is the custom of the people of that place<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">However, where it is normal practice to walk barefoot, as in particularly hot climates, it is permissible to pray that way (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> to stand before their most respected people<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This introduces a subjective element into these laws. The definition of proper clothing is dependent on local custom, i.e., how people dress when they want to make an impression on the respected people in one's community. See also the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 91:5.<br>Both the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> mention this idea in regard to wearing a hat and require one to do so during the <i>Amidah</i> if this is the usual way people present themselves.</i> with shoes.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">with shoes.</i><br> In all places,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the following rule in this halachah is in no way dependent on local custom, but applicable in all cases.</i> one should not hold <i>tefillin</i> in his hand or a <i>Sefer Torah</i> in his arms during the <i>Amidah</i>, since he will worry about them.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">lest they fall (Rashi, <i>Berachot</i> 23b).</i> [Similarly,] one should not hold utensils or money in his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (ibid) states: "A knife, money, a plate, and a loaf of bread are like them (i.e., like the <i>Sefer Torah</i> and <i>tefillin</i>)." Rashi explains that in these instances as well, one will fear that they will fall and injure him or become lost or ruined.<br>Based on Rashi's statements, there are authorities who rule that a person may hold an object which would not present a danger or causes worry if it falls, e.g., a notebook. Rabbenu Yonah, however, forbids the holding of any object except the lulav, which is explicitly mentioned in the Talmud. This is also the position of <i>Terumat Hadeshen</i>. (Even according to the latter view, one may hold a prayerbook.)<br><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 96:1) rules that one may also hold other objects (in line with Rashi's position), but states that the optimum manner of performing the mitzvah is not to hold anything, so that his hands may be clasped together over his heart, as the Rambam mentions in Halachah 4.</i> However, he may pray while holding his lulav on Sukkot,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 41b relates that Rav Ashi would pray with his lulav in his hand.</i> since it is the commandment of the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is the mitzvah of the day, it is dear to him and caring for it is not considering an encumberance that will disturb one's concentration (Rashi).</i><br>If one is carrying a burden of less than four <i>kabbim</i> on his head<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah quotes <i>Bava Metzia</i> 105b. See our commentary on Chapter 4, Halachah 6, for the modern equivalent of a <i>kav</i>.</i> when the time for the <i>Amidah</i> arrives, he should throw it over his shoulder and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., holding a burden of this size will not distrub one's concentration.</i> If it is larger than four <i>kabbim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">holding it during prayer will be a distraction. Hence,...</i> he should place it on the ground and then pray.<br>It is customary for all Sages and their students to pray only when wrapped in a <i>tallit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"></b> <i>Shabbat</i> 10a relates that Rav Kahana would wrap himself in a <i>tallit</i> and pray. <i>Ta'anit</i> 20a also tells of Nakdimon ben Gurion, who when praying in the Holy Temple for rain, "wrapped himself in a <i>tallit</i> and stood to pray."<br><b>In <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:11, the Rambam writes: "It is a great disgrace for a Torah scholar to pray without being wrapped in his <i>tallit</i>."<br></b></i>
Proper place: What is implied?<br>One should stand in a low place<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 10b bases this law on the premise, "there is no loftiness before God, as [Psalms 130:1] states: 'From the depths, I call out to you, God.'”</i> and turn his face towards the wall.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 5b bases this law on Isaiah 38:2: "And Chizkiyahu turned his face towards the wall and prayed."<br>In his responsa, the Rambam explains that the intent of this law is that one should not have anything before him that will disrupt his concentration on the <i>Amidah</i>. (See <i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 90)<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90:21) discuss this concept. Among their conclusions are that people do not constitute a separation between a person praying and the wall, nor do small objects less than 10 handbreadths (approximately 80 centimeters) tall.</i> Also, one should open windows or doors<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 31a states "A person should always pray in a house with windows."<br>In his responsum, the Rambam mentions that this halachah applies only to a house, as explicitly mentioned in the Talmud and not necessarily to a synagogue. However, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) quotes the <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. II, p. 251a), which requires a synagogue to have 12 windows, which correspond to the 12 <i>seraphim</i> in the Heavenly Court. He also quotes this law in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90:4).<br>Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 34b) explains that windows are an aid to concentration, as one looks heavenward and is humbled. Rabbenu Yonah suggests that the light has a calming effect, allowing one to concentrate on his prayers.</i> that face Jerusalem and pray opposite them,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">in keeping with the obligation to face Jerusalem mentioned in Halachah 3.</i> as [Daniel 6:11] states: "...and he had windows open in his room facing Jerusalem."<br>A<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The verse concludes: "...and he kneeled on his knees three times a day and prayed."</i> person should establish a fixed place where he always prays.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 6b states: "Anyone who establishes a fixed place for his prayer will be assisted by the God of Abraham." Abraham is mentioned because, as can be inferred from Genesis 19:27, he had a fixed place for prayer.<br>The Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 4:4, states that even in a synagogue, a person should have a fixed place for prayer and should not move from place to place.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 90:19 quotes this law. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> adds that within four cubits (approx. six feet) of one's place is also considered acceptable, since it is impossible to be in exactly the same spot at all times. Therefore, a person who finds a colleague sitting in one's place in the synagogue, may pray within four cubits of his place. This is preferable to embarrassing the person sitting in his seat, who might very well be a visitor and not be aware of which seats are occupied by regular congregants.</i> One should not pray in a destroyed building,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah is derived within the context of the following narrative (<i>Berachot</i> 3a):<br>Rabbi Yossi said: Once I was walking on the road and I entered one of the destroyed buildings of Jerusalem in order to pray. Eliyahu [the Prophet] of blessed memory came and waited for me by the entrance until I had finished praying.After I finished my prayer, he greeted me: "Peace unto you, Rebbe."I replied to him: "Peace unto you, Rebbe and teacher."He asked me: "My son, why did you enter this destroyed building?"I answered him: "To pray."He told me: "You should have prayed in the road."<br>The Talmud mentions that one should never enter a destroyed building on any account. Nevertheless, since the Talmud made this statement regarding Rabbi Yossi's prayer, the Rambam teaches the laws concerning destroyed buildings here in <i>Hilchot Tefillah</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> nor [should one pray] behind a synagogue, unless he turns his face towards the synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 6b states: "Anyone praying behind a synagogue is called wicked,... this applies only when he faces away from the synagogue, but if he faces the synagogue there is no problem."<br>Rashi (in <i>Berachot</i> 6b) states that by facing away from the synagogue when one prays, one appears to deny God who is worshiped within. Therefore, even if facing the synagogue requires one to turn away from Jerusalem, it is desirable.<br><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Eruvin</i> 18b, explains that this refers to a person standing on the side of the door facing away from the synagogue, and thus towards the opposite direction of those worshipping inside. Therefore, he must simply turn the other way and solve the problem. <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> suggests that “behind a synagogue” may even refer to standing on the side of the synagogue and looking away.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90:7) mentions all these possibilities and rules that one should refrain from praying in any of these places.</i><br>It is forbidden to sit down next to someone in the midst of the <i>Amidah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 31b refers to Chanah's prayers for a son and her subsequent remarks to Eli, the priest: "I am the woman who stood here with you" (I Samuel 1:26 . This verses indicates that Eli, as well as Chanah, was standing at the time.<br>Rabbenu Yonah also mentions that this applies only to a person sitting idly, but one studying or reciting the <i>Shema</i> need not worry and may continue to sit.</i> or to pass in front of him,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">for walking in front of someone will disrupt that person's concentration.<br>Rabbenu Yonah (commenting on <i>Berachot</i> 27a) explains that this refers only to "in front of him," but not to the side.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 102:5) points out that the <i>Zohar</i> (<i>Parshat Chayei Sarah</i>) mentions that passing on the side is also forbidden.The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 102:5 mentions a practical application of this halachah:<br>If one completes his <i>Amidah</i> when a colleague is praying behind him, he is forbidden from taking his three steps [backwards] until the person behind him finishes his <i>Amidah</i>. Were he to [step backwards], he is like one passing before another in the midst of the <i>Amidah</i>. One must be especially careful in this, even if the last one began his <i>Amidah</i> after the first person.</i> except at a distance of four cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">which is not likely to distract one's colleague.</i>
One should not stand in a place three or more handbreadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A handbreadth is the size of a fist: approximately 8 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>. Based on the principle of <i>lavud</i>, anything lower than three handbreadths is considered to be part of the ground itself.</i> and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 10b mentions this and the following laws within the context of the requirement to pray in a low place stated in the previous halachah.</i> [Similarly, he should not pray while standing] on a bed, bench, or chair.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The special mention of these objects requires explanation, since presumably, they would be included in the general category of a place three handbreadths or more high. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90) quotes Rabbi Yitzchak Abuhav as suggesting that it is forbidden to pray while standing on these objects even if they are less than three handbreadths high. Even at this low height, a person may be unable to concentrate out of fear that he might fall.<br>The <i>Prisha</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90) maintains that it is unlikely that a person would fear falling from such a low height and offers a different rationale. He explains that these objects constitute a separation between the person and the ground. In the Temple, the sacrificial service could be performed only while one was standing directly on the ground. Therefore, our prayers must be recited in a similar manner.</i><br>A raised platform that has a surface area of four cubits by four cubits which is the [minimum] size of a house,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This measure is also significant in regard to the laws of property and the prohibition of transferring from one domain to another on the Sabbath.</i> is considered like an attic.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is considered a space in its own right rather than a raised area within a larger space.</i> Thus, one is permitted to pray there.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Migdal Oz</i> points out that on the basis of this halachah, the leader of the congregation often stands on a raised platform in the synagogue in order that he be heard by all the congregants.</i> Similarly, if it is surrounded by walls, even if it is not four cubits by four cubits,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">though it lacks the necessary size, its walls cause it to be considered as an independent entity. Hence,...</i> one may pray there, since its height is not noticeable, because it constitutes an area unto itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., because of the walls, no one will worry about falling despite the height.</i>
Craftsmen<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">who are hired to work for others by the hour or day, in contrast, to a person working on his own time.</i> working at the top of a tree, or on top of a board<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Berachot</i> 2:4, the Rambam explains the meaning of the word נדבך as follows:<br>The custom of those building a wall of earth (as opposed to bricks or stones) was to set up two large boards of wood and throw the earth in between them. They would pack it tight with wooden tools until it had the shape of a wall, and then tie [the two boards together.] Afterwards, they would remove the boards from the structure they had built...<br>בראש הנדבך means "on top of the wall at the time that they are packing (the earth) between the boards that are called נדבכים."</i> or wall when the time of the <i>Amidah</i> arrives must descend in order to pray,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 16a) contrasts the <i>Shema</i> which these individuals may recite in their places and prayer, which requires that they descend. The difference is that, in the <i>Shema</i>, intense concentration is only required when reciting the first verse, while the entire <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> should be recited with such intention. Hence, it is likely that in such precarious positions, the fear of falling will prevent a person from maintaining his concentration for this prolonged period. See also <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i>, Chapter 2, Halachah 4.</i> and then return to their work.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., though they are hired workers and thus, their time is not their own, nevertheless, they are granted the time to fulfill their fundamental religious obligations in the proper manner. However, after discharging those duties, they must return to work immediately.</i> If they were at the top of an olive or fig tree, they may pray where they are, because of the excessive effort [involved in descending].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 16a differentiates between these trees and those of other species. Rashi explains that the danger of falling from these trees is minimal because of their thick branches, and, therefore, one may stand there firmly and pray.<br>The Rambam, based on the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 2:5, explains that the wide branches make the descent problematical, and, therefore, the workers may pray in the trees themselves.</i><br>What is it that they pray? If they are working for meals alone, they recite three prayers of 19 blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) arrives at this differentiation based on an apparent contradiction between two <i>beraitot</i>, one stating that workers must recite the entire <i>Amidah</i>, and the other stating that the abridged version is sufficient.<br>Those working for daily wages are in effect paid by the hour and must use their time for their employer in the fullest manner possible. In contrast, implicit in an agreement to work for meals is that a high rate of productivity is not expected from such a worker. Hence, he is granted greater leniency.</i> If they are working for wages, they recite "Give us understanding."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the abridged version of the <i>Amidah</i>, discussed in Chapter 2, Halachot 2-4.</i> In either case, they do not lead the congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">nor participate in communal prayer because of the additional time involved. Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 16a) also agrees that this applies even to one working for meals.</i> or lift up their hands [to bless the people].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if the artisan is a <i>Cohen</i> he should not bless the people during the repetition of the <i>Amidah</i>. Rather, he should pray and then return to his work at the earliest possible time.</i>
Control of one's voice: What is implied?<br>A person should not raise his voice during his <i>Amidah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 31a explains that these laws are derived from the description of Channah's prayer (I Samuel 1:13 : "Channah spoke unto her heart, only her lips moved, but her voice was not heard."</i> nor should he pray silently.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., merely contemplating on the words of prayer in his heart. The Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 4:1, states that the phrase, "only her lips moved," implies that she was speaking, not only thinking to herself.</i> Rather, he should pronounce the words with his lips, whispering in a tone that he can hear.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 32b relates: "Why did they decree that the <i>Amidah</i> must be whispered? In order that those who have transgressed will not be embarrassed."<br>Rashi explains that transgressors confess their sins before God during the <i>Amidah</i>, and, therefore, the Sages ruled that the entire <i>Amidah</i> should be recited in a hushed tone so that people would not feel embarrassed to confess.</i><br>He should not make his voice audible<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The first clause of this halachah implies that one should not raise his voice and speak very loudly during the <i>Amidah</i>. This clause teaches that one should not even speak in a normal tone of voice.<br>Both of these laws are based on <i>Berachot</i> 24b:<br>One who makes his voice audible during the <i>Amidah</i> is among those of little faith (i.e., he seems to indicate that it is necessary to speak loudly in order that God should hear him - Rashi).One who raises his voice in the <i>Amidah</i> is among the false prophets. (Rashi explains that this is based on I Kings 18:28, which describes the actions of the false prophets of Ba'al: "And they called out loudly.")<br><i>Lechem Yehudah</i> notes that the mention of both these halachot is somewhat unnecessary. The prohibition of making one's voice audible alone would appear to be sufficient. However, since that prohibition is lifted on occasion as explained below, the Rambam mentioned also the first law which is followed regardless of the circumstances involved.</i> unless he is sick or cannot concentrate otherwise.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">When stating the abovementioned restriction, <i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) mentions that it can be relaxed in this eventuality.</i> In such a case, it is permitted except when in a congregation, lest the others be disturbed by his voice.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 101 allows an exception to this rule on Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur. On these days, people devote more concentration to their prayers. Also, they generally pray from a prayerbook. Therefore, they are less likely to be disturbed by someone else's voice. Nevertheless, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> notes that the Zohar suggests praying in a hushed tone even on these days.</i>
Bowing:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The bows in the <i>Amidah</i> are intended to express the concept of complete abnegation of self (See <i>Tanya,</i> Chapters 39 and 42).</i> What is implied?<br>One praying bows five times in each and every <i>Amidah</i>:<br>In<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 34a relates that a person who bows at any other time should be taught to discontinue his practice.</i> the first blessing, at the beginning and at the end;<br>in<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., one bows during the recitation of ברוך אתה at the beginning of the blessing and the recitation of ברוך אתה at the end.</i> the blessing of thanks,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The blessing מודים, the second to last blessing of the <i>Amidah</i>.</i> at the beginning and at the end;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., at the recitation of the words מודים אנחנו לך (We are thankful to You) and the recitation of ברוך אתה at the end.</i> and<br>upon completing the <i>Amidah</i>, one bows and takes three steps backwards<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> points out that one should begin his three steps backwards with his left foot, in order that his right foot be the last to withdraw from before God.</i> while bowing.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 53b mentions that Rav Chiya, the son of Rav Huna, saw that Ravva and Abbaye took three steps backwards in the midst of a prolonged bow.</i> He takes leave<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) states: "A person who prays must take three steps backwards and then take his leave. Anyone who does not do this is better not to have prayed at all"</i> from his left and afterwards, from his right.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11 for the explanation of this halachah.</i> Then, he lifts his head up from the bowed position.<br>When he bows the [other] four times, he does so at [the utterance of the word] "Blessed" and straightens up when [reciting] G‑d's name.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 12a explains the latter law based on Psalms 146:8: "God straightens those bent over."<br>The Maharal (<i>Netiv Ha'avodah</i>, Chapter 10) explains that this reflects how God is the source of all life and He grants man the power to act.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Then, he lifts his head up from the bowed position.</i><br> To whom does the above apply? To an average person. However, the High Priest bows at the beginning and end of each and every blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question the Rambam's statements, noting that there are two opinions concerning this law mentioned in <i>Berachot</i> 34a, b and neither corresponds to the Rambam's text. See <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.</i> A king bows at the beginning [of the <i>Amidah</i>] and does not lift his head until he completes his whole <i>Amidah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) bases this statement on I Kings 8:54: "And so it was when Solomon completed praying to God..., he rose from kneeling before the Altar of God."<br>Rashi explains that the High Priest and King bow down more because a person who occupies a position of greatness must lower and subjugate himself before God. The Maharal (<i>ibid.</i>) mentions that bowing is a natural response to one's feelings of closeness to God. Therefore, these two men, closer than others to God, bowed more frequently.</i>
Why should one take leave from the left first?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as stated in the previous halachah</i> Because one's left is to the right [side]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 53b relates:<br>One should take leave from the right and then from the left, as [Deuteronomy 33:2] states: "From His right [came] a fiery teaching for them," and [Deuteronomy 33:2] states: "From His right [comes] a fiery teaching for them."<br>of His countenance - Rashi, <i>Yoma</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) explains that during prayer, one must envision himself standing before the Divine Presence. Thus, his right side is actually opposite God's left, so to speak.</i> of His countenance; i.e., just like when one stands before a<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">mortal</i> king, he takes leave from the right of the king, and then afterwards from the left of the king. Thus, they<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages of the Great Assembly, when laying down the rules for prayer, established that one should withdraw from the Amidah in the same manner as he withdraws from before a king.</i> established that one should withdraw from the <i>Amidah</i> in the same manner as he withdraws from before a king.
All these bows require that one bow until the vertebrae in his spine protrude and he makes himself like a bow.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) states that one should bow until he could see a coin placed opposite his heart. The Rashba explains that this refers to a coin that would be placed on the ground opposite one's heart. Thus, one should not bend over completely, but rather curve his back such that he can see the ground.</i><br>However, if one bows slightly [to the extent that] it causes him pain and he appears to have bowed with all of his power, he need not worry.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) states:<br>Rabbi Chanina says: "One need not do more than nod his head."Ravva commented: "This is the case if it causes him pain, as long as he appears as one who is bowing."</i>
Prostration, what is implied?<br>After one lifts his head from the fifth bow, he sits on the ground, falls with his face towards the earth, and utters all the supplications<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 22b relates that the custom of falling to the earth in supplication was followed in Babylonia in Talmudic times.<br>The <i>Tur, Orach Chayim</i> 131, explains that the reason for prostrating oneself after <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> is that by doing so, one prays in all three positions of prayer employed by Moses:<br>sitting (during the verses of Praise), - "...and I sat on the mountain 40 days and 40 nights" (Deuteronomy 9:9), standing (during <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>) - "And I stood on the mountain as on the first day for 40 days and 40 nights" (Deuteronomy 10:10), prostration - "And I fell down before God, as at first, for 40 days and 40 nights" (Deuteronomy 9:25).<br>The <i>Zohar</i> (<i>Parshat BaMidbar</i>) explains that after prayer, a person should feel as if he has returned his soul to God and has no life-energy left. Thus, he falls to the ground where he receives new spiritual power.</i> that he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., there is no fixed text of prayers recited at this time. In his <i>Seder Tefilot kol HaShanah</i>, the Rambam writes: "It is our custom to make supplication while prostrated with these statements and verses; sometimes, mentioning them all and sometimes, mentioning only some of them."<br>Thus, though there was a basic text for these prayers (which is, to a large extent, included in the extended <i>Tachanun</i> recited on Mondays and Thursdays), what a person actually said was still left to his own creative impulse.<br>There are two different versions of תחנון today. The תחנון of <i>Nusach Ha'ari</i> is based on Psalm 25, whereas that of <i>Nusach Ashkenaz</i> and <i>Nusach Sefard</i> is based on Psalm 6.</i><br>"Kneeling" always refers to [falling to] one's knees;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 22b derives this law from I Kings 8:54: "And so it was when Solomon completed his Prayer to God... that he rose from kneeling on his knees."</i> "bowing," to bending over on one's face;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This is derived from I Kings 1:31: "And Bat Sheva bowed with her face to the earth" (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and "prostration," to stretching out on one's hands and feet until he is flat with his face on the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is derived from Genesis 37:10: "Will it come to pass that I and your mother and brothers will prostrate ourselves to you on the ground" (<i>ibid.</i>).<br>The definition of these three terms is important within the context of the following halachah.</i>
When uttering the supplication<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the prayer recited after <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. In Hebrew, the Rambam uses the term נפילת אפים, which means literally "falling on the face." However, this translation is employed since the intent of "falling on one's face" is to recite these supplications.</i> after the <i>Amidah</i>, there are those who bow and there are those who prostrate themselves.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 22b indicates that this variance in custom existed even in Talmudic times.<br>It is interesting that in Halachah 13, the Rambam mentioned only <i>histachavah</i>, prostrating oneself, as the proper method for נפילת אפים. Perhaps, he considered it the most preferable position.</i><br>It is forbidden to prostrate oneself on stones except in the Holy Temple, as we have explained in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Halachot 6 and 7. There, the Rambam explains that it was a normal practice of idolators to place stones down in order to prostrate themselves upon them. He states that the Biblical prohibition - (Leviticus 26:1 : "You shall not place a paved stone in your land to bow upon it" - refers to השתחואה alone, as opposed to simply bowing, which although forbidden, does not obligate punishment. See also <i>Sefer Hamitzvot</i>, Negative Commandment 12.<br>The Rambam also notes that prostration was permitted in the Temple, since the above verse mentioned only "your land". Similarly, because of this prohibition, it is customary to lie rugs or mats on top of the stone floors in most synagogues.</i><br>An important person is not permitted to fall on his face unless he is certain that he is as righteous as Yehoshua.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) relates:<br>An important person is not permitted to fall on his face unless he will be answered like Yehoshua bin Nun, as [Joshua 7:10] states: "God said to Yehoshua: 'Get up and go. Why is it that you have fallen on your face?'”<br>Rashi explains that a person of stature would be subjected to ridicule if his prayer were not answered as Yehoshua's was.<br>The Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Ta'anit</i> 2:6, restricts the application of this Halachah. According to one version, the limitation exists only to יחיד על הציבור (an individual who is praying on behalf of the community). Another version states יחיד בציבור (an individual in a congregation).<br>In these cases, there might be cause for embarrassment if he was not answered. However, there is no such restriction when an individual prays by himself. See also <i>Tosafot, Megillah</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> Rather, he should tilt his face slightly, but not press it to the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) derives this law from the actions of Ravvah and Abbaye, who followed this practice instead of falling to the ground in accordance with the above rule.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 131:8) rules in accordance with the Rambam. The Ramah adds that no one should prostrate himself on the floor even if it is not stone. Therefore, the custom today is that everyone rests their heads on their arms while reciting <i>Tachanun</i>.</i><br>One may pray in one place and offer this supplication in another<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> maintains that this can be derived from the following narrative in <i>Megillah</i> (<i>ibid.</i>). Rav came to a synagogue in Babylonia, but did not fall to the ground after the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> as they did. One of the explanations offered by the Talmud for his behavior is that there was a stone floor in front of Rav, but not in front of the other congregants. The Talmud then asks: "Why didn't Rav move to their place?", obviously implying that one may offer this supplication in a place other than one's place of prayer.</i>
It is an accepted custom among the entire Jewish people not to utter the supplication on Sabbaths or festivals. Nor [does one utter it] on Rosh Hashanah, Rosh Chodesh, Chanukah or Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This custom is also mentioned in the Siddur of Rav Saadia Gaon.<br>The days mentioned are all joyous occasions. At such times, we do not beseech God with extra supplication. Rather, we concentrate on the goodness that we have already received from Him. Similarly, we refrain from נפילת אפים at other occasions of joy; for example, a bride and groom on the day of their wedding and the week following it, and the participants in a ברית מילה (circumcision) are exempt from נפילת אפים. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim╟13 131:4.)<br></i><br>or in Minchah on the eve of Sabbaths or holidays, - when the festive nature of these days can already be felt.</i> or in Minchah on the eve of Sabbaths or holidays, nor in the Evening Prayer of any day.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 131) explains that we refrain from reciting <i>Tachanun</i> at night for mystic reasons. <i>Tachanun</i> relates to the aspect of stern judgement which is also identified with the night. It is not proper to combine both aspects of judgement together.</i> There are [however,] individuals who do utter the supplication in the Evening Prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar Geonim</i> on <i>Megillah</i> 23b discusses the various customs regarding the utterance of נפילת אפים. Rav Sar Shalom mentions that, although it is not his custom, there are some people who do נפילת אפים after מעריב.</i><br>On Yom Kippur only, one utters the supplication prayer in every prayer, since it is a day of supplication, requests, and fasting.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Though we recite confessional prayers in each service of Yom Kippur, the only times we fall to the ground in supplication is during the <i>Avodah</i> of the Musaf prayers (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).</i>

Chapter 6

A person is forbidden to walk behind a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the side of the synagogue on which there is an entrance. However, a person walking on the other side of the synagogue does not appear to dismiss the idea of prayer, since he has not willfully abstained from entering the place of worship (<i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 90).</i> at the time that the congregation is praying,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Berachot</i> 61a, explains that a person passing by appears to be fleeing from the synagogue and the obligations observed therein.</i> unless he is carrying a burden<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is carrying a bundle, it is clearly understood why he is not entering the synagogue.<br><i>Berachot</i> 8b quotes the above prohibition in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi. It then quotes Abbaye who mentions the various qualifications related by the Rambam.</i> or there are two entrances to the synagogue on different sides. [In the latter instance], anyone who sees him would presume that perhaps he is planning to enter [the synagogue] through the other entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 8b mentions the idea of two entrances to the synagogue. However, the specification that the entrances be on different sides is the Rambam's addition. Only in such a case could one seeing the person imagine that he is entering the synagogue by its other entrance. If the entrances are on the same side, the observer need simply wait to see if he also passes by the second entrance.</i><br>Similarly, if there are two synagogues in the city, a person seeing him would say that perhaps he is going to his usual synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and is not shirking his responsibility to pray.</i><br>If one is wearing <i>tefillin</i> on his head,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot</i> tefillin 4:25 which mentions the obligation to wear <i>tefillin</i> throughout the entire day, even after one finished prayer. At present, as explained in the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39), it is customary to wear <i>tefillin</i> only during prayer.</i> he is permitted to pass [a synagogue] even without any of these conditions, since the <i>tefillin</i> indicate that he is a person who is seriously interested in the performance of commandments, and not one to refrain from prayer.
One praying with a congregation should not lengthen his prayer excessively.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">From the passage from <i>Berachot</i> quoted below, it would appear that the prohibition was instituted to prevent the discomfort that would be caused were the congregation to be forced to wait for one individual to finish his prayers. Rav Kapach notes that even if the congregation would not necessarily wait, it is forbidden to do so, lest one appear haughty.</i> [However,] he may do so when praying alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 31a states:<br>This was the custom of Rabbi Akiva; when praying in a congregation he would shorten his prayer in order not to bother those present, but when he prayed alone, one would leave him while he was in one corner of the room and [return and] find him in the other corner because of his bowings and prostrations.</i> If, after praying,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., after he has completed <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i></i> he desires to [add to his prayers], he may,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Talmud (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 8a and <i>Berachot</i> 31a) states that even though generally one should limit his requests during the recitation of <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> to the blessing of שומע תפילה, this restriction does not apply after one concludes praying.</i> including even the confession of Yom Kippur.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Talmud (<i>ibid.</i>) (and, similarly, the authoritative Oxford manuscript of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>) states that after prayer, one may add “the order of the day of Yom Kippur” and does not mention וידוי (confession).<br>One might conclude that this indicates that one may add as much as he likes. The prayers of Yom Kippur are mentioned since they serve as the paradigm of long prayer.</i><br>Similarly, he may add in each of the middle blessings something relevant to that blessing if he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the following halachah.</i>
What is implied?<br>If<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">by the last clause of the previous halachah.</i> one has a sick person [for whom he wants to pray], he should request mercy for this person in the blessing for the sick<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 8a notes:<br>Even though [the Sages] taught that a person should make his special requests in [the blessing of] שומע תפילה, one may add at the end of each blessing something relevant to that blessing.<br>The Talmud quotes a second teaching which specifically mentions the two examples given by the Rambam.</i> as eloquently as he can.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note Chapter 1, Halachah 4, which explains that the reason the Sages established a standard text of prayer was to prevent people from praying in a non-eloquent manner. Though a person is allowed to make whatever additions to prayer he desires, he should try to use the most elegant means of expression as possible.</i><br>If he requires sustenance, he should add a supplication and request in the blessing for material prosperity.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The sixth of the intermediate blessings.</i> The same applies regarding each of the other blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For example, Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i>, <i>ibid.</i>) explains that one desiring not to forget that which he has learned should make such a request in חונן הדעת [the first of the intermediate blessings].</i><br>Should one desire to ask for all his needs in the blessing of "the One who hears Prayer," he may do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">without qualms. There is no requirement to make one's requests in the other blessings as mentioned above.</i> However, he should make no requests in the first three or last three [blessings].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Berachot</i> 34a and also Chapter 1, Halachah 9, and commentary.</i>
A person is forbidden to taste<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This includes both eating and drinking and indicates that one is forbidden from even tasting the smallest amount of food.</i> anything<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 10b relates: What does [Leviticus 19:26]: "You should not eat with blood" mean? You should not eat before you have prayed <i>al dimchem</i> [lit. “for your blood”]... Anyone who eats and drinks and [only] afterwards prays - about him [I Kings 14:9[ states: "... and you have cast Me behind your body." Do not read גויך (your body), but rather גאיך (your pride). The Holy One, blessed be He, says: "After you have acted proudly and haughtily [by eating], will you accept upon yourself the sovereignty of Heaven?" Thus, it is clear that one must deal with his spiritual obligations before fulfilling his physical needs. <br> Note Chapter 5, Halachah 2, where the Rambam states that one who is hungry or thirsty is considered sick and must eat or drink before prayer in order to pray with proper intention. For this reason, medicines also present no problem and may be taken before <i>Shacharit</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 89:3).<br>The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (89:22) grants permission to drink tea or coffee before prayer, if this will help one's concentration during prayer. However,he suggests not putting sugar into the drink. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 89:23) allows one to add sugar to tea and even suggests that adding milk is permissible.</i> or to do any work<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 14a relates:<br>A person is forbidden to deal with his personal matters before he prays, as [Psalms 85:14] states: "[He will place] righteousness before him and he will place his feet in the path."<br>Rashi explains that first one must pay heed to the righteousness of the Creator, and only then walk in the way - i.e., do work that is necessary for his own personal needs.</i> from dawn until after he has recited the Morning Prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Dawn is the earliest possible time for the Morning Prayer. (See Chapter 3, Halachah 7.) Therefore, as soon as the obligation of prayer exists, one is forbidden to eat or drink.</i> He should also refrain from visiting the house of a friend to greet him before he has recited the Morning Prayer;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 14a relates:<br>Anyone who greets his friend before praying has, so to speak, made his friend into an altar, as [Isaiah 2:22] states: "Desist from the man whose life is in his nostrils, as for what is he considered." Do not read <i>bemah</i> (“for what”) but rather <i>bamah</i> (“an altar”).<br>Nevertheless, the Talmud explains that this prohibition only applies to going to a friend's house to greet him. Greeting him in the street is permitted.</i> nor should he set out on a journey before he has prayed.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 14a derives this law based on the same verse used as the source for the prohibition of working before praying.</i><br>However, one may taste food or do work before reciting <i>Musaf</i> or <i>Minchah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 28b quotes two opinions, one of Rav Huna forbidding eating before <i>Musaf</i>, and one of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi forbidding eating before <i>Minchah</i>, but does not accept either of them as binding.</i> although he should not have a full meal close to the time for <i>Minchah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Though the Talmud rejects the opinion of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi regarding טעימה (tasting). However, there are restrictions in regard to eating a meal as explained in the following halachah (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
Once the time for <i>Minchah Gedolah</i> arrives,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., six and a half hours (שעות זמניות) of the day, as explained in Chapter 3, Halachah 2.</i> one should not enter a bathhouse,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following laws are based on the Mishnah (<i>Shabbat</i> 9b): "One should not sit in front of the barber close to <i>Minchah</i> until he has prayed..." The Talmud explains that this refers to <i>Minchah Gedolah</i>.</i> even [if only] to sweat, until he has prayed,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This person is not interested in bathing in the hot or cold bath or washing himself well, which are time-consuming activities. Nevertheless, since a prohibition was instituted against bathing...</i> lest he faint and neglect prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the restriction was enforced even in this instance.</i><br>He should not eat, even a snack, lest he continue eating and neglect prayer,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous halachah, the Rambam rules that one may taste food before <i>Minchah</i>, but forbade eating a meal. Here, he states that even a snack is forbidden. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that eating a כביצה (the equivalent of an egg, 52 or 100 cubic centimeters, according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and the <i>Chazon Ish</i>, respectively) of fruit, or a כזית (an olive size, approximately 26 or 50 cubic centimeters, according to the same references cited) of bread constitutes <i>achilat ari’ai</i> which we have translated as a snack.</i> nor [should he] judge [a court case], even if only to render a final judgment, lest the decision be questioned and the matter be drawn out and cause him to miss prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah quoted above states simply that one must not sit in judgment close to <i>Minchah</i>. In his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam states:<br>Even if the case is finished and the judges have heard the claims of the adversaries in the case, and have deliberated [and reached a verdict], and need only announce their final decision, the judge should not sit to render this decision close to the time of <i>Minchah</i>, lest he see the case in a different light and be required to start again, or the adversaries restate their claims and the matters drag on until the time [for <i>Minchah</i>] passes.</i><br>Similarly, one should not sit in a barber's chair, even for a regular haircut,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as opposed to the especially intricate haircut given the High Priest, called the haircut of "ben Elasa" (<i>Shabbat</i> 9b). (See also <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 5:6)</i> until he prays, lest the scissors break.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">and the time for prayer pass before the barber can secure another scissors.</i> He should not enter a tanning house close to <i>Minchah</i> before he has prayed, lest he see a deficiency in his work which he will deal with, and [thereby] be delayed from praying.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam explains, in his commentary on the Mishnah, that if one sees a deficiency in the skin he will attempt to stop further damage, and his involvement in this work could last until after the end of the time for <i>Minchah</i>.<br>The Rambam's interpretation of the mishnah is not accepted by other authorities. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Shabbat, loc. cit.</i>) rules that only complicated activities are forbidden and simple acts, e.g., a snack or a normal haircut are permitted.<br>This disagreement finds expression in the rulings of the later authorities. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 232:2) quotes the Rambam's view. The Ramah brings a third opinion (of the <i>Ba'al HaMa'or</i>) which rules that even a large meal is permissible before <i>Minchah Gedolah</i>, and that a snack is permissible even before <i>Minchah K'tanah</i>. He adds that perhaps the reason for this leniency is that since it was customary to call people to synagogue, it is unlikely that they would not forget to go. However, he advises following the more stringent view when praying alone.</i><br>If he begins doing one of these things,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he began after the time of <i>Minchah Gedolah</i> and, therefore, his beginning was a transgression (<i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>).</i> he need not stop, but may finish and then recite <i>Minchah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This leniency is also based on the Mishnah (<i>Shabbat, loc. cit.</i>). After listing all the activities that are forbidden once <i>Minchah Gedolah</i> arrives, the Mishnah concludes: "And if he starts, he need not stop."<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this is the case only if he will be able to recite <i>Minchah</i> before the conclusion of the appropriate time. To emphasize this point, the Rambam mentions explicitly: "and then recite <i>Minchah</i>" - i.e., after he finishes doing whatever it is that he has started doing. However, if there will not be time to pray afterwards, he must stop and pray. Only then may he resume his activity.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 232:2, follows this interpretation of the Rambam and adds that if there will not be time afterwards, one must stop what he is doing immediately. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> points out that this means that even if it is still relatively early, if one is eating and expects the meal to continue until after the time of <i>Minchah</i>, he must stop immediately and pray, rather than wait until sometime later before the end of the day.</i>
When is the beginning of a haircut?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Halachah 5, the Rambam taught us that if one had already begun his involvement in any of these activities, he need not stop in order to recite <i>Minchah</i>. Therefore, it is necessary to understand what constitutes involvement in these activities.</i> When he puts the barber's cloth over his knees.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The מעפורת is an apron used to collect the hairs falling from one's head during a haircut, so that they do not fall onto his clothes. (<i>Shabbat</i> 9b) In his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam states that one who has put the cloth over his knees "is prepared for a haircut." From that point on, even though no hair has actually been cut, he is considered involved in the haircut, so that he need not stop to pray.</i><br>When is the beginning of a bath?<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., when is he considered to be bathing, so that he need not stop to pray?</i> When he takes off his underclothes.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "the garments next to his skin." This is based on the Rambam's interpetation of <i>Shabbat</i> 9b: "When he removes his מעפרתו from himself." Rashi, in his commentary on that passage, offers a different interpretation - one's outermost garment, i.e., the first garment removed. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 232:2) rules in line with Rashi's position.</i><br>When is the beginning of [work in the] tanning house? When he ties the apron between his shoulders, as is the way of artisans.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 9b states simply: "When he ties between his shoulders." In his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that it is when he ties his belt in order to work with the skins. Evidently this means the belt around his apron. There is a version of the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah which mentions also that he begins working with the skins. This is, however, hard to understand in light of his commentary here in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i><br>When is the beginning of eating? For those who live in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, it is when one washes his hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 9b records a disagreement between Rav and Rabbi Chanina. Rav states that washing one's hands constitutes the beginning of eating, whereas Rabbi Chanina is of the opinion that loosening one's belt before eating is the beginning of the meal. The Talmud concludes that there is no real argument. Rav described the halachah for inhabitants of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, and Rabbi Chanina, for those of Babylonia.</i> For inhabitants of Babylonia, it is when they loosen their belts.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi explains that the custom in Babylonia was to wear their belts particularly tight, necessitating that they be loosened prior to eating. This was not the case in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<br>It is interesting to note that in his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam mentions only the halachah of washing one's hands. Since the Mishnah was written in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, its interpretation must be in line with the customs and realities of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. However, in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Rambam focuses on the actual halachah. Hence, he defines the beginning of eating in line with both positions, one relevant to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and the other to Babylonia.</i><br>When is the beginning of judgment? When the judges robe themselves in their <i>taleisim</i> and sit down.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi explains that the judges wore their <i>talleisim</i> in court in order to focus their attention on the serious nature of the matter at hand. <i>Shabbat</i> 10a states: "Any judge who adjudicates a judgment of absolute truth ..., is considered by the Torah to be a partner with God in the creation of the world."</i> If they were [already] sitting, it is when the adversaries begin to make their claims.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 10a makes this point of clarification.</i>
Even though the Evening Prayer is not obligatory,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 6 and the commentary there.</i> one should not come home from his work and say: "I will eat a little and sleep a little and then I will pray," lest sleep overtake him and he sleep all night.
