Rather, he should [first] pray, and afterwards he may eat, drink or sleep.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 4b discusses the decree of the Sages requiring the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> before midnight even though its actual time lasts until dawn (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:9.). That passage states:<br>The Sages produced a support for their words in order that a person not come in from the field in the evening and say: "I shall go home, eat a little, sleep a little and then recite the <i>Shema</i> and pray," lest he be overcome by sleep and sleep all night.Rather, he should come from the field in the evening and enter the synagogue. If he is accustomed to recite the <i>Shema</i> immediately, he should. If he is accustomed to study Torah first, he should study, recite the <i>Shema</i>, pray and then eat his bread and recite grace.<br>In contrast to the restrictions mentioned in the previous halachah in regard to the afternoon prayers...<br>It is permissible to have a haircut or enter a bathhouse before the Morning Prayer. [The Sages] only established their decree before <i>Minchah</i>, since it is common for most people to go there during the day. However, in the morning, [these actions] are uncommon. Hence they did not establish a decree. - Since the Mishnah cited above mentions only "close to <i>Minchah</i>," the Rambam assumes that the halachot apply only to מנחה and not to שחרית for the reason he explains.<br>The Rambam does not mention going to the tanning house or eating in this halachah, because they are included in Halachah 4 regarding working or eating before the Morning Prayer, both of which are prohibited.</i><br>It is permissible to have a haircut or enter a bathhouse before the Morning Prayer. [The Sages] only established their decree before <i>Minchah</i>, since it is common for most people to go there during the day. However, in the morning, [these actions] are uncommon. Hence, they did not establish a decree.
A person who is involved in the study of Torah when the time for prayer arrives must stop and pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Shabbat</i> 9b) states that one must stop for <i>Kri'at Shema</i>, but not for prayer. The Talmud (11a) explains that this refers to one involved in the study of Torah.</i> If the study of Torah is his full-time occupation and he does not work at all,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) continues:<br>This applies only to Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai and his colleagues, whose full time occupation was the study of Torah, but we stop studying to recite the <i>Shema</i> and to pray.<br>Rashi explains that anyone who interrupts his studies in order to work, must also interrupt his studies in order to pray.</i> and he is involved in the study of Torah at the time of prayer, he need not stop, since the commandment of the study of Torah is greater that the commandment of prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 3:3, the Rambam states: "There is no commandment among all the commandments that is equal to the study of Torah. Rather, the study of Torah is equal to all the other commandments, since study brings one to action. Therefore, study comes before action at all times."<br>A person involved in Torah study achieves a level of unity with God that is unparalleled by any other activity. One's intellect and God's wisdom become inextricably linked, until they are indistinguishable during the moments of one's actual involvement in study. On this basis, we can understand the description of Torah with the metaphor of bread (Proverbs 9:5, <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i> 4:13). After bread is consumed by a person, it becomes assimilated into his blood and becomes part of the person himself. Similarly, when Torah is studied, it becomes absorbed into the person's character and becomes an integral part of his being (<i>Tanya</i>, Chapter 5).<br>As explained in the introduction to this text, prayer focuses on our attempt to relate to the Godliness that permeates our worldly existence. Though this is an extremely significant service, the connection established through Torah study is more complete. Thus, <i>Shabbat</i> 10a records how Ravvah rebuked Rav Hamnuna for neglecting his Torah studies to prolong his prayers. By doing he was "abandoning eternal life for transcient life." Therefore, Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai and his colleagues whose commitment to Torah was all encompassing were not required to interrupt their studies for prayer.</i><br>Anyone involved in efforts for the welfare of the community is like one involved in Torah study.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., he can be compared to one whose full time occupation is the study of Torah. Therefore, he is exempt from the <i>Amidah</i> at that time.<br>This comparison of community work to the study of Torah is found in the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 5:1. However, there it is taught in the context of praying in the midst of words of Torah. (See Chapter 4, Halachah 18.) Community work is also considered like Torah, so that one may pray after such work and it is considered as if he prayed in the midst of words of Torah.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam derives this law from the continuation of the Talmudic passage (<i>Shabbat</i> 11a) cited above. The Talmud states that one involved in the decision of whether or not to proclaim a leap year (עיבור שנה) need not stop to pray, even though Torah study is not his full-time occupation. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> posits that the Rambam views involvement in עיבור שנה as community work. On this basis, he concludes that one need not stop in order to pray if he is involved in such activities.</i>
One is forbidden to interrupt his <i>Amidah</i> except in a situation where his life is endangered.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 2:15-17, we discussed the various situations in which one is allowed to interrupt his recitation of the <i>Shema</i>. Here, the Rambam teaches that the laws regarding the <i>Amidah</i> are much more strict. Only a threat to one's very life is considered sufficient cause to interrupt the <i>Amidah</i>.</i> Even if the King of Israel greets him, he must not answer him.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 29b) states: "Even if the king greets him, he may not answer." The Talmud explains that this applies only to a Jewish king, but not to a foreign king. A Jewish king is expected to appreciate the seriousness of prayer and realize that a person's unwillingness to interrupt his prayers is not intended as an insult to the king's authority, but rather, submission to an even greater authority.<br>It is interesting to note that this halachah is taught in the Mishnah within the context of halachot stressing the serious nature of the <i>Amidah</i> and the high level of concentration necessary. Just as we learned that the level of intention necessary for the <i>Amidah</i> is greater than that necessary for <i>Kri'at Shema</i> (see Chapter 5, Halachah 8 and the commentary there), so too, the halachot regarding interruptions are stricter, as mentioned.</i> However, he may interrupt [to answer] a non-Jewish king, lest he<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">fail to understand the concept explained above and...</i> kill him.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as a rebel against the king.<br><i>Berachot</i> 32b-33a relates the following story which shows that the Sages did not always avail themselves of the leniency granted:<br>A righteous man was once praying on the road. A minister approached him and greet him. However, he did not respond. [The minister] waited until he had finished praying and said to him: "Empty one! Is it not written in your Torah: 'Take heed and watch yourself carefully' (Deuteronomy 4:9 , and 'Guard yourselves very carefully' (Deuteronomy 4:15)." "When I greeted you, why didn't you answer? If I had cut off your head with my sword, who would have sought vengeance for your life?"He said to him: "Let me appease you with words. If you were standing in front of an earthly king and your friend came and greeted you would you answer him?"[The minister] said: "No.""And if you did answer him what would they do to you?"He replied: "They would cut off my head!"He said: "And is it not reasonable that if you [would not interrupt] while standing in front of an earthly king who is here today and in the grave tomorrow, so too I, standing in front of the King of Kings, the Holy One blessed be He, who is eternally present, all the more so [should I not interrupt in order to return greetings]."The minister was appeased immediately, and the righteous person returned home in peace."</i><br>A person standing in the [midst of the] <i>Amidah</i> who sees a non-Jewish king or tyrant approaching him should shorten [his prayer].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Yonah explains that this means simply to recite the beginning and end of each blessing. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> also agrees with this explanation.<br>In Chapter 2, Halachot 2-3, the Rambam mentions the text for a shortened prayer. However, there is not necessarily a contradiction between this opinion and that law.</i> If unable to do so, he may stop.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 32b offers these two alternatives.<br>Rabbenu Yonah maintains that if possible, one should move slightly in order to avoid confronting the king or tyrant, and thus avoid the need to talk.</i> Similarly, if one sees snakes or scorpions approaching him in a place where their bite is fatal, he should stop [praying] and flee. If they are not fatal, he should not stop.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 29b) states: "...and even if there is a snake curled around his ankle, he should not interrupt [his <i>Amidah</i>]." The Talmud (33a) mentions that a snake does not cause an interruption, but a scorpion does. Rashi explains that though snakes are not likely to bite, scorpions are likely to sting. Thus, the passage raises questions on the Rambam's decision, for he does not distinguish between snakes and scorpions.<br>The Rambam's statements appear to be based on the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 5:1, which states that one ignores a snake only if it is simply curling around his leg. However, were the snake to approach him in a menacing fashion, appearing likely to bite, he may flee from the snake.<br>It is important to note that there is no actual need to interrupt his <i>Amidah</i> in the case of the dangerous creatures. He need simply leave that spot, find a safe place and continue praying.<br><i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> points out that in all the cases mentioned, even if he did actually speak in the middle of <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he need only start again at the beginning of the blessing in which he stopped. If, however, he interrupted himself during the first or last three blessings, he should return to the beginning of the beginning of the first of the three blessings.<br>The laws mentioned in this halachah are discussed in the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 104.</i>
Women, slaves and children are obligated to pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 20a-b). This halachah is discussed in Chapter 1, Halachah 2.</i> Anyone exempt from <i>Kri'at Shema</i> is also exempt from the <i>Amidah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The laws regarding those exempt from <i>Kri'at Shema</i> are contained in <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 4:3-6. The exemption of these people from the <i>Amidah</i> is stated explicitly in the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 17b).</i><br>All those accompanying the dead [for burial], even if they are not required to carry the coffin, are exempt from the <i>Amidah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is also based on the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 17b) which teaches that though there are differences between the obligations people have regarding the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 4:4.), "both are exempt from the <i>Amidah</i>."<br>Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i> explain that the distinction between <i>Kri'at Shema</i> and the <i>Amidah</i> is based on the fact that the recital of the <i>Shema</i> is a Torah obligation. Some commentaries explain that this explanation would even be acceptable according to the Rambam. Though he holds that the <i>Amidah</i> is also a Torah obligation (see Chapter 1, Halachah 1), it is only necessary to pray once daily in order to fulfill one's Torah obligation. Therefore, one may be more lenient in granting an exemption from the <i>Amidah</i>, since the Torah obligation may have already been fulfilled or may be fulfilled by another <i>Amidah</i> later in the day.<br>However, the Rambam, himself, offers a different explanation in his commentary on the Mishnah. There, he states that since one is troubled (לבו טרוד) by the burial, he is exempt from the <i>Amidah</i>. This seems to be a function of the greater need for intention in the <i>Amidah</i> rather than its non-Torah status.</i>

Chapter 7

When the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ezra and his court, the <i>Anshei K'nesset HaGedolah</i></i> instituted [a text for] these prayers,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachot 2-5.</i> they [also] established other blessings to be recited every day.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 1:4, the Rambam writes:<br>There are three types of blessings: blessings associated with [deriving] satisfaction, blessings associated with mitzvot, and blessings of thanksgiving which are a process of praise, thanksgiving, and request so that we will continually remember the Creator and fear Him.<br>In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 10:1, the Rambam writes that the blessings associated with the prayer service are included in the category of blessings of thanksgiving. This position is not accepted by all authorities. For example, the <i>Avudraham</i> considers the blessings associated with the prayer service as a separate category of blessings.</i> These are:<br>When a person gets into bed<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Otzar HaGeonim</i> which requires that both this blessing and the <i>Shema</i> be recited in one's bed, directly before retiring.</i> to sleep at night,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam begins his discussion of the blessings of prayer with one recited at night in keeping with the concept that the new day starts after sunset. (See also <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:1 and our commentary there.)</i> he says:<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who causes<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This blessing, with certain textual differences, is found in <i>Berachot</i> 60b.</i> the bonds of sleep<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on Esther 1:6 which mentions חבלי בוץ - "bonds (or cords) of linen" (<i>Otzar HaTefilot</i>). The phrase may also be rendered as "the pains of sleep" based on Hoshea 13:13 חבלי יולדה - "the pains of childbirth." Exhaustion is painful to one's eyes and makes it difficult to keep them open.</i> to fall upon my eyes, who sinks [one into] restful slumber,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kapach's manuscript of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> omits the latter clause. He also notes that the Yemenite siddur, which was composed in accordance with the Rambam's opinion, follows this version. Rav Yitzchok Alfasi's <i>Halachot</i>, which the Rambam often used as a source, also omits this clause.</i> and illuminates the pupil of the eye.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Targum</i> to Psalms 17:8. In Nusach Ashkenaz, this clause appears later in the blessing.</i> May it be Your will, God, our Lord,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The version in our texts of <i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) contains additional requests:<br>May it be Your will, God, my Lord, to lay me down to peace and grant my portion in Your Torah. Accustom me to commandments and not to transgressions and bring me not to sin or transgression, trial, or embarrassment.<br>This text is found in the Siddur of Rav Amram Gaon. The Vilna Gaon, in his emendations to the Talmud, suggests that these requests were not originally included in the Talmud and were added later by Rav Amram Gaon.</i> to save me from the evil inclination and from a bad occurrence. May I not be disturbed by bad dreams or evil thoughts.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The prevention of sinful thoughts and the avoidance of nocturnal emissions during sleep are two of the main reasons given for reciting the <i>Shema</i> before retiring.</i> Let my bed be perfect before You<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 60b) explains that this is a prayer for spiritually healthy children. See <i>Sifre</i> (Deuteronomy 31).</i> and may You raise me up from it to life and peace and illuminate my eyes lest I sleep a sleep of death.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on Psalms 13:4.</i> Blessed are You, God, who illuminates the whole world in His glory.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on Ezekiel 43:2. <i>Metzudat Tzion</i> explains that the radiance of the Divine Presence is a source of light to the whole world. As we go to sleep at night in a dark world, we express our thanks for the Divine light that constantly fills the world.<br>The recitation of a blessing for sleep is based on our Sages' conception of sleep as one of God's greatest gifts because it allows man to rest and awake refreshed, able to serve his Creator with renewed energy and vigor. In <i>Bereishit Rabbah</i> 9:8, Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar interprets the words uttered by God at the end of the sixth day of creation, "and behold it was very good" (Genesis 1:30 , as referring to the creation of sleep.</i>
[Then,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 60b states that one first recites <i>Kri'at Shema</i> and then, the blessing of <i>hamapil</i> (the blessing discussed in Halachah 1). This opinion is quoted by the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 239:1). However, this order is only followed in Nusach Ari. Both Nusach Ashkenaz and Nusach Sephard follow the order stated by the Rambam.<br>The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 1:1) relates that Rav Zeira was accustomed to recite the <i>Shema</i> over and over until he fell asleep. This indicates that the <i>Shema</i> is the last thing said at night before sleeping (<i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> in the name of Rabbenu Nissim Gaon).</i>] one reads the first section<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is consistent with our text of <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) which states that, before going to sleep, one recites "from <i>Shema</i> until <i>V'haya im shamoa</i>." This is the opinion followed in most communities at present.<br>Rabbenu Chananel rules that one recites the first two sections of <i>Shema</i> since both contain the phrases "with all your heart" and "while laying down" (See the commentary below). Rabbenu Asher also follows this position.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (239:1) quotes the <i>Ari</i>, Rabbi Yitzchok Luria, as requiring the recitation of all three sections of the <i>Shema</i> so that the 248 words it contains will guard the 248 limbs of the body during sleep. According to the opinion of Rashi mentioned below, it is also necessary to read all three sections.</i> of <i>Kri'at Shema</i> and goes to sleep.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 4b explains that "it is a mitzvah" to recite the <i>Shema</i> before retiring. These statements are based on Psalms 4:5: "Tremble, but do not sin. Speak in your heart upon your bed and be still." Our Sages interpreted "Speak in your heart" and "upon your bed" as allusions to the phrases "with all your heart" and "upon your bed" in the<i>Shema</i>.<br>That Talmudic passage implies that the reason for reciting the <i>Shema</i> before retiring is to protect oneself from undesirable influences during the night. According to Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 2a) and others who allow the evening service to be recited before sunset, the recitation of <i>Shema</i> before retiring enables us to fulfill the mitzvah of <i>Kri'at Shema</i> at night. According to the writings of the Ari, the reciting of the <i>Shema</i> before retiring is a service of spiritual preparation for the soul's ascent from the body during sleep.<br>Since <i>Berachot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) mentions that "it is a mitzvah" to recite the <i>Shema</i> before retiring, it was customary in certain communities to recite a blessing before reciting the <i>Shema</i> as is customary before performing other mitzvot. In his responsa (<i>Pe'air HaDor</i> 100), the Rambam specifically rules against reciting such a blessing. [However, note the <i>Kolbo</i> (29) who quotes the Rambam as stating in a responsa that such a blessing is required.]</i> [This applies] even if his wife is sleeping with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This line does not appear in either the Oxford or Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:<i>ibid.</i> which permits the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> while touching one's wife because one is familiar with her.</i><br>If he is overcome by sleep,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and cannot recite the entire first section, at the very least</i> he should read the first verse [of <i>Kri'at Shema</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">which contains the essential statement of the unity of God.</i> or verses of mercy<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 4b-5a states:<br>Rabbi Nachman says: "A Torah scholar need not [recite <i>Kri'at Shema</i> before sleep] for his Torah will protect him."Abbaye says: "Even a Torah scholar must recite at least one verse describing God's mercy, for example: 'Into Your hand I entrust my spirit, You will redeem me, God, God of truth' (Psalms 31:6).<br><i>Sh'vuot</i> 16b mentions that Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi would recite Psalm 91 as protection against unfavorable influences at night. Other verses that have a similar intent have been included by Kabbalistic authorities in the text of the <i>Shema</i> recited before retiring.<br>and, afterwards, he may go to sleep.</i> and afterwards, he may go to sleep.
When a person awakes after concluding his sleep,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in the morning when he has completed his night's sleep.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> points out that although <i>Berachot</i> 60b states that one recites this blessing upon arising, the Rambam specifies that it be said only after concluding one's sleep. This makes it clear that a person who wakes up in the middle of the night and intends to return to sleep, need not recite this blessing.</i> while still in bed,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Yonah (in his commentary to the <i>Halachot</i> of Rav Yitzchok Alfasi) takes issue with this statement. He writes:<br>This is difficult to understand. Since [upon waking] one's hands are not clean, how is it possible that [the Rambam] says that one should recite these blessings [while still in bed]. The Talmud mentions this [practice. However, this is because the Sages] were particularly holy. They would wash their hands [at night before sleep] and would be able to maintain a state [of purity] such that they could recite the blessings in the morning in cleanliness. We, however, are unable to maintain this state of cleanliness. Therefore, it is proper that we recite them only after נטילת ידים (the washing of the hands).<br>This position is widely accepted and is the basis of our practice today. See the commentary to Halachah 9.</i> he says:<br>My Lord,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:1, the Rambam writes:<br>All of the blessings begin with <i>Baruch</i> and conclude with <i>Baruch</i> except the final blessing of the blessings associated with the <i>Shema</i>, a blessing which follows directly after another blessing, and a blessing over fruit, those [blessings] which resemble it, or a blessing for mitzvot.<br>Given this statement, the commentaries wonder why this blessing does not begin with <i>Baruch</i>. Furthermore, in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 1:5, the Rambam writes that, unless a blessing follows another blessing, it must mention God's sovereignty over the world and no such mention is made in this blessing.<br>The <i>Meiri</i> in his commentary to <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) offers the following resolution to these difficulties: Despite the fact that the entire period of a person's sleep divides between the recitation of <i>hamapil</i> and <i>E-lohai Neshamah</i>, the sleep is not considered as an interruption and <i>E-lohai Neshamah</i> is considered as following directly after <i>hamapil</i>. Accordingly, it is not required to begin with <i>Baruch</i> or mention God's sovereignty.<br>Other commentaries who follow different halachic perspectives than the Rambam offer these resolutions:1) <i>E-lohai Neshamah</i> is a blessing of praise and prayer and does not require an opening phrase of blessing, in the same way that the blessing recited before travelling does not (<i>Tosafot, Pesachim</i> 104b).2) <i>E-lohai Neshamah</i> is generally recited immediately after the blessing of אשר יצר and therefore can be considered as a blessing which follows another blessing.</i> the soul that You have placed within me<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Each morning, we renew our thanks for the soul which God has returned to us, refreshed and revitalized.</i> is pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Despite his lowly physical state, man possesses an innate spirituality, a pure Godly soul, which is the source of all his positive actions and thoughts.</i> You have created it, You have formed it, You have breathed it into me<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The three verbs used in this blessing correspond to three different descriptions of the creation of man in the Torah:1) ויברא א-להים את האדם - "And the Lord created man" (Genesis 1:27). 2) א-להים את האדם וייצר ה' - "And God, the Lord, formed man" (Genesis 2:7). 3) ויפח באפיו נשמת חיים - "And He blew into his nostrils the soul of life" (<i>ibid.</i>).<br>These three verbs also relate to the three components of the soul, נפש, רוח, ונשמה (<i>Maharsha, 14Berachot</i> 60b).<br>In Kabbalah, the three verbs are understood as referring to the three worlds: עולם הבריאה - the world of creation, עולם היצירה - the world of form, and עולם העשיה - the world of physical action. The word טהרה - "pure" refers to עולם האצילות - the world of emanation, the highest of the four worlds. Thus, this prayer traces the descent of man's soul from God's Throne of Glory into the physical world.</i> and You preserve it within me.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Although the natural inclination of the soul is to return to its Source, God preserves its presence within the body in order that the person may serve His Creator in this world (<i>Midrash Tehillim</i> 62).</i> You will ultimately take it from me<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">at the time of death.</i> and restore it to me in the Time to Come.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">at the time of the resurrection of the dead.</i> As long as the soul is lodged<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Though the word, "lodged," is present in the printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, it is lacking in most manuscripts. Similarly, it is not included in the text of the blessing in most siddurim.</i> within me, I am thankful before You, O God, my Lord, Master of all works.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Some siddurim add the phrase, "Lord of all souls," at this point so that the concluding phrase of the body of the blessing will correspond to the wording of the blessing itself.</i> Blessed are You, God, who restores souls to dead bodies.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 57b compares sleep to 1/60 of death. Therefore, waking can be compared to the resurrection of the dead (Rabbi Ya'akov Emden in his commentary on the siddur).</i>
When one hears the crow of a rooster, he recites: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who gives the rooster understanding to distinguish between day and night.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the crowing of the rooster generally heralds the dawn, the Sages established this blessing in response to it.<br>The word שכוי is translated as "rooster" based on <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 26a which refers to Job 38:36: "Who has put wisdom in the inward parts and [given] understanding to the rooster (שכוי)."<br>Rabbenu Asher (in his halachic commentary to <i>Berachot</i>) interprets שכוי as "heart." The heart is the source of understanding which allows man to distinguish between day and night.</i><br>When he puts on his clothes,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to one's outer garment. Therefore, even a person who has slept in pajamas or in his underwear should recite this blessing (<i>Pri Chadash, Orach Chayim</i> 46).</i> he recites: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who clothes the naked.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Understood simply, this blessing expresses our appreciation to God for the clothes we wear. On a deeper level, it also expresses our cognizance of the sanctity of physical modesty.</i><br>When he puts his cloth on his head,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This blessing is also mentioned in <i>Berachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i> The Rambam interprets it as referring to an ordinary head covering. In contrast, Rabbenu Yitzchok Alfasi interprets the Talmud's statement as referring to donning the <i>tallit</i>.<br><i>Tosafot</i> and other Ashkenazic authorities maintain that this blessing should be reciting upon putting on a hat, yarmulke, or other headcovering.</i> he recites: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who crowns Israel in glory.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) associates this blessing with the prohibition of walking four cubits with one's head uncovered (<i>Shabbat</i> 118b; See <i>Hilchot De'ot</i> 5:6). Covering our heads symbolizes Israel's fear and awe before God's presence. This is their glory, as Isaiah 62:3 states: "You shall be a crown of glory in the hand of God."</i><br>When he passes his hands over his eyes,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In order to cause sleep to pass from their eyes, people often rub them vigorously.<br>Our text of <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states when "one opens his eyes." Many authorities favor this version over the text quoted by the Rambam (and Rav Yitzchok Alfasi) because <i>Shabbat</i> 108b warns of the dire consequences of touching one's eyes before washing one's hands in the morning. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46:2) suggests that people might have avoided the problem by cleaning their eyes with a cloth or rubbing their eyes with closed eyelids (<i>Sha'arei Teshuvah</i>).</i> he recites: [Blessed...] who opens the eyes of the blind.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This blessing is based on Psalms 146:18 and can be interpreted as a statement of gratitude for more than our physical eyesight. We thank God for the ability to see His greatness in creation. These are our metaphorical "eyes of reason."</i><br>When he sits up in his bed, he recites: [Blessed...] who unties those bound.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">thanking God for the ability to move our limbs after sleep.</i><br>When he lowers his feet from the bed and rests them on the ground, he recites: [Blessed...] who spreads the earth over the waters.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This blessing is based on Psalms 136:6: "[Give thanks] to the One who spread the earth over the waters, for His lovingkindness is eternal."</i><br>When he stands up, he recites: [Blessed...] who straightens the bowed.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on Psalms 146:8. This blessing expresses gratitude for the uniquely human characteristic of upright posture. It may also be interpreted as giving thanks to God, the source of salvation for those bowed and downtrodden.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) states that if one recites this blessing before the blessing "who unties those bound," he should refrain from reciting the latter blessing. Though there is some difference of opinion about the matter, most later authorities accept the <i>Tur's</i> decision.</i><br>When he washes his hands,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 6:2, the Rambam specifically states that one should recite this blessing before washing one's hands so that the blessing will precede the performance of the mitzvah (<i>Pesachim</i> 7a). Rabbenu Yonah states that one should not recite this blessing until after washing one's hands because until one has washed his hands, he is not in a fit state to mention God's name (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 4:2). (According to others, the recitation of this blessing should be delayed until after drying one's hands.)<br>The Rambam obviously does not accept this rationale since he requires many blessings to be recited before the washing of the hands as above.</i> he recites: [Blessed...] who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Although this washing was instituted by the Rabbis, this wording is appropriate, as is explained in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> regarding the washing of hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This washing is required in preparation for the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> and the morning service.<br>Based on the <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. I, 169b, 184b), the Rabbis rule that, on washing in the morning, one pours water over his hands three times alternately, i.e., once over the right hand, once over the left hand, once more over the right, etc. This is done in order to remove the spirit of impurity that remains after sleep.</i><br>When he washes his face,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Note Chapter 4, Halachah 2, which states that a person must wash his face in the morning as a preparation for prayer.</i> he recites:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">There are slight, but significant, differences between the text of the blessing here and its source in <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who removes the bonds of sleep from my eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The last traces of drowsiness disappear when one washes his face (<i>Avudraham</i>).<br><b>and slumber from my eyelids. May it be Your will, God, -</b> This entire prayer is considered one blessing. Therefore, one should not recite "Amen" after hearing a colleague recite the opening sentence of the blessing (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 46:1). <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 46a) states that one should say "and may it be..." to emphasize that the prayer is a single blessing.<br><b>my Lord and Lord of my fathers, that You accustom me -</b> The Rambam, and similarly the <i>Tur</i>, use the first person singular, thus, making the prayer an individual statement. However, most siddurim use the first person plural, "us."<br><b>to the performance of [Your] commandments and do not accustom me to sins or transgressions. Cause the positive inclination to rule over me and not the evil inclination. -</b> Though we always have free will, we ask God to strengthen our potential for making the proper choice (<i>Taz</i>).<br><b>Strengthen me in Your commandments and grant my portion in Your Torah. Allow me to find favor, lovingkindness, and mercy in Your eyes and the eyes of all who see me and bestow upon me benevolent kindnesses. Blessed are You, God, who bestows benevolent kindnesses. -</b> The kindnesses refer to the return of the soul in the morning (<i>Tosafot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) and also the instilling of a spirit of purity within us (Rikanti).</i> and slumber from my eyelids. May it be Your will, God, my Lord and Lord of my fathers, that You accustom me to the performance of [Your] commandments and do not accustom me to sins or transgressions. Cause the positive inclination to rule over me and not the evil inclination. Strengthen me in Your commandments and grant my portion in Your Torah. Allow me to find favor, lovingkindness, and mercy in Your eyes and the eyes of all who see me and bestow upon me benevolent kindnesses. Blessed are You, God, who bestows benevolent kindnesses.
Whenever one enters the toilet,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah is dealt with among the morning blessings, since a person generally relieves himself shortly after arising. Nevertheless, its instructions apply whenever one relieves himself.<br>[Note, however, the opinion quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 6:1, which maintains that the blessing, אשר יצר, can be considered as one of the morning blessings and should be recited even if one does not relieve oneself.]</i> before entering, he says:<br>Be honored, holy honorable ones, servants of the Most High.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is quoted from <i>Berachot</i> 60b, but there are slight variations between the way the passage appears in the Talmud, the manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, and its published text.<br>In his commentary to <i>Berachot</i>, Rashi explains that this statement is addressed to the angels that accompany us at all times as Psalms 91:11 states: "He will command all His angels for you, to guard you in all your ways."</i> Help me. Help me. Guard me. Guard me.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., continue to do that which you have been commanded even though I am about to take leave of you momentarily.</i> Wait for me<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for it is not fitting for the angels to enter a toilet.</i> until I enter and come out, as this is the way of humans.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Avudraham</i> states that this prayer should only be recited by extremely righteous men. Anyone else who recites it would appear conceited. For this reason, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 3) rules that this prayer is not recited today.</i><br>After he<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">relieves himself, whether he defecated or urinated (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, in contrast to an opinion quoted by the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> which rules that the blessing which follows should be recited only after defecating).</i> comes out, he recites:<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who formed man in wisdom<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Targum Yonaton</i> to Ezekiel 28:12 which uses similar terms to praise the Divine wisdom manifest in the creation of the human body.</i> and created within him many openings and cavities.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the mouth, nose, anus.</i> It is revealed and known before the throne of Your glory that if one of them were to be blocked or if one of them were to be opened, it would be impossible to exist for even one moment.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) quotes <i>Bereishit Rabbah</i> (1:3) which states that unlike a manmade container which is unable to hold liquid when pierced by even the smallest pin, the body that God created is full of holes and cavities which open and close in a manner that allows man to continue to exist.<br>The <i>Midrash Tanchuma</i> (<i>Shemini</i>) relates: Happy are you, Israel! A mitzvah was granted correspondent to each and every one of your limbs.... The numerical value of חלולים חלולים is 248, the number of limbs in the human body and the number of positive commandments in the Torah.</i> Blessed are You, God, who heals all flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on Exodus 15:26: "For I am God, your healer."</i> and works wonders.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on Psalms 77:5: "You are the God who works wonders." The phrase מפליא לעשות is found, in a different context, in Judges 13:19.<br>The <i>Darchei Moshe</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 6) explains that the wonders spoken of in this blessing refers to the maintenance of our spiritual soulsáwithin ouráphysical bodies. This may explain the accepted order of the blessings in which א-להי נשמה (Halachah 3) is recited immediately after this blessing, אשר יצר.</i>
When one fastens his belt,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam accepts the interpretation most commentaries have given to the words מייניה אסר in <i>Berachot</i> 60b, the source for this blessing. The Ra'avad interprets the Talmud as referring to putting on one's trousers.</i> he recites: [Blessed...universe,] who girds Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Taz</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) points out that, of the morning blessings, only this and the blessing uttered after covering one's head (עוטר ישראל בתפארה) mention Israel. The other blessings praise God for kindnesses enjoyed by all people. In contrast, these two blessings highlight actions performed by the Jewish people as an expression of modesty. The belt is intended to separate the heart from the lower half of the body (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 2:7 and 3:17). The covering of one's head signifies one's awareness of the Divine Presence, as stated above (See Halachah 4).<br>The <i>Avudraham</i> notes that this blessing is conceptually rooted in Jeremiah 12:11: "'As a girdle clings to a man's loins so have I caused the whole of the House of Israel and the House of Judah to cleave unto Me,' says God; 'that they might be unto Me, a people, a name, a praise and a glory.'”</i> with strength.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Avodat Yitzchok</i> explains that a belt or girdle protects a person's body and affords him greater strength. Rav Asher of Lunil explains that this blessing refers to the spiritual strength granted to Israel through Torah study.</i><br>When he puts on his shoes, he recites: [Blessed...universe,] for You have provided me<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Our text follows the published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The same text is also quoted by Rav Yitzchok Alfasi and Rabbenu Asher.<br>However, our text of <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the source for this blessing, reads: שעשה לי כל צרכי - "who provides me with all my needs"; referring to God in the third person rather than in the second person. This version is found in most siddurim today. It is also found in the authoritative Oxford manuscript of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and many Yemenite manuscripts.</i> with all my needs.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Several different explanations are given for the association of this blessing with the putting on of one's shoes. The <i>Avudraham</i> states simply that a person is unable to move about freely without shoes. Therefore, putting on shoes enables him to acquire whatever he needs.<br>Rav Shlomo Kluger cites our Sages' statement, "A person should sell everything he owns and buy shoes," as evidence that a person who has shoes to wear has acquired that which is most necessary.<br>The <i>Sh'loh</i> quotes the Maharshal as explaining the source for this blessing as Psalms 8:6-9.<br>You have made him [man] a little lower than angels.... You have given him dominion over the works of Your hands. You have put everything under his feet, all sheep and oxen, and also the beasts of the field, the birds of the sky and the fish of the seas.<br>Wearing leather shoes demonstrates the way in which the animal kingdom has been placed "under man's feet" and makes us aware that the dominion we have been granted over all creation allows us to acquire "all our needs."</i><br>When he walks to depart on his way, he recites:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Our text of <i>Berachot</i> 60b and similarly, most contemporary siddurim, place this blessing before the previous one. Nevertheless, Rav Yitzchok Alfasi and Rabbenu Asher follow the same order as the Rambam.</i> [Blessed... universe,] who prepares the steps of man.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This blessing is based on Psalms 37:23: "The steps of man are ordered by God" and Proverbs 20:24: "The steps of man are from God," both of which imply that in addition to giving man the ability to walk, God also guides the direction of his steps.</i><br>[Also,] every day, a person should recite:<br>Blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Menachot</i> 43b, the following three blessings are recited daily as an expression of thanks for the renewal of our being. Some authorities require a person to have seen a gentile, woman, or slave before reciting the appropriate blessing. However, the Rambam maintains that the blessing should be recited each morning unconditionally (The Responsa of Rav Avraham, the Rambam's son).</i> are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has not made me a non-Jew.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">We thank God for creating us with the opportunity to relate to Him through the Torah and mitzvot which were given only to the Jews.<br><i>Menachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) uses a positive statement for the formula of this blessing: "Blessed... who has made me a Jew." Rabbenu Asher quotes this text. The Rambam's version is found in the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 9:2. Rabbenu Yitzchok Alfasi also follows the Jerusalem Talmud.<br>The <i>Levush, Orach Chayim</i> 46, explains the reason for reciting the blessing in the negative: <i>Eruvin</i> 13a states that it would have been better for man not to have been created than to have been created (i.e., man's soul was in a higher state before its descent into the world than it is while in this world). Therefore, we do not express thanks for being created. Nevertheless, having been created, we thank God for not creating us as gentiles since gentiles have a far lesser number of commandments to fulfill.<br>The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> explains that the negative formula was chosen for the following reason. Our Sages required that we recite three blessings each morning. Torah law states that whenever a single blessing covers two different circumstances, the second blessing may not be recited. Therefore, since a statement thanking God "for making me a Jew" would make the blessing for "not making me a servant" superfluous, it is preferable to recite this blessing using the negative formula.</i><br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has not made me a woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A woman is not obligated to fulfill all the positive mitzvot whose performance is limited to a specific time. As a result, a man has many more mitzvot to fulfill and recites this blessing thanking God for that privilege.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) mentions that a woman should recite a blessing thanking God "for making me according to His will."</i><br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has not made me a servant.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a Canaanite servant who is not a full member of the Jewish people (see <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 13:11). Like women, a Canaanite servant is exempt from all the positive mitzvot whose performance is limited to a specific time.<br>The published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and <i>Menachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) list these blessings in this order. However, the authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, Rav Yitzchok Alfasi, and most siddurim today reverse the sequence of the last two blessings. This order reflects an ascending hierarchy, with women who are endowed with the holiness of the Jewish people, placed after servants.</i>
These eighteen blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Only seventeen blessings are recited in the morning. The eighteenth blessing is <i>hamapil</i>, the blessing recited before retiring at night. Nevertheless, as explained in the commentary to Halachah 3, the Rambam views it as connected to the morning blessings.<br>The eighteen blessings correspond to the eighteen blessings of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, and also to the number of times God's name is mentioned in Psalms 29, in the song sung by the Jewish People after the splitting of the Red Sea (Exodus 15:1-19), and in the three sections of <i>Kri'at Shema</i>. (See also our commentary on Chapter 1, Halachah 4.)</i> do not have a particular order.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as might be inferred from the previous halachot. (The practice of reciting the blessings in a set order is discussed in Halachah 9.)</i> Rather, one recites each of them in response to the condition for which the blessing was instituted, at the appropriate time.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements in this and the following two halachot are all based on the idea that these blessings are ברכות הודאה - "blessings of thanks." Therefore, a person who does not receive the benefit associated with a particular blessing does not recite the blessing thanking God for that particular condition.<br>Rav Natrunai Gaon and Rav Amram Gaon differ with the Rambam. They consider these blessings to be ברכות שבח - "blessings of praise" for the goodness which God has granted the world as a whole and not thanks for benefit which one has derived as an individual. This opinion is accepted by the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) and other Ashkenazic halachic authorities. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and the Sephardic authorities accept the Rambam's view with certain reservations.</i><br>What is implied?<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam explained, each blessing is recited in response to a particular action generally carried out each morning. Therefore, should these actions be performed in a different order, the sequence of the blessings is changed correspondingly.</i> One who fastens his belt while still in his bed recites [the blessing] "who girds Israel with strength."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although in Halachah 6, the Rambam stated that this blessing should be recited after one has gotten off his bed, if one fastens his belt while still in bed, he recites the appropriate blessing at that time.</i> One who hears the voice of the rooster recites [the blessing] "who gives understanding to the rooster."<br>Any<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah is restated here as a prelude to the law that follows, i.e., that one not obligated in a blessing should not recite it. According to the Rambam, one who did not hear a rooster in the morning does not recite the blessing of הנותן לשכוי בינה.</i> blessing in which one is not obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">because one has not personally derived the benefit associated with the mitzvah</i> should not be recited.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, this is one of the differences between the Rambam's conception of the morning blessings and that of the other Geonim, who maintain that the blessings should be recited regardless. In consideration of the Rambam's position, the <i>Shulchan Aruch (Orach Chayim</i> 46:8<i>)</i> suggests reciting the blessings without mentioning God's name. The Ramah maintains that one should follow the other opinion.<br>Even among those who accept the Rambam's conception of these blessings, some authorities would disagree regarding this particular blessing. With this blessing, we are not thanking God for hearing the rooster's crow itself, but for the benefit which we receive from the daylight which the rooster discerns. (See <i>Tosafot</i> and Rabbenu Asher, <i>Berachot</i> 60b.)</i>
What is implied?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah exemplifies the final principle mentioned in the previous halachah, that a person should not recite a blessing if he does not derive the benefit associated with it.</i> One who sleeps in his outer garment<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in contrast to pajamas or underwear</i> should not recite the blessing "who clothes the naked" upon rising.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since, though he is clothed, he performed the activity associated with the blessing on the previous day.</i><br>One who walks barefoot does not recite the blessing, "for You have provided me with all my needs."<br>On<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the blessing associated with wearing shoes.<br>On Yom Kippur and the ninth of Av, when one does not wash, - Washing is one of the five afflictions forbidden on these two days. See <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Asor</i> 1:5 and 3:1-6 and <i>Hilchot Ta'aniot</i> 5:10.</i> Yom Kippur and the ninth of Av, when one does not wash, one does not recite the blessing <i>al netilat yadayim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In Hilchot Sh'vitat Asor 3:2, the Rambam qualifies the prohibition against washing by stating: "One [who is] soiled from excrement or dirt may wash the dirty area in the normal fashion without worry." He also allows a woman to wash her hands in order to feed her child.<br>The source for the Rambam's statements, <i>Yoma</i> 77b, explains that the reason for this leniency is the presence of <i>shivta</i> on her hands. Rashi interprets this as רוח רעה, the spirit of impurity which rests on our hands before the morning washing.<br><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Yoma</i>, <i>ibid.</i>) rules that one does wash his hands upon rising in the morning on Yom Kippur since רוח רעה is considered like any filth and may be removed in its normal fashion. As apparent from the previous halachot, the Rambam does not consider רוח רעה as a significant factor and forbids such washing. (See <i>Lechem Mishneh, Hilchot Sh'vitat Asor</i>.)<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 613:2) accepts <i>Tosafot's</i> opinion and states:<br>One washes his hands in the morning [on Yom Kippur] and recites על נטילת ידים. One should be careful to wash only the ends of the joints of his fingers.<br>The Ramah adds: "And he should not intend to enjoy the washing, and do so [only] to remove the רוח רעה from his hands."</i> nor the blessing "who removes the bonds of sleep...."<br>One<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">which is associated with washing one's face. Since washing in this manner is prohibited on these days, one does not recite this blessing.<br>This ruling is also questioned by other halachic authorities. The Ra'avad states that since it is permissible to clean one's face if it is dirty, one may clean the dirt that collects in one's eyes overnight and recite the blessing associated with this washing.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 613:4 rules that one who is particularly fastidious may wash his face with water on the morning of Yom Kippur. The Ramah adds that the custom is to be very strict in this regard and not even to wash the eyes. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (basing himself on the <i>Bayit Chadash</i>) permits one to moisten his fingertips and clean the dirt from his eyes. All of these laws regarding Yom Kippur also apply to the ninth of Av.<br>One who does not relieve himself - in the morning.</i> who does not relieve himself does not recite the blessing, "who created man in wisdom...."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as part of the morning blessings.</i> The same applies regarding the remainder of the blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Though Ashkenazic authorities do not accept the Rambam's ruling as explained above, we do find a law which parallels the Rambam's stance here. Even according to the Ashkenazic authorities, one should not recite the blessing "who has provided me with all my needs" on Yom Kippur or Tish'ah B'Av, since it is forbidden for everyone to wear shoes on these days.</i>
It is the custom of the people in the majority of our cities to recite these blessings one after another in the synagogue, whether or not they are obligated in them.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This practice has its source in the opinions of the other Geonim mentioned above. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) explains the custom:<br>Since one's hand are not clean [upon rising], the [rabbis] instituted the practice of reciting the [blessings] in order in the synagogue. [Another reason for the institution of this practice is that] many of the common people do not know how to recite the [blessings] and when they are recited in the synagogue, they can answer "Amen" afterwards, and thus, fulfill their obligation.<br>This is a mistake and it is not proper to follow this practice. One should not recite a blessing unless he is obligated to. - The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam sees two difficulties in this practice:a) The blessings are not recited at their proper time, i.e., immediately after the action to which they are a response;b) In the synagogue, all the blessings are recited and it is improper to recite a blessing that one is not obligated to recite.<br>In answer to the first objection, Rabbenu Asher and Rabbenu Yonah explain that there is no problem with reciting these blessings after the actions to which they relate have been completed. The only blessings that must be recited in direct conjunction with the deed with which they are associated are those recited before fulfilling commandments. However, blessings of thanks or praise may be uttered later. This opinion is accepted by all Ashkenazic authorities.<br>Today, many people recite these blessings at home after washing and dressing. In certain communities, they are recited communally in the synagogue. In all cases, however, each individual who is able to read the blessings from a siddur or knows them by heart should recite the blessings himself every morning.</i><br>This is a mistake and it is not proper to follow this practice. One should not recite a blessing unless he is obligated to.
One who rises to study Torah, whether the Written or Oral Law,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">There is debate in the Talmud about this matter. Some Sages did not require the recitation of a blessing for the study of certain portions of the Oral Law (See <i>Berachot</i> 11b). Nevertheless, the decision quoted by the Rambam is accepted by all halachic authorities. The <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 47:2 explains the reason for this decision, "They [all portions of the Oral Law] are all Torah and were given to Moses at Mount Sinai."</i> before he recites the <i>Shema</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 11b states:<br>Once one has recited the <i>Shema</i>, he need not recite a blessing [for Torah study] since he has fulfilled his obligation with <i>Ahavah Rabbah</i> (the second blessing before the <i>Shema</i>).<br>This blessing praises God for teaching our ancestors "the laws that bring eternal life" and includes our prayers that He "grant our hearts understanding... to learn and to teach... all the teachings of Your Torah." Therefore, <i>Ahavah Rabbah</i> is considered as comparableáto the blessings recited before Torah study (Rashi, <i>Berachot</i>, <i>ibid.</i>).<br>Rabbenu Asher quotes an opinion in the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 5:1) which states that the blessing of <i>Ahavah Rabbah</i> can replace the blessings before the study of the Torah only when one studies immediately after reciting the <i>Shema</i> and <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. The Ra'avad and the Rashba, however, maintain that the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> is also considered as Torah study. Therefore, if one recites the <i>Shema</i> after <i>Ahavah Rabbah</i>, one is considered to have fulfilled his obligation.<br>The Rambam appears to follow the latter opinion, however, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 47:8) accepts Rabbenu Asher's view.<br>should wash his hands beforehand, - to fulfill the obligation of נטילת ידים upon rising in the morning.</i> should wash his hands beforehand, recite [the following] three blessings, and then study.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Both here and in Halachah 11, the Rambam states that one must study immediately after reciting these blessings. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 47) explains the motivating principle: All blessings recited before the performance of a commandment must be followed immediately by the fulfillment of the commandment.<br>This position is, however, not universally accepted. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 11b), Rabbenu Yonah, and the Mordechai disagree and do not require the repetition of the blessings even if an interruption was made between their recitation and Torah study. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>,:9) warns against making such an interruption.<br>[The comparison of the blessings before Torah study to the blessings of the mitzvot is not universally accepted. In addition to the opinion of the Ramban mentioned in the following halachah, the <i>Levush</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 47) compares the blessings before Torah study to the blessings recited before partaking of food. Just as one thanks God for the satisfaction granted from food and other physical things, one blesses Him for the satisfaction derived from Torah study. See also <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 47:1, <i>Likkutei Sichot</i> Vol. 14.]</i><br>[These blessings] are:<br>[Blessed... universe,] who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us concerning the words of Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There are two different versions of this blessing:a) one which has its source in the published text of the Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 11b, and concludes: לעסוק בדברי תורה - "to occupy [ourselves] in the words of Torah."b) the version quoted by the Rambam: על דברי תורה - "concerning the words of Torah." This version is also found in the siddur of Rav Amram Gaon and the <i>Halachot</i> of Rav Yitzchok Alfasi.<br>The siddurim of the Sephardim, Yemenites and Nusach Ari follow the Rambam's text, whereas Nusach Ashkenaz reads לעסוק בדברי תורה.[<br>In both versions, this blessing contains thirteen words corresponding to the thirteen principles of Biblical exegesis that apply to the Torah (יºג מדות שהתורה נדרשת בהן, <i>Shulchan Tahor</i>).]</i><br>And<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The presence here of the Hebrew letter <i>vav</i> (ו) which is generally translated as "and" is the subject of much discussion among the Rabbis.<br><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 46a) explains that, according to <i>Rabbenu Tam</i>, any blessing that follows another blessing of only a single line must itself begin "Blessed are You..." and cannot be included in the phrase "Blessed are You..." in the first blessing. Therefore, since this paragraph does not begin "Blessed are You...," it appears to be a continuation of the first blessing and not a blessing of its own. The <i>vav</i> - "and" - indicates the connection between the two paragraphs.<br>The Rambam, however, clearly states that the second paragraph is considered as a blessing in its own right and not a continuation of the first blessing. The <i>vav</i> would, therefore, appear to be superfluous. Though most printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> include a <i>vav</i>, the authoritative Oxford manuscript of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Yemenite manuscripts, and the Ramah's text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> all lack a <i>vav</i>. (Note <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 47:5, which explains that even according to the opinion that the second paragraph is a separate blessing, adding the <i>vav</i> is in place.)<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 47:6 rules that the <i>vav</i> should be recited. The Ramah adds that although the custom is not to say it, it should be said. Most siddurim of <i>Nusach Ashkenaz</i> and <i>Sefard</i> include the <i>vav</i>.</i> please, God, our Lord, make pleasant the words of Your Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">We ask God to let us appreciate the sweetness of His Torah so that we will study it with love (Rashi, <i>Berachot</i> 11b).</i> in our mouths and in the mouths of Your people, the entire House of Israel. May we, our offspring,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> adds "and our offsprings' offspring" as a reference to the statement (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 85a) that the Torah will never depart from a family which has three generations of Torah scholars. However, this version is not included in many siddurim.</i> and the offspring of Your people, be knowers of Your name and among those who occupy themselves with Your Torah. Blessed are You, God, who teaches Torah to His people, Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This version is found in the published texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and the published texts of <i>Berachot</i> 11b, the source for this blessing. However, in his responsa, the Rambam suggests a different conclusion for this blessing: "Blessed are You, God, the Giver of the Torah." He explains the reasons why he favors the latter version:<br>This [wording] alludes to our recognition that God gave us the Torah so that we will learn to do everything it states. [The blessing is a request] that He open our hearts to the study of Torah, for this was the reason it was given to us as a heritage.However, to conclude [the blessing with] "who teaches Torah..." is incorrect, for God... does not teach us the Torah. Though in His love for us, He separated us from the gentiles and gave us the Torah of truth, the actual performance of the mitzvah is left to us and doing His will is in our hands.</i><br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has chosen us from among all the nations and given us His Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The designation of Israel as God's chosen people is associated with His giving them the Torah on Mount Sinai (<i>Tur, Orach Chayim</i> 47).</i> Blessed are You, God, who gives the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) refers to this as "the most exalted of these blessings" because it contains thanks to God and praise of Israel and the Torah (Rashi).<br>Rabbenu Yonah points out that although this blessing follows directly after another blessing, it departs from the normal pattern of such blessings and begins "Blessed are You...." He explains that this is because this blessing is also often recited in isolation, before the reading of the Torah in the synagogue.<br>The recitation of the blessings of the Torah is very important. <i>Bava Metzia</i> 85a states that Eretz Yisrael was destroyed because the Jews forsook the Torah. Our Sages clarified that statement, explaining that it means that the people would study Torah without reciting the blessings before it. A person who studies Torah without reciting the blessings fails to realize its holiness and sanctity, therefore, it is considered as if he ""forsook the Torah.''</i>
One is obligated to recite these three blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The nature of this obligation is debated among the commentaries. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 47) and others understand the Rambam as considering these blessings similar to any other blessing recited before the performance of a mitzvah and hence, of Rabbinic origin.<br>Nevertheless, <i>Berachot</i> 21a and <i>Yoma</i> 37a state that the obligation to recite a blessing on Torah study stems from the Torah itself. In keeping with this view, the Ramban (<i>Hosafot</i> to <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, positive commandment 15) reckons the recitation of the blessings of the Torah as a separate mitzvah.</i> every day.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps with the words "every day," the Rambam is implying that these blessings should be recited even if one does not sleep at night. See <i>Magen Avraham</i> 47:11.<br>The blessings are recited only once a day. In contrast to the blessings recited over other mitzvot, even if one interrupts his study and occupies himself with other matters, one need not recite a second blessing. The <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 4:7 gives two reasons for this difference:1) The blessings before the Torah resemble the other morning blessings. A person enjoys the benefits associated with the morning blessings intermittently throughout the day, yet he only recites the blessings once in the morning. Similarly, though one may study at several different occasions in a single day, the blessings are recited only once daily.2) The obligation to study Torah is incumbent upon one throughout the entire day and, at all times, one's attention should be on one's studies.</i> Afterwards, one should read a few words of Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If these blessings are comparable to other blessings recited before the performance of mitzvot, the mitzvah (studying Torah) must also be fulfilled immediately after the recitation of the blessing (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> [To fulfill this obligation,] the people adopted the custom of reading the Priestly Blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Priestly Blessing is found in Numbers 6:22-27. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 11b) also mentions the custom of reciting the Priestly Blessing each morning and Rav Amram Gaon includes the Priestly Blessing in his siddur.<br>The <i>Avudraham</i> mentions that the recitation of these verses arouses Divine blessing for the Jewish people. The <i>Even Yarchi</i> notes that the verses of the Priestly Blessing contain 60 letters, corresponding to the 60 tractates in the Talmud.</i> In certain places, they recite [the passage, (Numbers 28: 1-9)]: "Command the children of Israel...,"<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">These verses describe the morning sacrifice offered daily in the Holy Temple. The <i>Machzor Vitri</i> (an early siddur of French origin) does not include the Priestly Blessing and mentions these verses instead.</i> and there are places where they read both of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In the Order of the Prayers for the Entire Year found at the conclusion of <i>Sefer Ahavah</i>, the Rambam includes both these passages, with the verses describing the sacrifices preceding the Priestly Blessings.<br>The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 47:9) both understand the Priestly Blessing to be a fulfillment of the requirement to study Torah after the blessings. The verses of the Daily Sacrifice are recited later, with the intention that our recitation of these verses will compensate for our inability to actually offer these sacrifices (<i>Ta'anit</i> 27b, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 48).</i><br>Also, [it is proper] to read chapters or laws from the Mishnah and the <i>Beraitot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">so that we will have studied passages from the written law, the Mishnah, and the Talmud and thus, emphasize how our obligation to study Torah applies to all three. Note <i>Kiddushin</i> 30a and <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 1:11, which advises one to divide his Torah study, a third in the Written Law, a third in Mishnah and a third in Talmud.<br>In his Order of Prayers, the Rambam lists the following passages from the oral law after the the Priestly Blessings:<br>These are the precepts for which no fixed limit is prescribed: <i>Pe'ah</i> (the corners of the field left unharvested for the needy, Leviticus 23:22 , <i>Bikkurim</i> (the first fruits, Exodus 23:19 , <i>HaRei'on</i> (the pilgrimage offerings, Deuteronomy 16:16-17), acts of kindness and the study of Torah. [A Mishnah, <i>Pe'ah</i> 1:1.]These are the precepts, the fruits of which a person enjoys in this world, while the principle remains [for him] in the world to come: Honoring one's mother and father, acts of kindness, concentration during prayer, visiting the sick, waking early to go to the study hall, hospitality to guests, bringing peace between man and his fellow man, and the study of Torah, which is equivalent to them all. [A <i>beraita</i>, <i>Shabbat</i> 127a]Rav Zeira says: "Jewish women imposed a stringent practice upon themselves. Even if they saw a drop of [vaginal] blood the size of a mustard seed, they would count seven clean days after it." [An example of Torah law, <i>Berachot</i> 31a, <i>Nidah</i> 66a, and <i>Megillah</i> 28b]It was taught in the School of Elijah: Whoever studies Torah law every day is assured of life in the world to come [as implied by Chabakuk 3:6]: "<i>Halichot</i> (the paths of) the world are his." Do not read <i>halichot</i>, but <i>halachot</i> (Torah laws) [<i>Megillah</i> 28b].Rabbi Eliezer said in the name of Rabbi Chanina: Torah scholars increase peace in the world as [implied by Isaiah 54:13]: "And all your children will be students of God and great will be the peace of your children." [<i>Berachot</i> 64a.]</i>
The Sages praised<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kapach notes that at the beginning of this chapter, the Rambam attributes the order of the prayers to the <i>Anshei K'nesset HaGedolah</i>. Therefore, we can assume that they also instituted the practice of reciting verses of praise before the blessings of the <i>Shema</i>. (Note the statement of Rabbi Simlai, <i>Berachot</i> 32a, which suggests praising God before reciting the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.) Though from the passage from <i>Shabbat</i> quoted below one might infer that the recitation of these verses was instituted later, perhaps this refers to the designation of the specific verses to be recited, while the practice of reciting verses itself had already existed.</i> those who recite songs from the Book of Psalms every day; from "A song of praise by David... (<i>Tehillah l'David</i>)" (Psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 4b states that anyone who recites Psalms 145 three times each day is guaranteed a place in the World to Come.</i> 145) until the end of the Book [of Psalms].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 118b relates:<br>Rabbi Yossi said: "May my portion be with those who complete [the recitation of] <i>Hallel</i> every day."That cannot be. Behold, the master taught that one who recites <i>Hallel</i> every day is considered as a blasphemer and abuser.In regard to what did [Rabbi Yossi] make his statement? In regard to פסוקי דזמרא - the verses of song.<br>The Rambam understands פסוקי דזמרא as referring to the last six chapters of Psalms (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). This definition is also accepted by Rav Yitzchok Alfasi and Rabbenu Asher.</i> It has become customary to read verses before and after them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his Order of Prayers for the Entire Year, the Rambam mentions a series of verses from Psalms and from I Chronicles 29:10-14 which are recited before and after these six Psalms. See <i>Soferim</i> 17:11. These verses are customarily recited today in both Ashkenazic and Sephardic communities.<br>They instituted a blessing before the [recitation of the] songs, Baruch She'amar..., - Tradition states that the <i>Anshei K'nesset HaGedolah</i> received this blessing and the blessing,<i>Yishtabach</i>, from Heaven. Therefore, our custom is to recite these blessings standing (<i>Or Zerua</i>).<br>The <i>Pri Chadash</i> takes issue with this view and maintains that these blessings were instituted by the <i>Geonim</i> after the conclusion of the Talmud. Though the <i>Or Zerua's</i> opinion is based only on "tradition," the fact that the Jerusalem Talmud refers to these blessings, as mentioned below, indicates that they were recited during the Talmudic era.</i><br>They instituted a blessing before the [recitation of the] songs, <i>Baruch She'amar</i>..., and a blessing after [concluding] them, <i>Yishtabach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> explain that the blessings, <i>Baruch She'amar</i> and <i>Yishtabach</i> are considered a single unit. Therefore, <i>Yishtabach</i> is considered a blessing which follows directly after another blessing - despite the verses recited between the two blessings. Accordingly, it does not begin "Blessed are You, God...."<br>Though the verses of פסוקי דזמרא are not considered an interruption between the blessings, idle conversation certainly would be. Thus, the Jerusalem Talmud rules that anyone who talks between <i>Baruch She'amar</i> and <i>Yishtabach</i> has transgressed and is not fit to be part of the Jewish army (which must be composed only of righteous men).</i> Afterwards, one recites the blessings for <i>Kri'at Shema</i> and recites the <i>Shema</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:5-8.</i>
