There are places where they are accustomed to recite the Song of the Sea (Exodus 14:30-15:26) each day<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Zohar</i> (II:54b) states that anyone who recites this song at present will merit to recite it in the Messianic age.<br>See also the <i>Tosefta, Berachot</i> (2:1) and the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 66:4 which emphasize (albeit in a different context) that the obligation to recall the exodus from Egypt each day should also include the recollection of the miracle of the splitting of the sea.</i> after they recite <i>Yishtabach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In his siddur, Rav Sa'adia Gaon writes that "though this is a fine custom, it is not obligatory or necessary."</i> Afterwards, they recite the blessings for the<i>Shema</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is improper to make an interruption between <i>Yishtabach</i> and the blessings of the <i>Shema</i>. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 54:3) However, verses of praise and prayer are not considered an interruption. Note the custom of reciting <i>Shir hama'alot</i> at this point in prayer during the Ten Days of Repentance.<br>Today, we recite the Song of the Sea before <i>Yishtabach</i>. <i>Sefer HaBatim</i> suggests that this is because it is also praise of God and thus, is appropriate within the context of פסוקי דזמרא.</i><br>There are places where they recite the song, <i>Ha'azinu</i> (Deuteronomy<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha'azinu</i> is also a song of general relevance. The Maggid of Meseritch advised reciting it daily as a means of evoking Divine blessing.<br>Among Sephardic Jews, there is a custom to recite <i>Ha'azinu</i> in place of the Song of the Sea on the Tish'ah b'Av because of the verses of reproof contained in it (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).</i> 32:1-43), and there are individuals who recite both of them. Everything is dependent on custom.
A person is obligated to recite 100 blessings [in the period of one] day and night.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 43b relates:<br>Rabbi Meir used to say: "One is obligated to recite 100 blessings every day as [implied by Deuteronomy 10:12]: 'And now Israel, what is it that God, your Lord, asks of you.'”<br>Rashi explains that the word, מה- "what" can be read as מאה - "100", allowing for the interpretation, "And now Israel, 100 [blessings] is what God, your Lord, asks of you."<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 46) quotes Rav Natrunai Gaon who attributes the institution of this custom to King David.</i> What are these 100 blessings?<br>The twenty-three blessings that we have counted in this chapter,<br>the<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the eighteen blessings discussed in Halachot 1-9, the three blessings recited before studying Torah (see Halachot 10, 11) and the two blessings associated with פסוקי דזמרא (see Halachah 12).</i> seven blessings before and after <i>Kri'at Shema</i> in the morning and in the evening;<br>When<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Two blessings before the <i>Shema</i> and one blessing afterwards in the morning; two blessings before the <i>Shema</i> and two afterwards in the evening. (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:5.)</i> one wraps himself in tzitzit, he recites: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to wrap ourselves in tzitzit.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:8.</i><br>When he puts on his Tefilin, he recites: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to put on Tefilin.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Hilchot Tefilin</i> 4:5, where the Rambam states that only one blessing should be recited when putting on <i>tefillin</i>. Other authorities require two blessings to be recited: one, on the Tefilah placed on the arm, and another, on the Tefilah placed on the head. See also the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 25:9 and the Ramah's notes there.</i><br>[One recites] three <i>Amidot</i>, each of which contains eighteen blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam mentions the addition of the nineteenth blessing in the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> in the next halachah. Here he is discussing the 100 blessings in their original historical context.</i><br>Behold, this is 86 blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the 23 of the morning blessings, the 7 associated with the <i>Shema</i>, 1 on <i>tefillin</i>, 1 on <i>tzitzit</i>, and 54 (18x3) from the three <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> prayers.</i><br>When one eats two meals, [one] during the day and [one] at night,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Pe'ah</i> 8:7, the Rambam explains that two meals, one during the day and one at night, is the normal fare for a person each day.</i> one recites 14 blessings, seven for each meal:<br>one when he washes his hands before eating,<br>and,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 6:2.</i> on the food itself, one before<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>HaMotzi</i>. See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 3:2.</i> and three afterwards,<br>on<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">These are the three blessings which constitute the Torah's requirement for the grace after meals:1) ברכת הזן,2) ברכת הארץ, and3) בונה ירושלים<br>See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 2:1. The Rambam mentions the fourth blessing included in the grace in the next halachah.</i> the wine, one before<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 7:14-15 which mentions the custom of reciting the grace after meals over a cup of wine. Before drinking from that wine, one recites the blessing: <i>borey pri hagefen</i>.</i> and one afterwards,<br>[a<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., מעין שלש - the blessing which is reciting after drinking wine. See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 8:15.</i> total] of seven.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">[a total] of seven. Thus, there are 100 blessings all told.</i><br> Thus, there are 100 blessings all told.
At present, since the [Sages] established the blessing [cursing] the heretics in prayer and added <i>hatov v'hameitiv</i> in the grace after meals,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the fourth blessing of the grace after meals. As explained in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 2:1, this blessing was instituted later than the first three blessings, its recitation having been ordained by the Sages of the Mishnah.</i> there are five more blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">recited every day.</i><br>On Sabbaths and holidays,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Yom Kippur presents a particular problem for although there are five prayer services, one is not permitted to eat and cannot recite blessings over food.</i> when the <i>Amidah</i> contains [only] seven blessings,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 5.</i> and similarly, on other days, if one is not obligated in all of these blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Rambam's view (see Halachot 7-9) that a person should not recite the morning blessings unless he performs the actions with which they are associated.</i> - e.g., one did not sleep at night,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If a person does not sleep at night, he misses at least two blessings: <i>hamapil</i> (Halachah 1) and <i>E-lohai neshamah</i> (Halachah 3). According to some opinions, he also should not recite <i>hama'avir sheinah</i> (Halachah 4).</i> nor loosen his belt,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">in which case, he would not be obligated to recite the blessing, <i>ozer Yisrael b'gevurah</i> - "who girds Israel with strength" - upon fastening it the following morning.</i> nor go to the bathroom,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">in which case, he is exempt from the blessing of <i>asher yatzar</i> discussed in Halachah 5.</i> or the like<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., any one of the morning blessings, with the exception of <i>shelo asani goy, shelo asani aved, shelo asani isha</i>.</i> - he must complete the 100 blessings by [reciting blessings over] fruits.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 16.<br><i>Menachot</i> 43b, the source for the requirement to recite 100 blessings, also recognized the difficulties inherent in fulfilling that requirement on the Sabbaths and holidays, and advises completing the sum of 100 blessings by reciting blessings before smelling spices or before eating sweets.<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> quotes an opinion that allows the blessings recited on the reading of the Torah and haftorah to be included in the calculation of the 100 blessings. This yields another 27 blessings. For this reason, <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> suggests that the blessings on the Torah should be recited loudly so that those present are able to answer "Amen." The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (46:8) suggests that one should rely on this opinion only when he cannot recite sufficient blessings on fruits.</i>
What is implied?<br>[When]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., how does one supplement the number of blessings recited on the Sabbath and festivals.</i> one eats a small amount of vegetables, he recites a blessing before<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>borey pri ha'adamah</i> (<i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 8:1).</i> and after it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>borey nefashot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> [When] he eats a small amount of a particular fruit, he recites a blessing before<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>borey pri ha'etz</i> (<i>ibid.</i>)</i> and after it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>borey nefashot</i> or <i>al hapeirot</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> He should count all the blessings [he recites] until he completes [the requirement of] 100 each day.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's choice of words implies that each day, a person should carefully keep track of the number of blessings he recites until he fulfills the requirement.<br>Note the comments of the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> who explains that although one should try to recite 100 blessings each day, one should be careful not to recite unnecessary blessings.</i>
The order of prayer is as follows:<br>In the morning, a person should rise early<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">so that he will be able to complete the recitation of the <i>Shema</i> and the blessing which follows it before sunrise, Hilchot Kri'at Shema 1:11).</i> and recite the blessings [mentioned above].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the morning blessings mentioned in Halachot 3-6, 10-11.</i> [Then,] he recites the songs [of praise] and the blessings before and after them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 12.</i> [Afterwards,] he recites the <i>Shema</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">in fulfillment of his obligation to recite the <i>Shema</i> each day (<i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:1).</i> [together with] the blessings before and after it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the two blessings before the <i>Shema</i> and the one blessing afterwards (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:5).</i> He should omit the <i>Kedushah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The passage which quotes the praises of the angels who recite "Holy, Holy, Holy is the God of Hosts, the whole world is full of His glory" (Isaiah 6:3 and "Blessed is the honor of God from His place" (Ezekiel 3:12). This passage is recited three times during the morning service, once in the first blessing before <i>Kri'at Shema</i>, once in the repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, and once at the conclusion of the service. (See Chapter 9, Halachot 4-5.)</i> from the first blessing before [the <i>Shema</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Precisely which portion of the blessing to omit is a matter of debate among the Rabbis. See <i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 59.</i> because an individual<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">praying without a quorum (<i>minyan</i>)</i> does not recite <i>Kedushah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 8, Halachot 4-6, any "holy matter" should not be recited in the absence of a <i>minyan</i> of ten people. The <i>Kedushah</i> recited when repeating the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> is considered as a "holy matter" of this nature. (See <i>Berachot</i> 21b, <i>Megillah</i> 23b. See also Chapter 8, Halachah 4, and Chapter 9, Halachah 5 for a discussion of the text of <i>Kedushah</i>.)<br>In this halachah, the Rambam equates all three recitations of the <i>Kedushah</i> in the morning service. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 59) cites Rav Natrunai Gaon as also sharing this opinion. There is no specific Talmudic or Midrashic source to this effect. However, the <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. II, 129b, 132b) also considers all three recitations of <i>Kedushah</i> to be bound by the same rulings.<br>Rabbenu Yonah quotes the opinion of the Ashkenazic authorities who distinguish between these recitations of <i>Kedushah</i>. They maintain that since, in the repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, we liken ourselves to the angels and recite the same praises, a <i>minyan</i> is necessary. However, the other <i>Kedushot</i> are simply narrative descriptions of the praises uttered by the angels and therefore, do not require a <i>minyan</i>.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 59:3) quotes the Rambam's position. The Ramah concludes that even an individual should recite the blessing without deleting the <i>Kedushah</i>.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> writes that Rabbi Avraham, the Rambam's son, states that his father later changed his position regarding this halachah and wrote in a responsum that one should recite <i>Kedushah</i> even without a quorum of ten. This is also recorded by <i>Orchot Chayim</i>, Rashba, and Rabbenu Manoach. However, there are other responsa which indicate that the Rambam did not change his mind.</i><br>When he concludes [the blessing,] <i>ga'al Yisrael</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The blessing beginning <i>Emet v'yatziv</i>, which follows the recitation of the <i>Shema</i>.</i> he should stand immediately to connect [the blessing of] redemption to prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 42a relates: "prayer should follow immediately after [the blessing of] redemption." <i>Berachot</i> 9b states: One who connects [the blessing of] redemption to prayer will not be harmed the whole day...[Rav Bruna] was a great man who experienced intense joy in his [performance of] mitzvot. One day, he connected [the blessing of] redemption to prayer and the smile did not leave his face all day.<br> The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 1:1) equates one who does not fulfill this practice to a loved one who knocks on the door of the king and then departs. When the king answers the door and sees no one there, he also turns away.</i> He should pray standing, as we have said.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2.</i><br>When he concludes, he should sit, fall on his face and recite the supplication prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 13.</i> [Afterwards,] he should lift up his head and recite a few [additional] supplications while sitting amid supplication.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In his Order of Prayers for the Entire Year, the Rambam lists these verses beginning with - "And we do not know what to do for our eyes are directed towards You" (II Chronicles 20:12).<br>In Chapter 4, Halachah 16, and Halachah 18 of this chapter, the Rambam requires one to sit a short while after prayer before taking his leave. Perhaps that is the basis for the suggestion to sit during these supplicatory prayers.</i><br>Afterwards, while sitting, he recites <i>Tehillah l'David</i> (Psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 4b states: "Anyone who recites <i>Tehillah l'David</i> three times daily is guaranteed a place in the World to Come." Since this Psalm is not included in the evening service, it is recited twice in the morning service.<b>adds supplications according to his ability -</b> In Chapter 9:5, the Rambam rules that one should recite the passage, <i>U'va l'Tzion go'el</i>. However, that passage includes the <i>Kedushah</i> and hence, according to the opinion mentioned above, should only be recited in communal prayer. Therefore, an individual recites only the supplications found at the end of that prayer.<br>In his Order of Prayers, the Rambam also mentions the custom of reciting the "song of the day" and of <i>eyn k'e-loheinu</i>.<br><b>and departs to his own affairs. -</b> The <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 155:1) quote <i>Berachot</i> 64a which states that after prayer, one should proceed to Torah study. It is unclear why the Rambam does not mention this point.</i> 145), adds supplications according to his ability and departs to his own affairs.
One begins the Minchah service by reciting <i>Tehillah l'David</i> (Psalm 145<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is the third time this psalm is recited in the daily prayers in keeping with the directive of <i>Berachot</i> 4b mentioned in the previous halachah.<br>Reciting <i>Tehillah l'David</i> here also fulfills the obligation (<i>Berachot</i> 31a, Chapter 4, Halachah 18) to pray "amid words of Torah." See also Chapter 9, Halachah 8.</i>) while sitting.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The siddur of Rav Sa'adia Gaon states that תהילה לדוד should be recited sitting until one is calm and properly prepared to pray. Only then should one stand and recite <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. See Chapter 4, Halachah 16.</i> Afterwards, one stands and recites the Minchah prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2.</i> When he finishes, he falls on his face and recites the supplication prayer,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 13.</i> raises his head and utters [more] supplication according to his ability and departs to his own matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the previous halachah.</i><br>In the evening service, he recites the <i>Shema</i> together with the blessings before and after it,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:5-6.</i> connects [the blessing of] redemption to prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous halachah. Note also the concluding statement of this halachah.</i> and prays standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2.</i> When he finishes, he sits a short while<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 16.</i> and departs.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the Rambam does not add "to his own matters" as in regard to the morning and afternoon service. Before the evening service, a person should have concluded his work and business affairs.</i><br>One who offers prayers of supplication after the evening service is praiseworthy.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 5, Halachah 15, the Rambam writes that the common custom is not to fall on one's face and recite supplications in the evening service. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 131) explains that both falling on one's face and night are associated with God's attribute of judgement and it would be inappropriate to combine the two.<br>It is unclear whether the Rambam is here referring to the practice of falling on one's face (<i>nefilat apayim</i>) or merely the recitation of supplicatory prayers (<i>tachanun</i>). Rav Amram Gaon records that in Babylonia, it was the custom to recite such prayers every night except Friday.</i><br>Even though one recites the blessing <i>hashkiveinu<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the second blessing recited after the <i>Shema</i> at night</i></i> after<i>ga'al Yisrael</i>, it is not considered as an interruption between [the blessing of] redemption and prayer, for they are both considered as one long blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hashkiveinu</i> is considered as a continuation of the theme of redemption (<i>Tosafot, Berachot</i> 4b) because it contains the request for God to save us from many different types of difficulties just as He redeemed us from Egypt (Maharit Algazi). The connection is further emphasized by the fact that in the exodus from Egypt, God protected the Jews on a night where permission was granted for the forces of destruction to act. Similarly, in <i>hashkiveinu</i>, we ask Him to protect us from "all evil matters and fears of night" (<i>Birkai Yosef</i>).</i>

Chapter 8

Communal prayer is always heard. Even when there are transgressors among [the congregation], the Holy One, blessed be He, does not reject the prayers of the many.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 8a cites Psalms 55:19: "He delivered my soul in peace from the battle against me, for there were many who strove with me," as the prooftext for this concept.</i> Therefore, a person should include himself in the community<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This phrase is quoted - somewhat out of context - from <i>Berachot</i> 29b-30a which explains why we recite our prayers in the plural. The Rambam borrows the concept and uses it to summarize the above teachings and derive the following directive.</i> and should not pray alone whenever he is able to pray with the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., though praying without a <i>minyan</i> is permissible, one should do so only when he has no other alternative.<br>One should always spend the early morning and evening [hours] in the synagogue - i.e., one should be found in the synagogue at the times of the three Daily Prayers; <i>Shacharit</i> in the morning, <i>Minchah</i> in the late afternoon and <i>Maariv</i> at night.<br>The Rambam uses the word ישכים - to rise early. Perhaps he is alluding to <i>Berachot</i> 47b which states:<br>One should always rise early and go to the synagogue in order to merit being counted among the first ten present. Even if 100 people come after him, he receives the reward of all of them.<br>Alternatively, perhaps he is referring to his statement, <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 1:11, that one should recite the <i>Shema</i> before sunrise.</i><br>One should always spend the early morning and evening [hours] in the synagogue, for prayer will not be heard<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., accepted by God</i> at all times<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> explains: Prayer which is recited with the proper intentions outside the synagogue may be accepted, but there is no guarantee that prayer will be accepted at all times...</i> except [when recited] in the synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 6a derives this concept from I Kings 8:28: "...to listen to the song and the prayer." "In the place of song (i.e., the synagogue where the people sing God's praises - Rashi), prayer should be [recited]."<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> adds that based on this concept, one should pray in the synagogue even when he is praying alone. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> also rules that if one is unable to go to the synagogue he should, at least, attempt to pray at the same time as the community. He quotes both these concepts in his <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 90:9.<br>Though this and the following two halachot mention the importance of a synagogue, the synagogue is discussed here only within the context of the importance of communal prayer. In Chapter 11, the Rambam focuses on the sanctity of the synagogue as a subject in its own right.</i><br>Anyone who has a synagogue in his city and does not pray [together] with the congregation in it is called a bad neighbor.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 8a derives this concept based on Jeremiah 12:14: "So says God regarding all my evil neighbors that touch the inheritance (i.e., the synagogues and houses of study) that I have bequeathed to My people Israel."<br>When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90:11) also quotes the continuation of the above Talmudic passage:<br>Furthermore, they cause dispersion to themselves and their children as [the verse in Jeremiah continues]: "Behold, I will uproot them from their land."</i>
It is a mitzvah to run to the synagogue as [Hoshea 6:3] states: "Let us know. Let us run to know God."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90) adds that it is also a mitzvah to run to perform other commandments.</i> A person should not take long steps when he leaves the synagogue. Instead, he should proceed [slowly,] step by step.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 6b) explains that hurrying away from the synagogue, suggests that one views the synagogue and the obligations contained therein to be a burden.</i><br>When one enters a synagogue, he should go in the distance of two doorways and then pray,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 8a) explains that we are obligated to enter the distance of two doorways widthwise in order not to appear anxious to leave. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90) explains that this refers to a distance of eight handbreadths.<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> interprets the Talmud's statement, "the measure of two doorways" as a matter of time, not distance. One should wait a few moments after entering the synagogue before he starts to pray.<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> also states that according to the interpretation that the "measure of two doorways" refers to actual distance, this requirement only applies when the synagogue is located near the public thoroughfare. If it is not, one may pray near the door. Rabbenu Yonah also mentions that a person whose fixed place for prayer in the synagogue is beside the door does not violate this requirement. It is obvious that he is sitting there not because of his desire to leave quickly, but because it is his seat in the synagogue.</i> [in order] to fulfill [the instructions of Proverbs 8:34] which states: "to guard the posts of My doors."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 5:1) points to the plural forms of "posts" and "doors" as indicating the need to enter the distance of two doorways.</i>
A study hall is greater<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is a more auspicious place to pray.</i> than a synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Other ramifications of this concept are mentioned in Chapter 11, Halachah 14, and in <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 4:9.</i> Even though [some of the] great Sages [lived in] cities where many synagogues were located, they would pray only in the place where they studied Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30b relates: "Even though there were thirteen synagogues in Tiberias, Rav Ami and Rav Assi would pray only between the pillars in the study hall."<br><i>Berachot</i> 8a interprets the phrase "the gates of Zion" in Psalms 87:2: "God loves the gates of Zion above all the dwellings of Jacob" as referring to "the gates of those who are בהלכה המצויינים (distinguished by the halachah studied there)" and states that they receive the highest degree of Divine attention.<br>The Talmud continues: Abbaye says: "Originally, I would study in my house and pray in the synagogue. After hearing this, I pray only where I study."</i><br>The above applies, [however, only] when one can participate in communal prayer there.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> mentions the view of certain rabbinic authorities who maintain that it is preferable for a scholar to pray in a study hall even without a <i>minyan</i>. However, he also mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Asher (See Responsum 4) who writes that if the scholars do not appear in the synagogue the common people will assume that their presence is also unnecessary. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 90:18) quotes the Rambam's position.</i>
What is implied by [the term,] communal prayer? One [person] prays aloud and all [the others] listen.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">With this statement, the Rambam touches on a point debated both by the Sages of the Talmud and the later Rabbis. The mishnah, <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 33b, states:<br>Just as the leader of the congregation is obligated [to pray], each individual is obligated. Rabban Gamliel states: "The leader of the congregation fulfills the obligation on behalf of the community."<br>The Talmud (34b) elaborates on the difference of opinion:<br>They asked Rabban Gamliel: "According to your opinion, why does the community pray?"He replied: "In order to allow the leader of the community to prepare his prayers."Rabban Gamliel asked them: "According to your opinion, why does the leader of the congregation pray aloud?"They replied: "In order to allow those who are not knowledgeable to fulfill their obligation."He told them: "Just as he fulfills the obligation on behalf of those who are not knowledgeable, he fulfills it on behalf of those who are knowledgeable."<br>The Sages of the Talmud continued to debate the issue. The ultimate decision accepted by Rav Yitzchok Alfasi, the Rambam (See Halachah 9), and Rabbenu Asher, is that during the entire year, the Sages' opinion is accepted. However, on Rosh HaShanah, because of the complexity of the blessings (See Halachah 10), Rabban Gamliel's opinion prevails.<br>Both Rabban Gamliel and the Sages agree that the practice of communal prayer involves two elements:a) the silent prayers of many individuals; andb) the <i>chazan's</i> repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.They differ, however, as to which of these elements is considered of primary importance. According to the Sages, the essential element of communal prayer is that ten or more people recite the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> at the same time. According to Rabban Gamliel, the essential element of communal prayer is, as the Rambam states in this halachah, the prayer of the leader of the congregation.<br>[Note <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 24, which explains that their difference of opinion revolves around a more general issue: Which is of higher priority, quantity (the prayers of the many) or quality (the prayers of the leader of the congregation)?]<br>The Rambam's statement here is difficult to place in perspective. Though in Halachah 9, he accepts the Sages' view (See also Chapter 9, Halachot 2-3) in this halachah, his opinion follows that of Rabban Gamliel. In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that after the Sages instituted the silent <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> to allow the leader of the congregation to prepare his prayers, even Rabban Gamliel would agree that all the individuals praying in a <i>minyan</i> also fulfill their obligation to pray by reciting their own prayers. Still it is difficult to understand why the Rambam would have the individuals forfeit the higher quality of communal prayer in order to pray individually.<br>In his notes to <i>Rosh HaShanah</i>, <i>ibid.</i>, Rav Chayim Soloveichik writes that to take into consideration the Rambam's statements in this halachah, after concluding his individual prayers, each person should stand and listen to the prayer of the leader of the congregation and thus, fulfill the mitzvah of communal prayer. Note <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, <i>ibid.</i>, which compares answering to the <i>chazan's</i> prayer to reciting a voluntary prayer (See Chapter 1, Halachah 9). By doing so, one will have both the advantages of individual and communal prayer.</i> This should not be done with fewer than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 4 and 5.</i> adult<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Males who have reached the age of thirteen years and a day and manifested signs of physical maturity. (See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:19.)<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam derives this halachah from <i>Berachot</i> 47b-48a which allows for the inclusion of a child in the <i>zimmun</i> for the grace after meals. (See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 5:7.) We assume that this leniency was granted only in regard to grace, but not in regard to prayer. See also <i>Bereishit Rabbah</i> 91:3.<br>Many Halachic authorities do not accept this conclusion. In his siddur, Rav Sa'adia Gaon rules that a child may be counted as the tenth in a <i>minyan</i>. Similarly, the <i>Ba'al HaMe'or</i> holds that even up to four minors may be counted in a <i>minyan</i> for prayer.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch (Orach Chayim</i> 55:4), rules that a minor may not be included in a <i>minyan</i> for prayer. The <i>Ramah</i> adds that under extraordinary circumstances, a minor may be counted. There are opinions that in such a case, the minor must be at least six years old, understand that prayer is service to God, and hold a <i>chumash</i> (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 55:5, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 55:5).</i> free males.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., excluding a Canaanite slave. However, a Jewish slave (עבד עברי) may be counted as part of a <i>minyan</i>. <i>Berachot</i> 47b relates that once, Rabbi Eliezer entered the synagogue and finding only nine people there, freed his slave to complete the quorum of ten.</i> The leader of the congregation is [counted as] one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Though there is no explicit source to this effect, a number of midrashim (e.g., <i>VaYikra Rabbah</i> 23:4) lead to this conclusion.<br>One of the communal rites listed in <i>Megillah</i> 23b which requires a <i>minyan</i> is ברכת אבלים - the blessing one utters to those in mourning. For the recitation of that blessing, the presence of ten men other than the mourner himself is required. (See <i>Hilchot Eivel</i> 13:1.) Perhaps the Rambam feels that this statement is necessary to clearly distinguish between the different requirements of a <i>minyan</i> for prayer and ברכת אבלים.</i><br>Even if some of them have already prayed and fulfilled their obligation, they can complete the [quorum of] ten<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Though they do not pray together with the others, their presence in the room where the others pray can complete a <i>minyan</i>.</i> provided the majority of the ten have not prayed.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soferim</i> 10:7 states:Our Sages in the West say [that <i>Kaddish</i> and <i>barchu</i> may be recited] even with seven. They explain their position [based on Judges 5:2]: "In the time of tumult in Israel when the people volunteered themselves, blessed be God." [The number of people correspond] to the number of words [in the verse which describes the blessing of God].There are those who say only six [are required], since ברכו is the sixth word [of the above verse].<br>The Rambam differentiates between the ruling regarding prayer and his statements in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 5:8 where he writes that it is necessary that seven people out of ten eat bread in order to constitute a <i>minyan</i> for <i>zimmun</i>, for "a noticeable majority" is required.<br>There are two views quoted in the name of Rabbenu Tam (<i>Tosafot, Megillah</i> 23b); one, requiring a minimum of seven people who have not prayed for a <i>minyan</i> just as seven who have eaten bread are required for a <i>minyan</i> in regard to grace after meals and, another, requiring, only five. However, the Rambam's opinion is universally accepted as halachah.<br>Similarly, we should not recite Kedushah, - See Chapter 7, Halachah 17.<br>In the <i>chazan's</i> repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, it is customary to recite three verses in the <i>Kedushah</i>:a) "Holy, Holy, Holy, is the Lord of Hosts; the entire earth is full of His glory" (Isaiah 6:3 ;b) "Blessed be the glory of God from His place" (Ezekiel 3:12 ;c) "The Lord shall reign forever, your God, O Zion, throughout all generations" (Psalms 146:10 .<br>Only the first two of these verses are recited in the <i>Kedushah</i> of the blessings before the <i>Shema</i>. However, in the<i>Kedushah</i> recited at the end of the service, a verse similar to Psalms 146:10 is also included.<br>The commentaries debate the definition of <i>Kedushah</i> and therefore, question which passage the Rambam requires to be omitted when one prays alone. The Rambam's statements in Chapter 9, Halachah 5, appear to define <i>Kedushah</i> as only the first of these three verses.</i><br>Similarly, we should not recite <i>Kedushah</i>, read the Torah with its blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the reading of the Torah as part of the prayer service. Reading from a Torah scroll for the purposes of study does not require a quorum.</i> before and after it,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 3.</i> or read the <i>haftorah</i> from the Prophets except in [a quorum of] ten.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Megillah</i> 23b) equates the reading of the haftarah with other "holy matters" that require a <i>minyan</i>.</i>
Similarly, one [person] should not recite the blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> uses the singular term, "blessing," as will be explained, the plural is more appropriate. The authoritative Oxford and Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also use the plural.</i> associated with the <i>Shema</i> while the others listen and answer "Amen" except [in the presence of a quorum of] ten. This is called <i>poreis al Shema</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This law is also based on the mishnah in <i>Megillah</i> 23b. Commenting on that mishnah, the Rambam defines פריסה as "presentation," i.e., the public reading of the blessings before the <i>Shema</i>. If people want to fulfill their obligation to recite the blessings associated with the <i>Shema</i> by answering "Amen" to the <i>chazan's</i> blessings instead of reciting those blessings themselves, a quorum of ten is required. See also Chapter 9, Halachah 1.<br>[Rabbenu Yonah supports this definition of פריסה based on the Targum Yonaton of I Samuel 9:13.]<br>Rashi, the Ra'avad, and others (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 69:1) define פריסה as "dividing in half." They explain the ceremony of <i>poreis al Shema</i> to refer to a situation where ten individuals who have already recited their prayers come together. One individual acts as a <i>chazan</i> and recites <i>Kaddish, Barchu</i>, and the first of the blessings before the <i>Shema</i>.<br>One only recites Kaddish - <i>Kaddish</i> is recited to mark different stages in the prayer service, after public study, and in memory of the departed.<br>Note also the brief remarks the Rambam makes concerning this prayer in his Order of Prayers for the Entire Year.</i><br>One only recites <i>Kaddish</i> with ten.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soferim</i> 10:7 also considers <i>Kaddish</i> as a "holy matter" which requires a <i>minyan</i>.</i> The priests do not bless the people<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "raise their hands," for the priestly blessing is recited with raised hands. See Leviticus 9:22 and Chapter 14, Halachah 3.</i> except [in the presence of a quorum of] ten. The priests [themselves] may be considered part of the quorum.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 15, Halachah 9.</i><br>[Ten are required] because<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">These prayers and rituals are considered "holy matters" which require a congregation (as mentioned in the following halachah) and...</i> every [group of] ten Jews is called a congregation as [implied by Numbers 14:27]: "How long [must I suffer] this evil congregation."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This verse records God's complaint about the spies sent to collect information about Eretz Yisrael (Numbers, Chapters 14 and 15). Twelve spies were sent.</i> They<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The spies whom God condemned for trying to persuade the Jews that it was impossible to conquer <i>Eretz Yisrael</i></i> were ten, for Joshua and Calev<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">though also sent on the mission</i> were not included [among them].<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">and, in fact, resisted these attempts, calling for the Jews to enter the land as God had commanded.</i>
Any holy matter<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the prayers and rituals mentioned in Halachot 4 and 5.</i> may only [be performed] in a congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a quorum of ten (see the previous halachah).</i> of Jews, as [Leviticus 22:32] states: "And I shall be sanctified among the children of Israel".<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 23b explains as follows: Leviticus 22:32 states, "And I shall be sanctified among the children of Israel." A גזרה שוה (an analogy based on the use of common words) is made between this verse and Numbers 16:21, "Separate yourselves from among this congregation" and a second גזרה שוה between the latter verse and the verse, Numbers 14:27, quoted in the previous halachah. Extending our use of the principle of גזרה שוה, we conclude that just as in regard to the spies, a congregation meant a minimum of ten people, the same applies in regards to all situations in which God is "sanctified."</i><br>Regarding all these matters, if they were begun with ten [people] and some leave<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the place of prayer</i> - even though they are not permitted to<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 4:4) states that Isaiah 1:28, "And those that forsake God will be consumed" applies to those that leave in the middle of communal prayer when less than ten men remain. If more than ten are left, although leaving is undesirable, there is no prohibition.</i> - the remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">of those left in the <i>minyan</i>. A minimum of six people is, nevertheless, required (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 55:2).</i> should conclude [the holy matter].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam writes that if a congregation began the first blessing before <i>Kri'at Shema</i> with ten people and then some left, leaving less than a <i>minyan</i>, the congregation may continue and recite the <i>Kedushah</i> in that blessing (see Chapter 7, Halachah 17). The <i>chazan</i> may also repeat the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> out loud including <i>Kedushah</i>, but he should not recite the <i>Kaddish</i> after <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, since it is not part of the "holy matter" started while the <i>minyan</i> was present.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 55:2-3) rules that, in such an instance, though the blessings of <i>Kri'at Shema</i> may be recited without deviating from the normal practice, the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> with <i>Kedushah</i> should not be recited. If the repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> was begun with ten, it may be completed including its <i>Kedushah</i>.<br>The Ramah (<i>ibid.</i>) adds that, in such a case, one even recites <i>Kaddish</i> after ובא לציון גואל since the <i>Kaddish</i> refers to <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.</i>
All [ten members of a congregation] and the leader of the congregation must be in one place.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is the basis for the particular laws which follow.</i><br>[The following rules apply when] a small courtyard opens up in its entirety into a large courtyard:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This situation is described by the Mishnah, <i>Eruvin</i> 92a. The laws that follow in this and the following halachah are discussed by the Talmud immediately thereafter.<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that the small courtyard has only three walls. The fourth side opens into the larger courtyard. The large courtyard has three full sides, with the fourth side consisting of two short walls on each side of the opening into the small one. The accompanying diagram is based on a diagram drawn by the Rambam (accompanying his Commentary on the Mishnah) to depict this situation.micha, drawing</i> If there are nine [people] in the large one and one in the small one,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">since the majority of the people are in the larger courtyard and the smaller courtyard opens up to the larger one in its entirety</i> they may be considered as a<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">single</i> group<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">located in the larger courtyard.</i> [to form a quorum of ten].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The same law would apply if four people were in the smaller courtyard.</i> If there are nine [people] in the small one and one in the large one,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the majority is in the smaller courtyard</i> they are not considered as a<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">single</i> group.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">located in the smaller courtyard because the walls of the larger courtyard separate it from the smaller one.<br>If a congregation is in the large one, but the leader of the congregation is in the small one, - he, like the individual(s) mentioned above, is considered part of the group in the larger courtyard. Hence,</i><br>If a congregation is in the large one, but the leader of the congregation is in the small one, they<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">the congregation, can</i> fulfill their obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">by listening to his prayers and answering "Amen" as stated in Halachah 9.</i> If the congregation is in the small one, but the leader of the congregation is in the large one,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">he, like the individual(s) mentioned above, is not considered part of the group in the smaller courtyard. Hence,</i> they do not fulfill their obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">by listening to his prayers</i> since he is separate from them and not with them in one place.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">since he is separate from them and not with them in one place.</i><br> [The motivating principle<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the reason why individuals in the smaller courtyard can be considered as part of a group located in the larger courtyard, but individuals in the larger courtyard cannot be considered as part of a group located in the smaller courtyard,</i> is] that the walls on each side of the large courtyard separate it from the small one. [However], the smaller one is not separated from the large one, but rather, is considered as its corner.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">It follows that if the smaller courtyard has walls on the side that opens to the larger courtyard, the above laws do not apply. By the same token, a person(s) standing in the courtyard of a synagogue or in an adjoining room cannot be considered as part of a <i>minyan</i>.<br>Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 55:14) qualifies this restriction and states that if the person in the outer room can see - and be seen by - the people in the main room, he may be counted together with them. Even if he is merely peering through a window, he is considered as part of that group.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 55:20 also mentions that even when the people in adjoining rooms cannot see each other, those in the outer room may respond to <i>Kaddish</i> and <i>Kedushah</i>. The rabbis differed as to whether a person praying in a separate room next to the room where a <i>minyan</i> is praying may be considered to have participated in communal prayer or not (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 55:58).</i>
Similarly, if there were feces in the larger [courtyard], it is forbidden to pray<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as stated in Chapter 4, Halachot 8-9,</i> or recite the <i>Shema</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as stated in <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:6-15, in the larger courtyard or</i> [even] in the smaller one.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for it is considered as part of the larger courtyard. Thus, one is considered as praying in a place which contains feces.</i> If there were feces in the smaller one,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The recitation of any holy matter is forbidden in the smaller courtyard. Nevertheless, because the larger courtyard is considered a separate place</i> it is permissible to pray and recite the <i>Shema</i> in the larger one<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">even if the feces are visible; see <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:10.</i> provided there is not a foul odor<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">in which case it would be forbidden. See <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:10.</i> since [the larger courtyard] is set apart<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">by its walls</i> from [the feces].
The leader of the congregation can fulfill the obligation [of prayer] on behalf of the congregation.<br>What is implied? When he<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the leader of the congregation, the <i>chazan</i></i> prays<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">recites the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> (and according to the Rambam - see Halachah 5 - the blessings before the <i>Shema</i>) aloud</i> and they listen<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">while standing in the posture for prayer</i> and respond "Amen" after each and every blessing, it is considered as if they prayed [themselves].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If one desires to fulfill his obligation with the <i>chazan's</i> prayer, he should answer "Amen," but he should not answer <i>Baruch Hu, uvaruch shemo</i> after the mention of God's name.</i><br>To whom does this apply? To one who does not know how to pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the opinion of the Sages in the difference of opinion between them and Rabban Gamliel (<i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 33b, 34b) as explained in Halachah 2.</i> However, one who does know how to pray, only fulfills his obligation by praying himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the essence of prayer is the request for Divine Mercy, it is fitting that each person request for himself (<i>Tosafot Yom Tov, Rosh HaShanah</i>).<br>See also the opening halachot of Chapter 9 which deal with the practical application of the concepts mentioned in this halachah.</i>
When does the above apply? Throughout the entire year with the exception of Rosh HaShanah, and Yom Kippur of the Jubilee year.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">specifically, in contrast to the yearly Yom Kippur services.<br>Even on Sabbaths and festivals when the prayers differ from those recited every day, a person who knows how to pray is expected to pray alone. However, note one of the Rambam's responsa which states that, on Sabbaths and festivals, rather than begin with silent prayer, the <i>chazan</i> should begin by reciting the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> out loud together with <i>Kedushah</i>. Those who do not know how to pray should listen to his prayers with the intention of fulfilling their obligation. Those who know how to pray should recite their prayers, word by word, together with the <i>chazan</i>.</i><br>On these two<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though we celebrate Rosh HaShanah as two days by Rabbinic decree, the Torah itself requires only one day to be celebrated.</i> days,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">All the Rabbis agree that although the halachah follows the Sages throughout the entire year, on these two days, an exception is made and Rabban Gamliel's opinion prevails.</i> the leader of the congregation can fulfill the obligation [of prayer]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In particular, this refers only to the musaf prayer.</i> on behalf of those who know [how to pray] just as he can fulfill the obligation [of prayer] on behalf of those who do not know [how to pray]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This leniency is granted</i> because [the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> recited on these days contains] long blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 6, and in greater detail, <i>Hilchot Shofar</i> 3:8-9. There, the Rambam explains that, on these days, one is required to recite three extra blessings:<i>Malchuyut</i> - which expresses the theme of God's sovereignty,<i>Zichronot</i> - which describes God's remembrance of His love for the Jews, and<i>Shofrot</i> - which praises the shofar as a means of manifesting the previous two concepts.<br>Each of these three blessings includes the recitation of ten verses that express these themes: four from the Torah, three from the prophets, and three from Psalms.<br>In Chapter 2, Halachah 8, the Rambam writes that on Yom Kippur of the Jubilee year, one recites exactly the same blessings as on Rosh HaShanah.</i> and most people do not know them [to the extent] that they can have the same intention as the leader of the congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the Rambam does not appear to interpret "intention" as referring to the <i>chazan's</i> concentration and spiritual awareness, but simply, to his familiarity with the text of the blessings.</i><br>Therefore, on these two days, even a person who knows [how to pray] is granted permission to rely on the prayers of the leader of the congregation to fulfill his obligation [of prayer] if he so desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Though in principle, this ruling is accepted by all authorities, in practice, Ashkenazic Rabbis have advised each person to pray individually even on Rosh HaShanah since, at present, the use of <i>machzorim</i> which include a full text of the blessings is widespread. Furthermore, the inclusion of <i>piyyutim</i> (liturgical poems) in the <i>chazan's</i> repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> makes it very difficult to fulfill one's obligation by listening to his prayer and reciting "Amen." See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 591:1-4.</i>
Only a person of great stature within the community in both wisdom<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Torah knowledge</i> and deed<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">performance of the mitzvot; alternatively, deeds of kindness, so that his appointment will be appreciated by all members of the community.</i> should be appointed<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> mentions that this applies only to the appointment of a permanent <i>chazan</i> and does not apply to someone who leads the congregation in prayer on occasion. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 53:6) mentions this in regard to a <i>chazan</i> having a full beard.</i> as the leader of the congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ta'anit</i> 16a interprets Jeremiah 12:8: "She raises his voice against Me, therefore, I (God) have hated her" as a reference to a <i>chazan</i> who is unfit for this responsibility.<br>Most of the commentaries point to the mishnah (<i>Ta'anit</i> 2:2) as the source for the Rambam's statements. Though that mishnah deals with the appointment of the leader of communal prayer for a public fast, there is no reason to assume that the same principles should not apply to the appointment of a <i>chazan</i> at other times. In the Talmud (e.g., <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 32a), we find many examples of leading Sages serving as <i>chazanim</i>.</i> If he is an older man,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Though the Hebrew, זקן, is often used to connote a wise man (See <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 6:1), this translation has been chosen based on the later statements of this halachah.</i> it is very praiseworthy. An effort should be made<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. it is desirable, but not an absolute requirement.</i> to appoint as the leader of the congregation, someone who has a pleasant voice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">so that listening to his prayers will be enjoyable.<br>Throughout the generations, in their responsa, our Rabbis have chided the people for putting a pleasant voice at the top of their priorities for a <i>chazan</i>. [Note the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 53:5) who states that it is preferable to appoint a boy of thirteen who understands his prayers as <i>chazan</i> rather than an older man with a pleasant voice who does not know what he is saying. See also <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>, 53:11).]<br>In particular, this applies regarding the High Holiday services. These days of sincere prayer should not be turned into concerts of ritual music. Instead, attempts should be made to find a <i>chazan</i> who can truly serve as a spiritual leader. If he also has a pleasant voice, it is obviously more desirable.</i> and is familiar with reading [Biblical verses].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">so that he will not err in his recitation of the prayers.</i><br>A person who does not have a full beard<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Rabbis have explained that this simply refers to a person of intermediate age. Therefore, in one of his responsa, the Rambam states that a person who is physically incapable of growing a beard may serve as <i>chazan</i> and the requirement of a beard is only mentioned as a sign of "a full state of knowledge which one [usually] reaches at the age when one's beard begins to grow." See also <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 53:8).<br>However, a beard is also considered a sign of commitment to Jewish practice. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 12:7) associates shaving with the practices of gentile priests. Many Rabbis have strongly encouraged growing a beard even when there is no question of violating the prohibition against shaving. (See <i>Sefer Mitzvot Gadol</i> 7, <i>Shibolei Leket</i> Vol. II, 41.)<br>However, in communities where removing one's facial hair in a permitted manner is an accepted practice, the lack of a beard is not considered as a deterrent against a person serving as a <i>chazan</i>.</i> should not be appointed as the leader of the congregation even if he be a wise man of great stature, as a gesture of respect to the congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:6) states that this only refers to the appointment of a permanent <i>chazan</i>. However, Rabbenu Nissim interprets the Rambam's words as meaning that even on occasion, a youth should not serve as a <i>chazan</i>. See also Chapter 15, Halachah 4.</i> However, he may recite the <i>Shema</i> publicly<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>haporeis al Shema</i> as described in Halachah 5. See <i>Megillah</i> 24a.</i> after he has reached the age of thirteen and manifested signs of physical maturity.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">until then, he is a minor and incapable of fulfilling the obligation of prayer on behalf of an adult.</i>
Similarly, the inarticulate who pronounce an <i>alef</i> as an <i>ayin</i> or an <i>ayin</i> as an <i>alef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 24b relates that a <i>chazan</i> should not be appointed from Beit Shean or Haifa, since they mispronounce these letters. Rashi explains that such mispronunciations will cause blessings to be read as curses. For example, if the word, יאר, the first word of the second priestly blessing, פניו אליך ‘ה יאר, is read with an ע instead of an א, it becomes a curse.</i> or one who cannot articulate the letters in the proper manner should not be appointed as the leader of a congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For many Jews today, this halachah is rather difficult to observe since we do not differentiate between the pronunciation of an <i>alef</i> and an <i>ayin</i> or a <i>sin</i> and a <i>samech</i>. The Radbaz and the <i>Pri Chadash</i> write that if these pronunciation errors are commonplace, even someone who pronounces a <i>chet</i> as a <i>hay</i> or a <i>shin</i> as a <i>sin</i> may serve as a <i>chazan</i>. See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 53:16, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 53:37-38.<br>The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> also cites Chapter 15, Halachah 1, from which it appears that a person who stutters, lisps, or has another speech defect which prevents him from pronouncing the letters properly should not serve as a <i>chazan</i>.<br>A teacher may appoint one of his students to lead the prayers in his presence. - even if the student lacks the above qualities (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Though the previous halachah stated that the leader of the congregation should be the man of the greatest stature in the community, the mishnah (<i>Megillah</i>, <i>ibid.</i>) and similarly, several sources throughout the Talmud (See <i>Berachot</i> 33b) indicate that a teacher could appoint a student to serve as a <i>chazan</i> instead of doing so himself.<br>The <i>Or Sameach</i> explains that this refers to a student who does not articulate the letters properly. Though such a person should not ordinarily serve as a <i>chazan</i>, if he is appointed by his teacher he may. Thus, we find that Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi appointed Rabbi Chiya as a <i>chazan</i> (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 85b) even though he would pronounce a <i>chet</i> as a <i>hay</i> (<i>Megillah</i> 24b).</i><br>A teacher may appoint one of his students to lead the prayers in his presence. A blind person may recite the <i>Shema</i> publicly<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">even though the first blessing before the <i>Shema</i> thanks God for creating light, a blind person also benefits from light (<i>Megillah</i> <i>ibid.</i>).</i> and serve as the leader of a congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps the Rambam thought it necessary to clarify this point because there was a difference of opinion among the Sages as to whether a blind person is obligated in all the mitzvot or not. The Sages concluded that he is obligated. Hence, he can also fulfill the obligations of others as the leader of the congregation.</i> A person whose shoulders are uncovered<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">though he may recite the <i>Shema</i> publicly - may not serve as the leader of the congregation until he is covered by a cloak. - <i>Soferim</i> 14:15 understands this as a gesture of respect for the congregation. See also <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 17b which states that when God taught Moses the thirteen attributes of mercy, "He robed himself as a <i>chazan</i>."<br>This does not necessarily refer to wearing a <i>tallit gadol</i>, but rather to a cloak or jacket that covers his shoulders and arms (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 53:13). However, in many communities, it is customary for a <i>chazan</i> to wear a <i>tallit</i> as a token of respect for the community.</i> - though he may recite the <i>Shema</i> publicly - may not serve as the leader of the congregation until he is covered by a cloak.

Chapter 9

The order of prayer is as follows:<br>In the morning, [while] all the people are sitting, the leader of the congregation descends before the ark in the midst of the people and recites the <i>Kaddish</i>. Everyone responds with all their strength: <i>Amen. Yehei shemeih rabba mevarach le'alam ul'almei almaya</i>. They answer "Amen" at the end of the <i>Kaddish</i>.<br>Afterwards, [the <i>chazan</i>] declares: <i>Barchu et Ado-nai hamevorach</i>, and they answer: <i>Baruch Ado-nai hamevorach le'olam va'ed</i>. He then begins by reciting the <i>Shema</i> and its blessings out loud. They answer "Amen" after each blessing.<br>A person who knows how to recite the blessings and read [the <i>Shema</i>] with him should read [the blessings by himself] until he recites the blessing <i>ga'al Yisrael</i>.
Immediately [afterwards], all stand and pray in a hushed tone. A person who does not know how to pray should stand in silence while the leader of the congregation prays in a hushed tone together with the others. Whoever concludes his prayers with the congregation should take three steps back and stand [still] in the place he reached when he stepped backwards.
After the leader of the congregation takes three steps backwards and stands still, he begins and prays in a loud voice from the beginning of the blessings, in order to fulfill the obligation on behalf of those who did not pray.<br>Everyone - both those who did not fulfill their obligation [to pray] and those who fulfilled their obligation - stands, listens, and recites "Amen" after each and every blessing.
[The leader of the congregation] recites <i>Kedushah</i> in the third blessing.<br>When the leader of the congregation reaches <i>Kedushah</i>, each individual may return to the place where he originally stood in prayer.<br>When the leader of the congregation reaches <i>Modim</i> and bows, everyone should also bow - but not bow exceedingly - and say:<br>We give thanks to You, God, our Lord, and Lord of all flesh, our Creator and the Creator of all existence. [We offer] blessings and thanks to Your great and holy name, for You have granted us life and sustained us. So may You continue to grant us life and sustain us, and gather our exiles into the courtyards of Your Sanctuary [so that we may] keep Your laws, serve You in truth, and fulfill Your will with a perfect heart, for we thankfully acknowledge You.<br>Whoever says <i>Modim, modim</i> should be silenced.
After the leader of the congregation concludes the entire <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he and the entire congregation should sit, fall on their faces, and lean over slightly, reciting supplicatory prayers while fallen on their faces.<br>Then, he and the entire congregation should sit, lift their heads, and recite a small amount of supplicatory prayer out loud while seated. Afterwards, the leader of the congregation alone should stand and recite <i>Kaddish</i> a second time. The congregation should answer as they did previously.<br>Afterwards, while he is standing, he recites <i>V'hu rachum...</i> and <i>Tehillah [l'David]...</i> The congregation remains seated and recites together with him. Afterwards, he recites [the following verses]: <i>U'va l'Tzion go'el..., Va'ani zot..., V'attah kadosh...</i> and <i>V'kara zeh el zeh, v'amar: Kadosh...</i> and completes the <i>Kedushah</i>.<br>They respond: <i>Kadosh, Kadosh</i> three times. He then recites the <i>Kedushah</i> in an Aramaic translation. Afterwards, he recites [the verse] <i>Vatisa'eni ruach</i> and reads it in Aramaic, and then recites [the verse], <i>Ado-nai yimloch le'olam va'ed</i> and reads it in Aramaic. [The Aramaic translations are intended] in order that the [common] people should understand.
These verses [which are recited] before <i>Kedushah</i> and afterwards, together with their Aramaic translations, are referred to as "the order of <i>Kedushah</i>."<br>Afterwards, he recites supplicatory prayers and verses of mercy. He then recites the <i>Kaddish</i>. The people respond as is customary, and depart.
One who says in his supplicatory prayers: "May He who showed mercy on a bird's nest prohibiting the taking of the mother together with the chicks, or the slaughter of an animal and its calf on the same day, also show mercy on us," or [makes other] similar statements should be silenced, because these mitzvot are God's decrees and not [expressions] of mercy. Were they [expressions] of mercy, He would not permit us to slaughter at all.<br>Also, a person should not be profuse in his mention of adjectives describing God, and say: "The great, mighty, awesome, powerful, courageous, and strong God," for it is impossible for man to express the totality of His praises. Instead, one should mention [only] the praises that were mentioned by Moses, of blessed memory.
In the Minchah service, the leader of the congregation recites <i>Ashrei yoshvei veitecha...</i>, <i>Tehillah l'David...</i>. He and the congregation recite this while seated. [Then,] the leader of the congregation stands and recites <i>Kaddish</i>. They stand and respond in their normal manner. [Afterwards,] they all pray in a hushed tone.<br>Afterwards, the leader of the congregation prays out loud, as in the morning service, until he concludes the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. Then, both he and the congregation fall on their faces, recite supplicatory prayers, lift their heads, and recite a few supplicatory prayers while seated, as in the morning service.<br>[The leader of the congregation] rises and recites <i>Kaddish</i>. The congregation responds in the normal manner and they depart to their affairs.
In the evening, all the people sit, and [the leader of the congregation] stands and recites: <i>V'hu rachum...</i>. [He announces:] <i>Barchu et Ado-nai hamevorach</i>, and they answer: <i>Baruch Ado-nai hamevorach le'olam va'ed</i>. He then begins by reciting the <i>Shema</i> and its blessings out loud and recites <i>Kaddish</i>. [Afterwards,] they all stand and pray in a hushed tone.<br>When they conclude, [the leader of the congregation] recites <i>Kaddish</i> and they depart. He does not repeat the evening <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> out loud, since the evening service is not obligatory. Therefore, he should not recite blessings in vain, for there is no one who is obligated [to recite these blessings] whose obligation he would fulfill [by his recitation].
On the night of the Sabbath, after praying together with the congregation in a hushed tone, the leader of the congregation prays out loud. However, he does not recite all seven blessings, but rather one blessing that includes all seven. He states:<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord and Lord of our fathers, the Lord of Abraham, the Lord of Isaac, and the Lord of Jacob, the great, mighty, and awesome God, the supreme God, who, in His mercies, creates heaven and earth.<br>He shielded our ancestors with His word. He resurrects the dead with His statements - the holy God, like whom there is none. He causes His people to rest on His holy Sabbath, for to them did He desire to grant rest. We will serve Him with awe and fear and give thanks to His name every day, continually, according to the blessings [appropriate for that day]. God who is worthy of thanks, Lord of peace, who sanctifies the Sabbath and blesses the seventh day and brings rest with holiness to a people satiated with delight in commemoration of the work of creation.<br>Our Lord, and Lord of our fathers, desire our rest.... Blessed are You, God, who sanctifies the Sabbath.<br>He recites <i>Kaddish</i> and the people depart.
Why did the Sages institute this [practice]? Because the majority of people come to recite the evening service on Friday night. It is possible that someone will come late, remain alone in the synagogue, and thus be endangered. Accordingly, the leader of the congregation repeats his prayers in order that the entire congregation will remain, [allowing] the one who came late to conclude his prayers and leave together with them.
Therefore, when a festival, Rosh Chodesh or Yom Kippur coincides with the Sabbath, the leader of the congregation who recites the evening service does not mention the unique aspect of the day in this blessing. Instead, he concludes, "[Blessed are You, God,] who sanctifies the Sabbath," [mentioning] only [the Sabbath], because [on the other] days, there is no obligation [to recite] this blessing.
On the Sabbaths and the festivals, after the leader of the congregation completes the recitation of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> of the morning service out loud, he recites <i>Kaddish</i> and afterwards, <i>Tehillah l'David</i>. He recites <i>Kaddish</i> [again] and [the congregation] recites <i>Musaf</i> in a hushed tone. Afterwards, he recites the <i>Musaf</i> <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> out loud, in the same manner as the morning service. He recites <i>Kaddish</i> after <i>Musaf</i> and the people depart.<br>We do not recite <i>Kedushah</i> and supplicatory prayers after the morning service. Instead, we recite them before the <i>Minchah</i> service. What is implied? We recite <i>Tehillah l'David</i>, the order of the day, and supplicatory prayers. [The leader of the congregation] recites <i>Kaddish</i> and the <i>Minchah</i> service is recited [in a hushed tone.] Afterwards, [the leader of the congregation] repeats the service out loud, and then recites <i>Kaddish</i>.
On Rosh Chodesh and <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, one recites the order of <i>kedushah</i> before the <i>Musaf</i> prayer. On the night following the Sabbath, the order of the day is also recited after the evening service. [Then] one recites <i>Kaddish</i> and afterwards, <i>Havdalah</i>.

Chapter 10

A person who prayed without concentrating [on his prayers] must pray a second time with concentration. However, if he had concentrated during the first blessing, nothing more is necessary.<br>A person who errs in the recitation of the first three blessings [of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>] must return to the beginning [of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>]. Should one err in the recitation of the final three blessings, one should return to [the blessing, <i>R'tzey</i>]. If one errs in the midst of [one of] the intermediate blessings, one should return to the beginning of that blessing and [then] conclude one's prayers in the [proper] order.<br>Should the leader of the congregation err when he is praying out loud, he should [correct himself] based on these principles.
However, if the leader of the congregation errs while he is praying in a hushed tone, I maintain that he does not repeat his prayers a second time, because of the difficulty it will cause the congregation. Rather, he relies on the prayer which he will recite out loud.<br>The above applies when he does not err in the first three blessings. If he errs in their [recitation], he always repeats [his prayers] in the same manner as any [other] individual.
Should the leader of a congregation err [in his repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>], become confused and not know where to begin [again] - if he waits for a prolonged period, another person should replace him.<br>If he errs in the recitation of the blessing [that curses] the heretics, we do not wait for him [to correct himself]; rather, another person should replace him. This applies only when he did not begin this [blessing]. However, if he did begin [the blessing], we wait for him.<br>The second person should not refuse at this time.
From which point should [the substitute for the leader of the congregation] begin? If the first one erred in one of the intermediate blessings, [the substitute] should begin from the beginning of the blessing in which he erred. However, if he erred in one of the first three blessings, the [substitute] starts from the beginning [of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. If he erred in one of the final [three] blessings, the substitute should begin from [the blessing, <i>R'tzey</i>].
A person who says, "I will not lead the congregation in prayer because [I am wearing] colored clothes," should not lead the congregation in that prayer service even when wearing white clothes. If he said, "I will not lead the congregation because I am wearing sandals," he should not lead the congregation even when barefoot.
A person who is in doubt whether he prayed or not should not repeat his prayers, unless he recites the second prayer with the intention that it is a voluntary prayer, since an individual may recite voluntary prayers throughout the entire day.<br>A person who remembers that he has already prayed while he is in the midst of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> should cease [praying] immediately, even if he is in the midst of a blessing. [However,] if he was reciting the evening service, he need not cease [praying], for even at the outset, he did not begin that prayer service with the thought that it was an obligation.
A person who erred and recited a weekday prayer on the Sabbath does not fulfill his obligation. If he recalled while he was in the midst of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he should conclude the blessing which he has begun and [continue] reciting the Sabbath prayers.<br>When does the above apply? In the evening, morning, and <i>Minchah</i> services. However, in the <i>Musaf</i> service, one should cease [praying] even in the midst of a blessing. Similarly, if one completed a weekday <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> with the intention that it be one's <i>Musaf</i> prayers, one must recite <i>Musaf</i> again. This applies on the Sabbath, on a festival, and on Rosh Chodesh.
[When] one errs during the rainy season and does not recite either <i>morid hageshem</i> or <i>morid hatal</i>, he must return to the beginning of the prayers. However, if he mentions dew, he need not repeat [his prayers].<br>Should one err in the summer and recite <i>morid hageshem</i>, he must return to the beginning of the prayers. However, if he omits mention of dew [in the summer], he need not repeat his prayers, for dew is never held back, nor is there a need to request it.
