[The following rules apply when] one forgets to request rain in the blessing for material prosperity: If he remembers before [the blessing,] <i>shome'a tefilah</i>, he should request rain in [that blessing]. If he [recalls] after reciting the blessing, <i>shome'a tefilah</i>, he should return to the blessing for material prosperity. If he does not recall until after he completes the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he must return to the beginning of the prayer and pray a second time.
[The following rules apply when] a person errs and fails to mention <i>Ya'aleh v'yavo</i>: If he remembers before he has concluded the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, he should return to [the blessing, <i>R'tzey</i>], and recite it. If he remembers after he has concluded his prayers, he must repeat the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> from the beginning. If he is accustomed to recite supplicatory prayers after <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> and remembers after he has concluded his prayers, but before he has lifted up his feet [to step backwards after prayer], he should return to [the blessing, <i>R'tzey</i>].
When does the above apply? On <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i> or in the morning or <i>Minchah</i> services of Rosh Chodesh. However, in the evening service of Rosh Chodesh, if one failed to mention it one need not repeat his prayers.
In every case in which an individual is required to repeat his prayers [because of an error], the leader of the congregation is also required to repeat his prayers if he made a similar mistake while praying out loud, with the exception of the morning service of Rosh Chodesh.<br>[In this instance,] if the leader of the congregation failed to mention <i>Ya'aleh v'yavo</i> before completing his prayers, he is not required to repeat his prayers because of the difficulty it would cause the congregation. The <i>Musaf</i> service is still to be recited and Rosh Chodesh will be mentioned there.
If during the ten days between Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur, one erred and concluded the third blessing, <i>Ha'El hakadosh</i>, he should return to the beginning of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.<br>If he erred and concluded the eleventh blessing, <i>Melech ohev tzedakah umishpat</i>, he should return to the beginning of the blessing and conclude <i>Hamelech hamishpat</i>, and continue reciting his prayers in order. If he did not remember until he concluded his prayers, he must recite [his prayers again] from the beginning.<br>[These laws apply] to both an individual and to the leader of the congregation.
If a person erred and did not mention <i>Havdalah</i> in the blessing, <i>chonen hada'at</i>, he should conclude his prayers, without returning [to correct himself]. Similarly, one who did not mention <i>Al hanisim</i> on Chanukah or Purim, or <i>Anenu</i> in the prayers on a fast day, need not repeat his prayers. [These laws apply] to both an individual and the leader of a congregation.<br>If one remembers before lifting his feet [to step backwards after prayer], he should say: "Answer us because You are the one who hears prayer, redeems, and rescues in all times of difficulty and distress. May the words of my mouth...."
[A person who] forgot to recite the afternoon service on the Sabbath eve should recite the Sabbath evening service twice. [The same law applies] on a festival.<br>[A person who] forgot to recite the afternoon service on the Sabbath or a festival should recite the weekday evening service twice after their conclusion.<br>He should recite <i>Havdalah</i> in the first of these prayers and not in the second. However, if he recited <i>Havdalah</i> in both prayers or omitted it in both prayers, he fulfills his obligation. Nevertheless, if he did not recite <i>Havdalah</i> in his first <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, but mentioned it in his second, he [must] return and recite a third <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, since his first prayers were not acceptable because they were recited before the evening service.<br>Whoever recites two prayers [in succession] - even the morning service and the <i>Musaf</i> service - should not recite them one immediately after the other. Rather, he should wait between prayers, so that his mind will be settled.
It is forbidden for a person who is praying with a congregation to pray before the congregation.<br>[The following rules apply when] a person enters a synagogue and finds the congregation praying in a hushed tone: If he could begin and complete his prayers before the leader of the congregation reached <i>Kedushah</i>, he should recite the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. If not, he should wait until the leader of the congregation begins reciting the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> out loud, and pray together with him word for word until the leader of the congregation reaches <i>Kedushah</i>. He should respond to <i>Kedushah</i> with the rest of the congregation and then recite the remainder of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> alone.<br>One who began reciting the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> before the leader of the congregation, [but was unable to conclude his prayers before] the leader of the congregation reached <i>Kedushah</i>, should not interrupt his prayers [to] respond to <i>Kedushah</i> with [the congregation]. Similarly, one should not respond <i>Amen, yehei shemeih rabba mevarach...</i> while in the midst of <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. Needless to say, [this applies regarding responding "Amen"] to other blessings.

Chapter 11

Wherever ten Jews live,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since communal prayer is impossible without a quorum of ten, this is the minimum number of people that must live in a community before constructing a synagogue becomes obligatory.</i> it is necessary to establish a place for them to congregate for prayer at the time of each prayer service.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Hilchot De'ot</i> 4:23, which states that a Torah sage is not allowed to live in a city which lacks any of ten community resources, one of which is a synagogue.</i><br>This place is called a <i>Beit K'nesset</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "a house of congregation," generally, translated as "synagogue."</i> The inhabitants of a city can compel each other<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Choshen Mishpat</i> 163:1) states that even if the majority of the inhabitants do not desire the construction of a synagogue, the minority have the right to compel them to build it.</i> to construct<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., participate both financially and in the actual construction work, if necessary,</i> a synagogue and to purchase scrolls containing the Torah, the Prophets, and the Sacred Writings.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">so that these texts will be available for Torah study (<i>Hilchot Sh'chenim</i> 6:1). At present, when a greater number of Torah texts are available, the congregation is obligated to buy the Torah texts needed by the congregation - both adults and minors - for study (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 150:1).</i>
When a synagogue is built, it should be built only at the highest point of the city [as implied by Proverbs 1:21]: "She cries at the head of the public places."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These statements are quoted from the <i>Tosefta, Megillah</i> 3:14. The commentaries question how this verse serves as a prooftext for the law which is stated.</i> It should be built [so that] its height exceeds [that of] all the other buildings in the city [as implied by Ezra 9:9]: "to lift up the house of our God."<br>The<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 11a states: "Any city whose roofs are higher than the synagogue will ultimately be destroyed."<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> maintain that this applies only to buildings with flat roofs. However, if the roofs are slanted and therefore, will not be used by people, the height of the other buildings may exceed that of the synagogue. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 150:2) states that apartments or offices in other buildings should also not be higher than the synagogue's roof.<br>The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 150:5 notes that many communities are not stringent in the observance of this law and quotes sources which explain that since buildings owned by gentiles are often taller than the synagogue, the synagogue is not likely to be the tallest building in the city in any case. Therefore, other Jewish houses may also be built higher than the synagogue. His own opinion, however, is not to seek leniency in this regard.</i> entrance to the synagogue should open only on the east [as implied by Numbers 3:38]: "...And those who camped before<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The word "before" is understood to mean "at the entrance to."</i> the sanctuary on the<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This law is quoted from the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>). The <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 150:5) explain that this law applied only in Babylon, where their synagogues faced west. Thus, the doorway on the eastern side would be opposite the ark. However, since the Rambam mentions the possibility of the <i>heichal</i> being built on different walls, but mentions only one position for the entrance, it appears that he requires the entrance to be placed at the east at all times.</i> east."<br>In [the synagogue], a <i>heichal</i>, where the Torah scroll is placed, should be constructed.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a fixed structure like the ark (<i>Aron HaKodesh</i>) which is found in contemporary synagogues. In addition, as explained in the following halachah, the custom was to have a <i>tevah</i>, a smaller ark, positioned next to the place where the <i>chazan</i> would stand.</i> The <i>heichal</i> should be constructed in the direction to which the people pray in that city,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 5, Halachah 3, one should always pray facing the direction of the Temple. Thus, if the synagogue is located to the west of Jerusalem, the <i>heichal</i> should be built into the eastern wall.</i> so that they will face the <i>heichal</i> when they stand to pray.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It is apparent from the Rambam's statements that, if for some reason, the <i>heichal</i> of a synagogue was not positioned in the direction of Jerusalem, one should pray facing Jerusalem and not facing the <i>heichal</i>.</i>
A platform is placed in the center of the hall,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">both lengthwise and widthwise. (See the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Sukkah</i> 5:1.)</i> so that the one who reads the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as described in the following chapter</i> or one who gives a sermon<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Ta'anit</i> 2:1) explains that on fast days, one of the elders of the congregation would speak and call the people to repent. Similarly, the Talmud mentions that some of the leading Sages would visit distant communities and address the people in the synagogue.</i> can stand on it, so that all the others will hear him.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">can stand on it, so that all the others will hear him.</i><br> When one positions the <i>tevah</i> which contains the Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the <i>heichal</i> whose position is fixed, the<i>tevah</i> is a movable ark, in which the Torah scrolls used for the public reading of the Torah were kept. (See the responsa of the Radbaz, Vol. II, 157.)</i> one should position it in the center of the hall,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">widthwise</i> in the direction of the <i>heichal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., close to the wall on which the <i>heichal</i> is constructed</i> and facing the people.
How do the people sit in the synagogue? The elders sit facing the people with their backs toward the <i>heichal</i>. All the people sit row after row, each row facing the back of the row before it. Thus, all the people face the sanctuary,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the <i>heichal</i></i> the elders, and the <i>tevah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">which should all be positioned in the direction of Jerusalem<br>When the leader of the congregation stands to pray, he stands on the ground - praying from a low place, as implied by Psalms 130:1: "From the depths, I called out to You, O God" (<i>Berachot</i> 10b).</i><br>When the leader of the congregation stands to pray, he stands on the ground, before the <i>tevah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the expression, 18ינפלáדרוי הביתה (literally, "descend before the ark") is often used to refer to leading communal prayer, because the <i>chazan</i> would pray "on the ground, before the <i>tevah</i>."</i> facing the sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the <i>heichal</i></i> like the others.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., facing the same direction as the other congregants.</i>
Synagogues and houses of study should be treated with respect.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishneh Berurah</i> (151:1) notes that <i>Megillah</i> 29a quotes Ezekiel 11:16: "I have been a sanctuary in microcosm to them in the countries where they have come" to describe the synagogues in the Diaspora. This association allows one to infer that the obligation to honor a synagogue is related to the mitzvah (<i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, Positive Commandment 21) to revere the Temple.<br>The <i>S'dei Chemed</i> cites <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 65) which mentions the prohibition against "destroying the houses where God is worshiped," indicating that the obligation to honor the synagogues has its source in a Torah commandment. (Note the commentary on Halachah 12.)</i> They should be swept clean<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Chassidim</i> 128 relates tht Rabbi Ya'akov bar Yakar, Rashi's teacher, would polish the floor before the ark with his beard.</i> and mopped.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Their synagogues often had dirt floors. Thus, mopping them would also keep the dust from rising (<i>Megillah</i> 28b).</i><br>All the Jews in Spain, the west,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., North Africa,</i> Babylonia,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The translation is based on <i>Targum Onkelos</i> to Genesis 19:1.</i> and <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The term ארץ הצבי is applied to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, based on Jeremiah 3:19: "I will give you a delightful land (ארץ צבי), the finest heritage of a host of nations." (See also Daniel 8:9, <i>Gittin</i> 57a.)</i> are accustomed to light lamps in the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam appears to imply that in addition to lighting lamps at night, when their light would be necessary, lamps were lit during the day as a mark of respect.</i> and spread mats over the floor to sit on. In European communities,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "in the cities of Edom (i.e., Rome)."</i> they sit on chairs.
No lightheadedness - i.e., jests, frivolity, and idle conversation<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">cheapen the respect and awe one has for the synagogue. Rashi also includes in the category of קלות ראש all the other activities the Rambam mentions in this halachah because they all cause us to view the synagogue as a place where mundane affairs can be carried out, and thus, minimize our appreciation of its holiness.</i> - should be seen in a synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sefer Mitzvot Gadol</i> states that the sin of frivolity in the synagogues causes them to be sold to gentiles and transformed into houses of idol worship.<br>The <i>Zohar</i> (<i>Parashat Vayakhel</i>) severely condemns the sin of idle conversation in a synagogue, because God's presence is manifest there, and, therefore, any activity of this sort indicates a lack of reverence for Him.</i> We may not eat or drink inside [a synagogue],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 101a relates that wayfarers would eat meals in the synagogue. However, <i>Tosafot, Megillah</i> 28a, explains that synagogues would have a side room that was used as a guest house. However, eating and drinking would not be permitted in the room used for prayer.</i> nor use [a synagogue] for our benefit,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Berachot</i> 8:6. Rashi (<i>Megillah</i> 28b) renders ניאותין as "adorn ourselves." According to his interpretation, the synagogue should not be a place where people come to show off their clothes and jewelry.</i> nor stroll inside one.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is forbidden to go to a synagogue for the purpose of walking around to release tension (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>On a sunny [day], one should not enter [a synagogue to seek shade] from the sun, and on a rainy [day], [one should not enter a synagogue to seek shelter] from the rain.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 28b relates that Ravina and Rav Ada bar Matanah were discussing a Halachic problem with Ravva outside. When it began raining, they entered a synagogue to continue their discussion. They explained, "We did not enter the synagogue because of the rain, but because the comprehension of the passage requires a relaxed state of mind."</i> [However,] the sages and their students<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 151:2 states that this license is granted only to scholars who spend the majority of their time in the house of study. Others, however, are not granted such privileges.</i> are permitted to eat and drink in a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 28b states that a house of study is called "the rabbis' house," implying that they can do anything they would do in their own homes in the house of study. Since the holiness of a house of study exceeds that of a synagogue (see Halachah 14), we can assume that these activities are also permitted in a synagogue.<br>Nonetheless, this license is granted only...</i> because of the difficulty [observing the prohibition would cause them].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Forcing the scholars to leave the synagogue whenever they wanted to eat or drink would cause them to waste time that could be devoted to Torah study (<i>Magen Avraham</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>:2).<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> cites authorities who permit wider use of synagogue facilities if they were originally constructed with this use in mind. This leniency is based on <i>Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, which states that the synagogues in Babylonia could be used for mundane purposes since they were constructed with this condition in mind.</i>
It is forbidden to calculate accounts in [a synagogue], unless the accounts are connected with a mitzvah:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ketubot</i> 5a, which states that though it is forbidden to think over one's accounts on the Sabbath, one is permitted to calculate accounts that are associated with a mitzvah even in a synagogue.</i> for example, the collection of charity,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For a more precise definition of the term קופה, see <i>Hilchot Matnot Ani'im</i> 9:1.</i> the redemption of captives,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Rambam's comments on the importance of the redemption of captives, <i>Hilchot Matnot Ani'im</i> 8:10-18.<br>or the like. [Similarly,] eulogies - This does not refer to a eulogy recited in the presence of the corpse itself, but rather a public meeting in honor of the deceased after his burial (Rav Kapach).</i> or the like.<br>[Similarly,] eulogies should not be recited inside them, except a eulogy that involves many [of the inhabitants of the city]; for example, [if] there were a eulogy of the great sages of that city for which all the people would gather together and come.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Megillah</i> 28b, it appears that this refers to a eulogy recited over a great sage, by a great sage, or attended by a great sage.</i>
If a synagogue or a house of study has two entrances, one should not use it for a shortcut,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 7:2, which mentions a similar prohibition with regard to the Temple Mount.</i> i.e., to enter through one entrance and leave through the other to reduce [the distance one] travels, because it is forbidden to enter [these buildings] except for a mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Note Halachah 10, which states that if one enters a synagogue for the sake of a mitzvah, one may afterwards leave from the other entrance to shorten his way.<br>The <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> (151) questions whether a person who is going to do a mitzvah may take a shortcut through a synagogue. Though the <i>Pri Megadim</i> states that this might be permissible, the <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> maintains that the wording the Rambam chose implies that one must enter the synagogue to do a mitzvah within, and not to pass through for the sake of a mitzvah.</i>
A person who has to enter a synagogue to call a child or his friend should enter and read [a portion of the written law] or relate a teaching [of the oral law] and then call his friend,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> explains that the person should study first and then call his friend, so that it will not appear that he entered the synagogue only to serve his personal concerns.</i> so that he will not have entered [a synagogue] for his personal reasons alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">which is forbidden, as mentioned above.</i><br>If he does not know [how to study], he should ask one of the children<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This suggestion is quoted from <i>Megillah</i> 28b, the source for this halachah. As obvious from <i>Gittin</i> 58a and other sources throughout the Talmud, synagogues were often used as classrooms for younger children.</i> [to] tell him the verse he is studying<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for listening to the words of Torah recited by another person is also considered as Torah study.</i> or, [at the very least,] wait a while in the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 151:8) states that one must wait at least the length of time it takes to walk eight handbreadths (slightly less than five feet).</i> and then leave, since spending time [in the synagogue] is one of the aspects of the mitzvah as implied by [Psalms 84:5]: "Happy are those who dwell in Your house."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 151) points out that the emphasis is not on sitting, but on spending time, whether one stands or sits. To clarify this point, the Rambam does not quote the Talmudic source (<i>Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) exactly. That passage reads, "[he should] wait a while, get up, and leave." The Rambam omits the expression, "get up," to indicate that one need not actually sit.</i>
A person who enters [a synagogue] to pray or to study is permitted to leave by the opposite door to shorten his way.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 29a bases this law on Ezekiel 46:9 which states that in the Messianic age, after the people complete their service in the Temple, they will not leave through the same gate through which they entered. If this will be permitted in the Temple, it is surely permitted in a synagogue.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions the order of the halachot chosen by the Rambam, noting that it would have seemed more logical to mention this law directly after Halachah 8, which forbids taking a shortcut through a synagogue. The <i>Or Sameach</i> points out a possible resolution of this difficulty, noting that in teaching this law after Halachah 9, the Rambam implies that leniency is granted only for the sake of calling a friend. However, it is forbidden to enter a synagogue and study Torah in order to leave by the opposite door.</i><br> A person is permitted to enter a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 9:6) forbids the following four activities on the Temple Mount, considering them as irreverent. See also <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 7:2.</i> [holding] his staff, [wearing] his shoes,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Though there is no obligation to remove one's shoes before entering a synagogue, we find the practice mentioned in various sources. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 2:8) relates that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi's son removed his sandals before entering a synagogue. When he left, he discovered that they had been stolen. He said, "If I had not entered the synagogue, my sandals wouldn't have been stolen."</i> wearing [only] lower garments,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the above mishnah, the Rambam defines אפונדתו as "a garment which one wears against his flesh to collect perspiration, so that... he will not spoil his dress clothing." Others render אפונדתו as "money-belt."</i> or with dust on his feet.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 63a compares a synagogue to one's house. Since these activities are accepted in one's home, they are also acceptable in the synagogue.<br>It must be noted that many authoritative manuscripts of the<i>Mishneh Torah</i> omit the phrases "wearing [only] lower garments" and "with dust on his feet." Note also the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 151:8) which states that a person should clean mud from his shoes before entering a synagogue.</i> If it is necessary for him to spit, he may spit in the synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach cites the following statement from the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 3:5):<br>A person who spits in the synagogue is considered like one who spits in [God's] eye. Rabbi Yonah would spit and wipe it with his foot.<br>Rabbenu Manoach explains that this passage does not contradict the Babylonian Talmud (<i>Berachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) which allows one to spit in the synagogue. As long as Rabbi Yonah's practice is followed, there is no prohibition.<br>Though some authorities disagree, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 151:7) accepts Rabbenu Manoach's decision. However, while reciting the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, spitting is forbidden (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 97:2). The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 151:9 mentions that the <i>Ari zal</i> would refrain from spitting within a synagogue.</i>
Synagogues and houses of study that have been destroyed remain holy [as can be inferred from Leviticus 26:31]: "I will destroy your sanctuaries."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that since the verse states והשמותי את מקדשיכם, with the noun "sanctuaries" following the verb "I will destroy," rather than ואת מקדשיכם אשמים, one may draw the following inference.</i> [Our Sages explained]: Even though they are destroyed, they remain<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">sanctuaries and therefore, must be regarded as</i> holy.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore,</i><br>Just as one must treat them with respect while they are standing,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachot 5-10.</i> so must they be treated [with respect] when they are destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 28b mentions that if one constructed a synagogue with the condition that it can be used for mundane purposes, one may do so. As mentioned in the commentary to Halachah 6, the Ramban and other authorities maintain that if a synagogue was constructed with such a condition, guests may eat inside and it may be used for other mundane purposes.<br><i>Tosafot</i> disagrees, maintaining that the condition has no effect while the synagogue is standing and applies only after it has been destroyed. Thus, were a synagogue to be built with such a condition, as were the synagogues in Babylonia in Talmudic times, mundane activities could be carried on within its premises after it was destroyed. However, even then, activities directly opposed to the sanctity of the synagogue, e.g., sowing crops on the land, are forbidden.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 151:11) quotes <i>Tosafot's</i> opinion. Interestingly, the Rambam does not mention either the Ramban's or <i>Tosafot's</i> interpretation of the possibility of making the condition mentioned in <i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> with the exception of sweeping and mopping them. [When destroyed], they need not be swept or mopped.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for there would be no benefit in doing so.</i><br>If grass grows in them, it should be pulled out and left there<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Megillah</i> 28b) states, "If grass grow in it, it should not be pulled out." The Talmud comments that one is not allowed to pull out the grass to use as fodder for animals, but one may pull out the grass and leave it there.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam writes that one is permitted to pull the grass out and leave it in its place. The published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> (which we have quoted) appears to imply that one should pull them out (i.e., it is imperative to do so). However, the version of the halachah found in authoritative manuscripts is closer to the understanding in the Commentary to the Mishnah.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:10) quotes the version of the halachah in the published texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. However, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (151:29) quotes the Commentary to the Mishnah.</i> so that it will be seen by the people [in the hope that] it will rouse their spirits and rebuild them.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam adds that if it is impossible for the synagogues to be rebuilt, the grass should be left there since seeing it will motivate the people to Teshuvah.</i>
One should not tear down a synagogue in order to build another in its place or in another place.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 65), which states that destroying a synagogue is a transgression of a Torah commandment. Nonetheless, if one's intention is to build a new synagogue, the destruction of the old one is permitted (See also Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 152:2).</i> Instead, one should build the [new synagogue] and then, one [may] tear down the [previous] one<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This law is quoted from <i>Bava Batra</i> 3b. Two reasons are mentioned by the Talmud. The Rambam quotes one:</i> lest unforeseen difficulties arise [which prevent it] from being built.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The second reason mentioned in the Talmud is so that people will have a place to pray in the interim. The Talmud notes that different corollaries result from these two explanations. If there is another synagogue in the city, according to the second explanation (not mentioned by the Rambam), one could tear down the first synagogue while building a new one, since people would be able to pray in the other synagogue in the interim. However, according to the explanation quoted by the Rambam, it would be improper to destroy the synagogue lest the new synagogue never be built.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 152:1) quotes the Rambam's decision. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 152:4 states that this law applies even if all the money necessary to build the new synagogue has been collected.</i><br>This applies even to a single wall of [a synagogue]. One should build the new wall next to the old wall and then, tear down the old wall.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> (152) mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Asher who permits a community to enlarge a synagogue by tearing down an existing wall before building a new one if there is no other alternative. He maintains that the Rambam might also agree to this decision. However, most authorities interpret the Rambam's words as forbidding such a practice.</i>
When does the above apply? When its foundations are not ruined or its walls are not leaning perilously.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">so that there is no danger in continuing to pray within.</i> However, if its foundation is destroyed or its walls are leaning perilously,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">and there is a danger that it may collapse</i> it should be destroyed immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> (152) states that this applies even if the community does not have another place to pray.</i> and [then, efforts to] rebuild it should be begun immediately throughout the day and night lest times become difficult and it remain destroyed.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 3b relates that Rav Ashi saw a dangerous flaw in the synagogue of Mata Machsia. He ordered the building destroyed and then took his bed into the ruins to make sure that the community would rebuild it quickly. He did not remove his bed until the final fixtures of the building were completed.</i>
It is permitted to transform a synagogue into a house of study.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 27a describes a house of study as "a great house," "a house where Torah is developed."</i> However, it is forbidden to transform a house of study into a synagogue because the sanctity of a house of study exceeds that of a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">since Torah study takes precedence over the performance of all other mitzvot (See <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 3:3). See also Chapter 8, Halachah 3; <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 4:9.</i> and one must proceed to a higher rung of holiness,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the opinion of Rabbenu Nissim who maintains that it is forbidden to exchange a sacred article for another of an equal level of holiness. There are other opinions who maintain that it is permitted to do so. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:11 states that even according to those opinions, the permission to do so is after the fact (בדיעבד), but not a priori.</i> but not descend to a lower rung.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This principle applies in a number of different halachic contexts, for example, the practice of adding a new Chanukah candle each night (<i>Shabbat</i> 21a).</i><br>Similarly, the inhabitants of a city<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 26b explains that this only applies to a synagogue in a village as explained in Halachah 16.</i> who sold a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's use of the past tense appears to imply that this is a only question בדיעבד. The<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:3, however, presents as להתחילה (a priori), the possibility of selling an article of lesser sanctity in order to purchase an article of greater sanctity.<br>The sale of a synagogue is a complicated matter. See Halachot 16-20 and also the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 153:7 and commentaries.</i> may purchase an ark<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3.</i> with the proceeds.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> emphasizes that a synagogue may only be sold for such a purchase if there is another synagogue in the city.</i> If they sold an ark, they may purchase a mantle<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In Ashkenazic communities, it is customary to hold a Torah scroll in a mantle.</i> or a case<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">as is customary in Sephardic communities. <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> 10:4 describes the sanctity of these ritual articles.</i> for a Torah scroll with the proceeds. If they sold a mantle or a case, they may buy <i>chumashim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, the expression <i>chumash</i> referred to a scroll on which only one of the five books of Moses was written. Since it contained only one book, its holiness was on a lower level than that of a complete Torah scroll.</i> with the proceeds. If they sold <i>chumashim</i>, they may buy a Torah scroll with the proceeds. If they sold a Torah scroll, the proceeds may only be used to purchase another Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">There is a some difficulty with the Rambam's statements when compared to <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> 10:2, where the Rambam writes:<br>It is forbidden for a person to sell a Torah scroll even if he has nothing to eat. [It is even forbidden] to sell an old scroll in order to purchase a new one. A Torah scroll should never be sold except for [one of] two purposes, so that one can use the money to study Torah or in order to marry.<br>Perhaps here the Rambam is speaking about a question that arose after the fact, once the Torah scroll has already been sold, while in <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i>, he is describing an a priori condition. Alternatively, he may be referring to circumstances where the new Torah scroll has already been written and all that is necessary is to pay for it. In such a case, one may sell an old Torah scroll (Rabbenu Manoach, <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i>).</i> for there is no level of holiness above that of a Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes, "A kosher Torah scroll should be treated with special holiness and great honor."</i><br>The same [laws apply] to [any money] which remains.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if one sold many <i>chumashim</i> to purchase a Torah scroll and some of the proceeds from the sale remained, those monies should not be used to purchase anything on a lower level of holiness.</i>
Similar [principles apply] if a congregation collected money to build a house of study or a synagogue or to purchase an ark, a mantle or a case [for a Torah scroll], or a Torah scroll, and desired to change [the purpose for which] all the [funds] had been [originally] collected.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Just as it is forbidden to sell sacred articles in order to purchase articles of lesser sanctity...</i><br>It is forbidden to change [the purpose for which the funds will be used]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Matnot Aniyim</i> 8:11 mentions an exception to this principle:<br>If the inhabitants of a city collected money for the construction of a synagogue and a matter involving a mitzvah arises, they may use the money for it. If they already bought stones and beams, they should not sell them for the sake of another mitzvah, except for the redemption of captives.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:6) states that a synagogue - and even a Torah scroll - may be sold for the purpose of supporting Torah studies or for other needs associated with mitzvot. Nevertheless, this measure should only be taken when there is no other way to cover these expenses (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:24). See also <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 252:1) which explains that one is only allowed to sell a synagogue for such purposes if the buyer will continue to have it used as a synagogue.</i> except from a matter of lesser sanctity to one of greater sanctity.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe Cohen questions the Rambam's decision, asking why it is forbidden to make a change of this nature. Even bricks intended for use in constructing a synagogue can be used for other purposes as long as they have not been actually built into the synagogue (See <i>Megillah</i> 26b).<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:2) explains that money collected for the purchase of religious articles does not possess the sanctity of those articles. However, since the donors intended that the money be used to purchase religious articles, its use is constrained by the terms of an implicit vow that it be used for this purpose. Despite this limitation, the congregation may use these funds for another purpose associated with a mitzvah and substitute other funds to accomplish the purpose for which these funds were originally collected. (Note the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 153:5, who does not accept the latter conclusion.)<br>The <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> (153) supports the <i>Turei Zahav's</i> position based on <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 1:20, which states that a sacred utensil made with the intent that it be used in the Temple is not considered as "sacred" until it has actually been used for its intended purpose.</i> However, if [the congregation] accomplished the purpose for which they had [originally] collected [the funds],<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., they collected funds to purchase a Torah scroll and were able to purchase it for less money than they had collected.</i> they may use the remainder for whatever they desire.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 27a mentions this leniency based on the principle that the money itself does not have the sanctity of the religious articles for whose purchase it was donated, but is merely designated to be used for their purchase.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Once the religious articles have been purchased, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (153:14) states that these funds can be used for any purpose of benefit to the community, even if it has no association with a sacred article. However, there are authorities who require the money to be used to purchase an article which has some degree of sanctity.</i><br> All the components of a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the platform from which the Torah is read or its benches (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Megillah</i> 3:1)</i> are considered<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">to be on the same level of holiness</i> like the synagogue itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">and can only be sold for similar purposes.<br>Our Sages placed certain restrictions on the sale of the components of a synagogue. In <i>Hilchot Matnot Ani'im</i> 8:6, the Rambam writes:<br>If someone donates a light or a candle to a synagogue, it is forbidden to exchange it [for something else]. If [the sale] concerns a matter which is a mitzvah, it may be exchanged.... If the name of the donor has been forgotten, it is permitted to exchange [the object] even for a mundane matter.<br>In <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> 10:4, the Rambam states that the platform "has no sanctity at all." However, this statement should be interpreted to mean that the platform is not considered a "sacred article" like the others mentioned in that halachah.</i> The curtain hanging before the ark<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">it is not clear whether the Rambam is referring to the <i>tevah</i> (the movable ark) or the <i>heichal</i> (the fixed ark). Rabbenu Nissim interprets the reference as to be to the <i>tevah</i>.</i> is considered like the mantle of a Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">since from time to time, it is placed under the Torah scroll (<i>Megillah</i> 26b).</i> If a condition<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">allowing the use of the curtains for mundane purposes</i> was made concerning them,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">when they were originally purchased</i> the terms of the condition are binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">and there is no prohibition against using them for such purposes.</i>
When do the above statements permitting the sale of a synagogue apply? In regard to a synagogue in a village. Since it was constructed for the sake of the inhabitants of that village alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even if the money for its construction was donated by people from outside the city (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:7).</i> so that they can pray inside it,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Based on this statement, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:25 explains that the main determinant is not the size of the village, but whether the synagogue is also frequented by people from the outside. According to this understanding, a synagogue in a small village where large trade fairs are held is bound by the same laws as the synagogues of a large city. By the same token, a synagogue used by a private group of people in a large city may be comparable to a village synagogue. An example of such a case, cited by the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i>, is the custom once common that craftsmen of different professions would build synagogues for themselves.</i> they are permitted to sell it if they all desire to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramban states that the consent of the majority of the village's inhabitants is sufficient. His opinion is accepted as halachah by the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:24.</i><br>In contrast, a synagogue in a metropolis, since it was constructed for the sake of all the people in the world, [i.e.,] so that anyone who comes to that country can come and pray in it,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, even if the money to build the synagogue was donated by the inhabitants of the city alone, they do not have the authority to sell the synagogue (Rabbenu Asher, <i>Megillah</i> 26a).</i> it is considered as [the property] of [the entire] Jewish people and it can never be sold.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 19 mentions an exception to this principle. Also, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 153:12 relates that if a synagogue in a city is no longer used for prayer, it may be sold. Based on this decision, the Rabbis (See <i>Iggeret Moshe, Orach Chayim</i>, Vol. I, 50) have permitted the sale of synagogues located in neighborhoods no longer inhabited by Jews.</i>
The inhabitants of a village<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous Halachah.</i> who desire to sell their synagogue in order to build another synagogue with the money, or to buy an ark or Torah scroll with the money,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in keeping with Halachah 15</i> must establish as a condition [of the sale] that the purchaser not use the building for a bathhouse, a leatherworks, a <i>mikveh</i>, or a laundry.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The translation of the Hebrew, בית המים, is based on Rashi's commentary to <i>Megillah</i> 27b. He also offers an alternate translation, "a latrine." The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> favors the translation "laundry" since the prohibition against using the synagogue as a latrine is self-evident. Nevertheless, in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:9), Rav Yosef Karo mentions a latrine, but not a laundry.<br>The sanctity of the synagogue is transferred to the money received from its sale and the building itself is no longer considered as "holy." Nonetheless, using the premises for such purposes is considered degrading.<br>As support for the prohibition against using premises that had previously served as a synagogue for such purposes, the <i>Or Sameach</i> cites II Chronicles 8:11: "I will not have a woman dwelling in the house of David... for the [places] where the ark of God has come are holy." Although the ark had already been placed in the Temple, it was not becoming to the ark for Pharaoh's daughter to be allowed to live in a place where it had been kept.</i><br>If, at the time of the sale, the seven officials of the community<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In a responsum, the Rambam explains that there is no obligation to appoint seven officials to lead the community. Our Sages' use of that term merely implies that the governing communal body should not be a small group. Since the number seven is often used in the Bible, our sages employed it in this context.<br>In the same responsum, the Rambam defines the expression 18יבוט ריעה to mean "sages, men of Torah and good deeds, [people of whom it can be said] 'pleasant are the words spoken by those who fulfill them.'</i> made a condition in the presence of the entire community<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:7) states that a public announcement of the sale is sufficient for it to be considered as if all the inhabitants of the city were present.</i> that the purchaser be allowed to use the building for the above purposes, he may.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 26a,b states that the restriction against selling a synagogue for an unbecoming purpose does not apply when the building was sold by the communal officials in the presence of the community. When it is sold in this manner, it can be "used even as a tavern."<br>The Meiri explains the rationale for this decision as follows: Even if an explicit statement was not made to the effect that the synagogue could be used for an unbecoming purpose, it is understood that the village's inhabitants consecrated the synagogue with the understanding that it be subject to the decisions of the community's officials. Therefore, they have the right to sell the synagogue for whatever purposes they wish.<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's decision and maintains that the license granted by <i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) only applies to the money received from the sale, while the building that was used as a synagogue can never be used for a purpose which is unbecoming. Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) quotes the Rambam's view, the <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> suggests following the Ra'avad's decision, quoting the law (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 21:2), which forbids using a <i>tallit</i> for an unbecoming purpose even after its <i>tzitzit</i> have been removed.<br>Rav Moshe Feinstein (Responsa, <i>Orach Chayim</i>, Vol. II, 45) mentions this halachah in regard to the sale of synagogues in neighborhoods where Jews no longer live. Unfortunately, the natural clients for the purchase of an abandoned synagogue are churches. Rav Moshe states that selling a synagogue for use as a church is definitely forbidden. Using a synagogue for such purposes is more demeaning than using it for the purposes mentioned by the Talmud. He maintains that even according to the Rambam, selling a synagogue for this purpose could not be permitted.</i>
Similarly, if the seven officials of the community made a condition in the presence of the entire community that [after the community accomplished the purpose for which they sold the synagogue], the remainder of the funds could be used for mundane purposes, they may be used for such purposes.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:7) accepts the opinion of Rashi, the Ra'avad, and Rabbenu Asher, who differ with the Rambam and maintain that the entire proceeds from the sale of a synagogue can be used for mundane purposes if the sale was carried out in the above manner.<br>In his <i>Kessef Mishnah</i>, Rav Yosef Karo explains the difference between these positions as follows. These authorities do not accept the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Megillah</i> 26a-b mentioned in the previous Halachah, and maintain that a certain measure of sanctity always remains in the synagogue. The Rambam maintains that the sanctity is transferred to the money received for the synagogue. Therefore, the condition made by the city officials can never effect the entire sum of money. Rather, the major portion must always be used for a sacred object.<br>[Interestingly, in his <i>Kessef Mishnah</i>, Rav Yosef Karo points out the advantages of the Rambam's position. However, in his<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, he accepts the decisions of the other authorities.]</i> Thus, after the money has been used for building another synagogue, for purchasing an ark, a mantle or case [for a Torah scroll], <i>chumashim</i>, or a Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in Halachah 14.</i> the remainder may be used for mundane purposes in accordance with their condition, and may be used for whatever they please.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 27a describes such a situation and states that the remainder of the funds may be used even to hire a messenger for the city.</i>
Similarly, if all the inhabitants of a city - or a majority of them<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">accept [the authority of] a single individual, - Note the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:35 which states that this applies only when the inhabitants of the city donated the money for the synagogue's construction themselves and accepted the authority of the person involved from the beginning of the synagogue's construction.</i> - accept [the authority of] a single individual, whatever actions he takes [in regard to a synagogue] are binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 26a relates:<br>Rav Ashi said: "Even though people from all over the world come into the synagogue of Mata Machsia to pray, since the people donated the money for its construction subject to my decisions, allowing me to do what I want, I am permitted to sell it if I so desire."<br>He may sell [the synagogue] - as mentioned in the previous halachot</i> He may sell [the synagogue] or give [it as a gift]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in the following Halachah.</i> alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This expression appears to indicate that the sale may be conducted even without a public announcement as is required of the city officials in the previous halachot. Rabbenu Nissim (and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) disagree, stating that the sale must be made with the consent of the community.</i> as he sees fit, and establish whatever conditions he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">in regard to the use of the premises or the proceeds from the sale.</i>
Just as it is permitted to sell a synagogue,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in a village, and afterwards, use the premises for mundane purposes</i> it is permitted to give it away as a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">to an individual to use as he sees fit. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 153:26 states that the gift must be made by the community officials in the presence of the inhabitants of the village. However, the <i>Pri Megadim</i> does not require these conditions. Since the Rambam equates giving the synagogue as a present to a sale, it is not likely that he would make such limitations.<br><i>Megillah</i> 26b states, "a gift is like a sale," because</i> If the community had not received any benefit from giving it as a gift, it would not have given it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is also reflected in questions of business law, e.g., <i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 9:9.<br>The Ritbah states that even when the community has not yet received any benefit from a person, it may give him a synagogue in the expectation of receiving such benefit.</i> However, it cannot be rented or given as security.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the Talmudic era, when property was given as security, the person who received the property would have the right to use it throughout the term of the loan. [This practice raises questions in regard to the prohibition against taking interest. See <i>Hilchot Malveh U'Loveh</i>, Chapters 6 and 7.]<br>When a synagogue is sold, its sanctity is transferred to the money received for it and does not remain within the building itself. However, if a synagogue was rented or given as security, there is nothing to which the sanctity is transferred. Hence, it remains within the building and the person who rents it or receives it as security is forbidden to use it for his own purposes.<br>Note the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:11) who states that the prohibition only applies when the person who receives the synagogue wants to use it for mundane purposes. If he continues to use it as a synagogue there is no difficulty.<br>Similarly, when a synagogue is being torn down so that it can be rebuilt, - Note Halachah 12 which states that the new synagogue must be built before the old one is torn down.</i><br>Similarly, when a synagogue is being torn down so that it can be rebuilt, it is permitted to sell the bricks, timber, and soil, exchange them,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">even if the articles given in return are received later (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 153:68)</i> or give them as gifts.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">in return for benefit, as explained above.</i> However, it is forbidden to lend them,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 153:11) states that this prohibition applies even when the loan is made by the community officials in the presence of the community.<br>Though the community officials have the power to nullify the sanctity of a synagogue as mentioned in Halachot 17 and 18, that provision only applies when they do so completely. In this instance, since the building materials have to be returned, their sanctity is not nullified. Hence, no one has the right to lend them to anyone for mundane use.</i> since their sanctity only departs in return for money or benefit which is equivalent to money.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Similar principles apply in regard to the redemption of other articles endowed with sanctity, e.g., <i>Ma'aser Sheni</i> (the second tithe) or articles dedicated to the Temple (הקדש).</i>
Although the people pray in a city's main street on fast days<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ta'anit</i> 4:1 states:<br>On each of the seven days of fasting for rain... the ark is taken out to the main street of the city and the entire populace gather together wearing sackcloth. Ashes are placed on the ark and the Torah scroll... and each person places [ashes] on his head.<br>and ma'amadot, - The mention of <i>ma'amadot</i> in this context in <i>Megillah</i> 26a (the source for this halachah), and here, in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, has raised questions. In <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 6:1-2, the Rambam describes the <i>ma'amadot</i> as follows:<br>The early prophets ordained that honest people who fear sin should be chosen to stand over the sacrifices. They are called <i>anshei ma'amad</i>. They were divided into 24 different watches....Each week, the <i>anshei ma'amad</i> of that watch would gather together. Those who lived in Jerusalem or close to it would come to the Temple.... Those who lived far away... would gather together in the synagogues of their locale.<br>In no source other than <i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) is it mentioned that the <i>anshei ma'amad</i> would pray in the street.</i> and <i>ma'amadot</i>, because too many people gather to fit within a synagogue,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Ta'anit</i> 16a which states that the people would pray in the street as an expression of shame and embarrassment; alternatively, because leaving one's normal place of prayer is a form of exile which brings atonement. Interestingly, the Rambam ignores both these reasons and offers a reason which is not mentioned in previous sources.</i> [the street] does not possess any quality of sanctity, because [praying there is only a temporary measure] and it is not established as a place of prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">[the street] does not possess any quality of sanctity, because [praying there is only a temporary measure] and it is not established as a place of prayer.</i><br> Similarly, buildings and courtyards where people gather to pray<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">even for communal prayer, e.g., a room in an office building where people gather for prayer.</i> do not possess any measure of sanctity,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, it is also permitted to perform mundane activities (those mentioned in Halachah 6) in these buildings.</i> because they were not designated for prayer alone. Rather, [people] pray within them as a temporary measure, as a person prays within his home.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 153:8 mentions a situation in which a building is designated as a synagogue temporarily, for merely a limited period. During that time, it has the holiness of a synagogue. However, once it is no longer used as a synagogue, it has no holiness.</i>

Chapter 12

Moses, our teacher, ordained that the Jews should read the Torah publicly on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See also Chapter 13, Halachah 8.</i> and on Monday and Thursday<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Bava Kama</i> 82a relates that these days are days of Divine favor.</i> mornings,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 135:1 states that, although ideally the Torah should be read in the morning service, if one fails to do so, it may be read the entire day.</i> so the [people] would never have three days pass without hearing the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 82b quotes Exodus 15:22: "And they travelled three days without finding water," and explains:<br>Water refers to the Torah, as [implied by Isaiah 55:1]: "May all the thirsty go to the water." Since they travelled three days without Torah, they complained. The prophets among them arose and ordained that they read [Torah] on the Sabbath, refrain [from reading] on Sunday, read on Monday, refrain [from reading] on Tuesday and Wednesday, read on Thursday, and refrain from reading on Friday, so that they will not spend three days without [reading from] the Torah.</i><br>Ezra ordained that [the Torah] should be read during the <i>Minchah</i> service on the Sabbath, because of the shopkeepers.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew, יושבי קרנות, literally means "those who sit on the street corners." Our translation is based on Rashi's commentary (<i>Bava Kama</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>). He explains that during the week, these people were involved with their businesses and could not attend the Torah reading. Therefore, Ezra instituted a special Torah reading for them on the Sabbath, when work is prohibited.<br>Rav David Arameah interprets the phrase literally, explaining that on the Sabbath, since work is prohibited, people would gather in the afternoon in the marketplace, and spend their time in idle conversation. Therefore, Ezra ordained the public Torah reading to draw them into the synagogue.</i> He also ordained that on Mondays and Thursdays, three<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 21b relates that the number three reflects the three divisions among the Jewish people: priests, Levites, and Israelites; alternatively, the three divisions in the Written Law (the Torah, Prophets, and Holy Writings).</i> people should read<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, the person called to the Torah would read from the Torah himself. Our custom of having one reader is discussed in the commentary on Halachah 17.</i> [from the Torah]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that originally either one person would read three verses, or three people would read three verses.</i>, and that they should read no fewer than ten verses.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that the ten verses allude to the ten commandments and the ten utterances of creation; alternatively, they represent the ten people who attend a synagogue at all times. (See Halachah 3.)</i>
These are the days when the Torah is read publicly: Sabbaths, festivals,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">including <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, the intermediate days of the festival. (See Chapter 13, Halachot 8-16.)</i> <i>Rashei Chadashim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soferim</i> 10:1 attributes the introduction of the practice of reading the Torah on all the abovementioned days to Moses. (See Chapter 13, Halachah 4, regarding the Rosh Chodesh Torah reading.)</i> fast days,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.</i> Chanukah, Purim,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 17.</i> and Mondays and Thursdays each week.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and Mondays and Thursdays each week.</i><br>The <i>haftarah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pardes</i> states that the reading of the <i>haftarah</i> was instituted on the days when work is forbidden because the people had more time then and were able to devote themselves to Torah study. The <i>Avudraham</i> relates that the reading of the <i>haftarah</i> was instituted when the Greeks forbade the public reading of the Torah. To compensate, the Jews instituted the reading of parallel portions from the prophets. Even after the decree was nullified, the custom remained.</i> is read only<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ta'aniot</i> 1:17, the Rambam states that on fast days declared because of communal distress, the <i>haftarah</i> is read in the afternoon service. However, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 574) explains that the laws concerning the reading of the Torah and the <i>haftarah</i> are discussed primarily in this and the following chapter of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Hence, the decision here should be followed and the <i>haftarah</i> should not be recited.</i> on Sabbaths, festivals,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">when work is not permitted</i> and Tish'ah B'Av.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Although work is permitted on Tish'ah B'Av, as mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ta'aniot</i> 5:10, Torah Sages have accepted the custom not to work on that day, and our Sages declared that no one will see a sign of blessing from work done on tAlthough work is permitted on Tish'ah B'Av, as mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ta'aniot</i> 5:10, Torah Sages have accepted the custom not to work on that day, and our Sages declared that no one will see a sign of blessing from work done on that day. Hence, there is no difficulty in reading the <i>haftarah</i> in the morning. (See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.)<br>In Ashkenazic communities, it is also customary to read the <i>haftarah</i> on fast days in the <i>Minchah</i> service (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 566:1).hat day. Hence, there is no difficulty in reading the <i>haftarah</i> in the morning. (See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.)<br>In Ashkenazic communities, it is also customary to read the <i>haftarah</i> on fast days in the <i>Minchah</i> service (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 566:1).</i>
The Torah is never read in public in the presence of fewer than ten adult free men.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 8, Halachot 4-6.</i> No fewer than ten verses are read.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 1. <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> notes that the reading for Purim contains only nine verses, but explains that since this passage discusses a complete subject, an exception is made. (See also <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 137:1.)</i> <i>Vayedaber</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a verse beginning "And God said to Moses:...." Although it is merely an introductory phrase, it...</i> is counted as one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., one of the required ten verses. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 137:4) states that, after the fact, if only nine verses were read, it is sufficient.</i> No fewer than three men should read.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 1.</i><br>[When] beginning a passage<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the 54 weekly Torah portions, the Torah is divided into 290 smaller passages (<i>parshiot</i>). (See <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i>, Chapter 8.)</i> from the Torah, [one should read] at least three verses,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A person who begins a new passage in the midst of his <i>aliyah</i> should read at least three verses, lest another person enter when he begins that passage and think that he has read fewer than three verses (<i>Megillah 22a).<br></i><br><i>The 14Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) states that if a person read only two verses, they must be read again.</i> and one should not conclude less than three verses from the conclusion of a passage.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">lest someone who leaves before the next person reads from the Torah come to the mistaken conclusion that he has read fewer than three verses (<i>Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>). (See Chapter 13, Halachah 4. See also <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i>, 138.)</i> Each reader should not read fewer than three verses.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The three verses allude to the threefold division of the written law (<i>Megillah</i> 24a). Even when an entire passage has only two verses, the reader must add at least three more verses from another passage.<br>From this halachah, it appears that the custom of reading the first <i>aliyah</i> of the portion to be read on the following Sabbath on Mondays and Thursdays was not fixed in the Rambam's time. Hence, these ground rules were necessary. (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 137:4.)</i>
[When] three people read ten verses: Two read three [verses each] and one, four [verses]. It is praiseworthy regardless of whether the one who read four [verses] is first,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for the most important person is called to the Torah first (<i>Megillah</i> 21b).</i> last,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">based on the principle to "always proceed higher in holy matters" (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> or in the middle.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">regarding the Menorah, the middle branch was most important (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
Each one of the readers opens the Torah scroll and looks at the place from which he is to read.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 32a mentions a difference of opinion between Rabbi Yehudah, who maintains that the blessings for the Torah should be recited while the Torah scroll is open (as quoted by the Rambam), and Rabbi Meir, who maintains that the Torah scroll should be closed, lest the people err and think that the blessings are written in the Torah.<br>Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 139:4) quotes the Rambam's decision, the <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> explains that Rabbi Yehudah maintains that one is not obligated to close the Torah scroll before reciting the blessing. However, there is nothing wrong in doing so. Therefore, in many communities the custom is to roll the Torah closed before reciting the blessing.<br>Note the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>, 140:3) regarding the ruling when one looks at the wrong passage in the Torah before reciting the blessing.</i> Afterwards, he declares,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>, 139:6) emphasizes how <i>Barchu</i> and the blessings for the Torah should be recited in a loud voice.</i> <i>Barchu et Ado-nai hamevorach</i>, and all the people<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">the person reciting the blessing also joins in reciting the following phrase (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>:7)</i> answer: <i>Baruch Ado-nai hamevorach le'olam va'ed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 1.</i> He then recites the blessing:<br>Blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note the description of Ezra's reading of the Torah, in Nechemiah, Chapter 8, which relates how "Ezra opened the scroll... and blessed God."</i> are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has chosen us from among all the nations and given us His Torah. Blessed are You, God, the Giver of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is the third of the blessings of the Torah mentioned in Chapter 7, Halachah 10. Even someone who had just recited the blessings before the Torah as part of his individual prayers recites this blessing as a gesture of respect for the community.<br>As mentioned in the commentary on that halachah, the Ramban considers the obligation to recite the blessings before the Torah as one of the 613 mitzvot. Some later commentaries explain that this refers only to the blessings recited before reading the Torah in public.</i><br>All the people respond: "Amen."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Note Rashi's commentary, <i>Berachot</i> 21a. Note also the comments of the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>, Chapter 7, Halachah 15, that a person can fulfill his requirement of reciting one hundred blessings on the Sabbath by answering "Amen" to these blessings.</i> Afterwards, he reads until he completes the reading, rolls the scroll [closed]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this is done in deference to Rabbi Meir's opinion mentioned above. The <i>Lechem Yehudah</i> explains that it is a gesture of respect for the Torah.</i> and recites the blessing:<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has given us His Torah, the Torah of truth,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text usually recited in Sephardic communities. In Ashkenazic communities, the word, תורתו (His Torah), is not included in the blessing. Interestingly, the Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also omit this word.</i> and implanted eternal life<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the printed text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Note the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i> 139:10), which follows the text - חיי העולם (adding a ה as a modifier) - "the life of the world (to come)."</i> in our midst.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">It is proper to recite this phrase only once the Torah has been read. Only after it has been studied, does the Torah serve as a source of life (<i>Avudraham</i>).</i> Blessed are You, God, the Giver of the Torah.
The person reading the Torah is not allowed to begin reading until the congregation ceases responding "Amen."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">so that their recitation of "Amen" will not drown out the Torah reading. Today, in communities where the Torah is read by a person other than the one reciting the blessings, it is customary for the reader to prolong his recitation of "Amen" slightly, so that everyone will know when the Torah reading begins (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 141:17).</i> If one erred while reading, even regarding the careful pronunciation of one letter,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach explains that this law reflects a fundamental philosophical principle. <i>Sanhedrin</i> 99a states that anyone who says that even one letter of the Torah was not given by God is considered as "one who scorned the word of God." Therefore, every letter in the Torah must be pronounced correctly.</i> [the reader] is forced to repeat [the reading] until he reads it correctly.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if one has already read other verses or even recited the blessing over the verse which was read incorrectly (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 142:2).</i><br>Two people should not read at the same time. Rather, one should read alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for the voice of two people cannot be heard at the same time. Note the Rambam's decision, <i>Hilchot Shofar</i> 3:6. See also a contrasting decision in <i>Hilchot Megillah</i> 2:7.<br>In communities where the Torah is read by a person other than the one who recites the blessings, the person reciting the blessings should be careful not to read out loud (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 141:2).</i> If one was reading and lost the ability to speak, another should replace him. He should begin from the place where the one who lost the ability to speak began,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his responsa, the Rambam cites the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 5:3) to explain why this law differs from the law mentioned in Chapter 10, Halachah 4, which states that if a <i>chazan</i> cannot continue the recitation of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, the person who replaces him begins from the point where he left off. The Rambam explains that it is necessary for the second person reading the Torah to repeat the verses, because otherwise, the verses read by the initial reader will not be included in the concluding blessing.</i> and recite the blessing after concluding.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, according to the Rambam, the second reader need not recite the blessing before the Torah reading.
