Rabbenu Asher (whose opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, 140:1) does not accept this decision, and requires the person who continues reading to recite the first blessing before he begins reading the Torah.<br>The difference between the two opinions is that the Rambam considers the blessings to be associated with the Torah portion and not with the reader. In contrast, Rabbenu Asher considers the blessings as the personal responsibility of the reader in preparation for reading from the Torah.<br>Rabbenu Asher's opinion is accepted by the later authorities. Even in communities where a person other than the one who recites the blessings reads from the Torah, this law applies (Ramah).</i>
The reader is not permitted to [begin] reading until the person of greatest stature within the community<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the Rabbi or spiritual leader.</i> tells him to [begin] reading.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though this law is apparent from the <i>Tosefta</i> referred to below, no explicit source is mentioned by the commentaries.</i> Even the <i>chazan</i> or the <i>gabbai</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on Rashi's commentary (<i>Yoma</i> 68b), which describes the <i>rosh hak'nesset</i> as the one who appoints the leader of prayer and gives out the <i>aliyot</i>.</i> should not begin reading on their own initiative until the community [as a whole] or the person of greatest stature begins to read.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This law is quoted from the <i>Tosefta, Megillah</i> 3:21, which explains that this restriction was instituted to prevent the synagogue functionaries from taking advantage of their position, and thus create a rift between them and the other congregants.</i><br>[When the <i>chazan</i> reads from the Torah,]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As discussed in the commentary on Halachah 17, the Rambam requires the person who receives an <i>aliyah</i> to read from the Torah himself. Thus, the <i>chazan</i> would read only when he, himself, received an <i>aliyah</i>.</i> another person should stand with him while he reads, just as the <i>chazan</i> stands together with the other readers.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 4:1) explains the source for this practice. Just as an intermediary was involved with the giving of the Torah - as Deuteronomy 5:5 states: "I stood between you and God, your Lord" - so, too, another person should stand together with the reader at the reading of the Torah.<br>Note <i>Soferim</i> 14:14 (quoted by the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 141:16), which mentions the custom that two other people stand by the reader while the Torah is read, one on his right and one on his left.</i>
The reader may skip from place<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">from one passage in the Torah</i> to place<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">to a different passage in the Torah</i> in one subject<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">However, skipping from one subject to another subject is forbidden, because it may confuse the listeners. (See the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 144:1.)</i> - for example,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam chooses as his example the passages read by the High Priest in the Temple on Yom Kippur. However, as explained in the Mishnah (<i>Yoma</i> 7:1), the High Priest, himself, would not skip from passage to passage in the Torah.</i> from <i>Acharei mot...</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus, Chapter 16, which describes in detail the Yom Kippur offerings.</i> to <i>Ach be'asor</i>, in the portion <i>Emor el Hacohanim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 23:26-32, which describes the mitzvah to fast and the prohibition of work on Yom Kippur.</i> - provided he does not read by heart. It is forbidden [for a reader] to say even one word [without looking at the text].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kapach cites the <i>Midrash Tanchuma, Va'era</i> 5, which states:<br>A reader is forbidden to take his eyes off the Torah scroll, for the Torah was given only in writing, as [Exodus 34:2] states: "And I will write the words on the tablets."<br>Note also <i>Gittin</i> 60b, which mentions a general prohibition against reciting verses from the written Torah by heart.</i> [When] skipping [in this fashion, the reader] should not wait longer than it takes for the translator<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> to translate one verse.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">because waiting any longer would be an affront to the congregation (<i>Yoma</i> 69b).<br>At present, it is customary to skip from passage to passage in the reading of the Torah on public fast days alone. (See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.) Even then, the transition is made between the first and second <i>aliyot</i>, so that it will not be noticeable to the listeners.</i>
Once the reader begins reading the Torah, it is forbidden [for the congregants] to talk,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 146:1) states that the prohibition applies even between <i>aliyot</i>.</i> even regarding matters of Torah law.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 146:5 mentions that if it is necessary to prevent a person from committing a sin, one may speak even while the Torah is being read.</i> Rather, everyone should listen, remain silent, and pay attention to what is being read, as [Nechemiah 8:3] states: "The ears of all the people were [attentive] to the Torah scroll."<br>It<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This describes Ezra's reading of the Torah to the people who returned to Zion on Rosh HaShanah.</i> is forbidden to leave the synagogue while the reader is reading from the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This prohibition applies even though one has already heard the Torah reading oneself and there are ten other people listening to the Torah reading (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 146:1).<br><i>Berachot</i> 8a interprets Isaiah 1:28, "Those who turn away from God will be destroyed," as a reference to a person who leaves the synagogue when the Torah scroll is open.</i> However, one is permitted to leave between <i>aliyot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">provided there are ten others who will remain to hear the Torah reading, and one has fulfilled (or will be able to fulfill) one's obligation to hear the Torah. Even under these circumstances, leaving the synagogue is not desirable (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 146:2-3).</i> A person who is constantly involved in Torah study, and Torah is his occupation,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The license for such a person to ignore the Torah reading in order to concentrate on his studies is taken from <i>Berachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), which relates that while the Torah was being taken out, Rav Sheshet would turn his back and occupy himself in his studies, saying, "Let them be occupied in what concerns them, while we will be occupied in what concerns us."<br>See <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 1:4, which states that the mitzvah of Torah study takes precedence over all other commandments. Based on this principle, Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai and his disciples would not interrupt their studies even for prayer (<i>Shabbat</i> 11a - see Chapter 6, Halachah 8). However, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i> 106:3) states that this applies only to scholars like Rabbi Shimon, who never interrupt their studies at all. However, at present, even scholars whose occupation is Torah must interrupt their studies for prayer, for they make other interruptions as well.<br>The Rabbis question whether the law under discussion applies only to scholars of Rabbi Shimon's level, or whether it is also relevant to scholars of the present day. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that the citation of the behavior of Rav Sheshet as a source for this halachah appears to support the latter opinion. Rav Sheshet, like the other Amoraim of the Talmudic period, was not considered to be on the same level as Rabbi Shimon. In contrast, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 146:9 maintains that, at present, we have no scholars who have the level of devotion to Torah study that would permit such a leniency.</i> is permitted to involve himself in Torah study while the Torah is being read.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i> 146:2) and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 146:8-10 place various restrictions on this license. There must be ten others listening to the Torah, the person must study quietly, and he should turn away and begin his studies before the Torah reading is begun.</i>
From the time of Ezra,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">when the Jews who returned from the Babylonian exile did not speak Hebrew fluently. (See Chapter 1, Halachah 4.)</i> it was customary that a translator would translate to the people the [passages] read by the reader from the Torah, so that they would understand the subject matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Book of Nechemiah, Chapter 8, describes Ezra's reading of the Torah to the people on Rosh HaShanah. Verses 7 and 8 explain that "they caused the people to understand the reading." <i>Megillah</i> 3a explains that this refers to the translation of the Torah.</i><br>The reader should read one verse alone and remain silent while the translator translates it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For the two voices will prevent the people from hearing either of them.</i> Afterwards, he should read a second verse. The reader is not permitted to read to the translator more than one verse [at a time].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lest the translator become confused.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 145) writes that even in Talmudic times, it was not customary to translate the Torah in all communities. He explains that, in his age, the custom of translating the Torah had already been ceased because the people did not understand the Aramaic translation traditionally used. The rabbis did not want to translate the Torah into the languages which the people did understand, because of the possibility of error and misinterpretation. Such fears had not existed in regard to the Aramaic translation, since it had been composed with <i>Ruach Hakodesh</i> (Divine inspiration).<br>[The <i>Tur</i>, however, also mentions the opinion of Rav Natrunai Gaon, who maintains that the translation should be done freely, so that the people can understand, without referring to the traditional text.]<br>The <i>Tur</i>'s opinion is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 145:3) and in nearly all contemporary Jewish communities, the custom of using a translator during the Torah reading is no longer practiced. For this reason, rather than present a running commentary on Halachot 11 and 12, we have limited our comments to short footnotes.1. <i>Berachot</i> 45a states that this principle is derived from the giving of the Torah on Mount Sinai. God, "the Reader," did not lift his voice over that of Moses, "the translator."<br>It was also customary to use a מתורגמן in teaching the oral law. Hence, parallels to many of the laws mentioned in this halachah can also be found in <i>Hilchot Talmud Torah</i> 4:3.2. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 4:1) mentions that we should approach the Torah reading with awe, reflecting the awe experienced by the Jews at Mount Sinai.3. Rabbenu Nissim maintains that this prohibition only applied before Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi permitted the oral tradition to be recorded. Afterwards, it was permitted for the translator to use a written text. Rabbenu Nissim's position is somewhat difficult to accept, since the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>), the source for this law, was written several hundred years after Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi granted this leniency. Nevertheless, Rabbenu Nissim's view was widely accepted.4. The <i>Tosefta, Megillah</i> 3:21, cites the example of Aharon, who served as a spokesman for Moses. See also <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.5. Genesis 35:22. The direct translation of this verse would be unbecoming to both Reuven and Jacob (Rashi, <i>Megillah</i> 25a).6. The third of the priestly blessings states: "May God turn His countenance to you...." This appears to be a direct contradiction to the description of God (Deuteronomy 10:17  as "not turning His face...." Though our Sages resolved that difficulty (<i>Berachot</i> 20b), a problem might come up in a simple translation of the verse.<br>Rav Kapach notes that the Yemenite texts of Targum Onkelos lack a translation for the three verses of the priestly benediction.7. Were the common people given the opportunity of hearing the full story of the golden calf, they might believe that it had spiritual power (Rashi, <i>loc. cit.</i>). Alternatively, the narrative places a major burden of responsibility on Aharon (<i>Tosafot, Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).8. The commentaries note that this passage is not read as a <i>haftarah</i> at present. Rav Kapach states that the need to make this statement indicates that in Talmudic times, the passages that were read as haftarot were not fixed and there was some room for choice.9. Verses from the Torah cannot be skipped in a public Torah reading. However, it is permitted to skip verses from the prophets while reading the <i>haftarah</i> (<i>Tos'fot Yom Tov, Megillah</i> 10:3).</i>
The reader is not permitted to raise his voice above that of the translator, nor should the translator raise his voice above that of the reader. The translator is not permitted to [begin] translating until the reader completes reading the verse, nor may the reader [begin] reading another verse until the translator has completed the translation.<br>The translator should not lean on a beam or on a pillar. Rather, he should stand with awe and fear. He should not translate from a written text, but rather should recite the translation by heart.<br>The reader is not permitted to assist the translator, lest people say: "The translation is written in the Torah scroll." A person of lesser stature may serve as a translator for a person of greater stature. However, it is not befitting the honor of a person of greater stature to serve as a translator for a person of lesser stature. Two people should not serve as translators simultaneously; rather, one person should read and one should translate.
Not all passages from the Torah are translated in public. All [of the following passages] should be read, but not translated: the incident involving Reuven, the priestly benediction, [the passage describing the sin] of the golden calf from "And Moses told Aharon" (Exodus 32:21  until "And Moses saw the people" (Exodus 32:25  and one other verse, "And God set a plague upon the people" (Exodus 32:35 .<br>In the [description of] the incident concerning Amnon (II Samuel, Chapter 13), the verse which states, "Amnon, the son of David" (13:1) should be neither read nor translated.
The person who reads the <i>haftarah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on Halachah 2 for a description of the origin of the custom of reading the <i>haftarah</i>.</i> must read from the Torah first.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This was instituted as a token of respect for the Torah reading. Reading from the prophets without reading from the Torah would imply an equivalence between the seven people who read from the Torah and the one who reads from the prophets (<i>Megillah</i> 23a).</i> Even three verses [are sufficient].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 3, each person called to the Torah must read at least three verses.</i> He should read again the passage that had been read previously.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the concluding three lines of the Torah reading.<br>Note <i>Tosafot, Megillah</i> 23a, which states that in Talmudic times, the person who recited the <i>haftarah</i> would read a separate portion from the Torah. It is our custom that on festivals and in the special circumstances described in Chapter 13, Halachot 20-24, that the person who reads the <i>haftarah</i> reads a separate Torah portion.<br>He should not [begin] reading the <i>haftarah</i> until the Torah scroll has been rolled closed. - Rashi, <i>Sotah</i> 39b, states that the person reading the <i>haftarah</i> should wait to allow the person who rolled the Torah closed also to hear the <i>haftarah</i>. Others explain that it is not respectful to the Torah for it be open while the <i>haftarah</i> is being read.</i><br>He should not [begin] reading the <i>haftarah</i> until the Torah scroll has been rolled closed. He should not read fewer than twenty-one verses [as the <i>haftarah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since an <i>aliyah</i> has a minimum of three verses, the twenty-one verses of the <i>haftarah</i> will parallel the seven <i>aliyot</i> of the Torah reading (<i>Megillah</i> 23a).</i> However, if a concept is completed in fewer [verses] than that, he need not add more.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) cites the eight concluding verses from Jeremiah, Chapter 7, which were read as the <i>haftarah</i> for <i>Parashat Tzav</i>. (At present, it is customary to add a number of other verses to that <i>haftarah</i>. The <i>haftarah</i> read for <i>Parashat Ki Tetzey</i> has only ten verses.)</i> If he read only ten verses, but the <i>haftarah</i> is translated, it is sufficient even if the concept is not completed.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim explains that after the final verse is translated, one should repeat the verse in its original to conclude with the words of the prophet. Thus, the ten verses, the ten translations, and the repetition of the verse will reach the sum of twenty-one.</i><br>[When reading] from the prophets, one reads and even two may translate.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Though Halachah 11 prohibits two people to serve as translators for the Torah simultaneously, this restriction is not enforced regarding the <i>haftarah</i>. Rashi, <i>Megillah</i> 21b, explains that the prohibition was instituted to prevent confusion. However, since the haftarot are concerned more with ethics than halachic concepts, the Sages did worry that much about the possibility of confusion arising.</i> One may skip from one concept to another.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the same principle is involved. Though skipping in this manner in a Torah reading is forbidden (Halachah 8), there is no such restriction for the <i>haftarah</i>. Indeed, in many of the haftarot read at present, it is customary to skip certain verses.</i> However, one should not skip from one prophet to another,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">for this would be too confusing (<i>Megillah</i> 24a).</i> except among the twelve prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">for they are considered to be a single book.</i> Furthermore, [even within a book from a single prophet,] one should not skip from the conclusion of the book until its beginning.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">for it is improper to reverse the order of the verses as they are mentioned in the Bible.</i> Whenever one skips, one should not wait longer than it takes the translator to complete his translation.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that this is a practice of respect for the congregation, to prevent them from being forced to wait in silence.</i>
A person reading from the prophets may read three verses to the translator at one time,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though verses from the Torah should be read to a translator one at one time (see Halachah 10), as mentioned in the previous halachah, more leniency is taken regarding verses from the <i>haftarah</i>.</i> and the translator translates them one after another. If the three verses are three separate passages,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 24a cites the verses from Isaiah 52:3-5 (included in the <i>haftarah</i> of <i>Parshat Shoftim</i>) as an example of such a phenomenon. [It must be noted that these verses are included in two passages (and not three as stated by the Rambam) in all texts of the Bible. Note the commentary of Rabbenu Nissim, who explains two passages as "two different concepts."]</i> [the reader] should read them to the translator only one at a time.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">to prevent confusion.</i>
The person who reads the <i>haftarah</i> recites one blessing before [beginning his reading]: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who chose prophets....<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The full text of this blessing is found in the Order of Prayers for the Entire Year. This blessing is considered to be one long blessing, which begins with <i>Baruch</i> and concludes with <i>Baruch</i> (Rav David Arameah).</i><br>After [completing the reading], he recites four blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, he recites a total of seven blessings, corresponding to the seven people called to the Torah (<i>Soferim</i> 14:1).</i> He concludes the first blessing:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">which begins: "Blessed are You..."; the entire prayer is considered to be one blessing.</i> "the God who is faithful in all of His words."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Avudraham</i> explains that since the object of most of the prophecies is "Zion, Elijah, and David" (i.e., the Messianic redemption), after praising God as faithful to fulfill His prophecies, we begin the second blessing, which centers on the return to Jerusalem.</i> He concludes the second blessing: "who builds Jerusalem."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as in the grace after meals. This text is also found in the siddurim of Rav Amram Gaon and Rav Sa'adiah Gaon. <i>Soferim</i> 13:12 concludes the blessing "who causes Zion to rejoice in her children." The Ra'avad and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggest concluding the blessing in this fashion. This is the commonly accepted practice today.</i> He concludes the third blessing: "the Shield of David."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 117b compares this to the conclusion of the first blessing of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, "the Shield of Abraham."</i> He concludes the fourth blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">which centers on the Sabbath or festival celebrated</i> with the mention of the sacred aspect of the day, as he does in the<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">fourth blessing of the</i> <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachot 5 and 7.</i> Similarly, if Rosh Chodesh falls on the Sabbath, the one who reads the <i>haftarah</i> mentions Rosh Chodesh in<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">the middle of</i> this blessing, as he does in the<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">middle of the fourth blessing of the <i>Musaf</i></i> <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">However, he does not conclude the blessing with the mention of Rosh Chodesh, as he would in the <i>Musaf</i> prayer. (See Chapter 2, Halachah 11.)<br><i>Shabbat</i> 24b questions whether Rosh Chodesh should be mentioned in this blessing. On one hand, when Rosh Chodesh falls during the week, the <i>haftarah</i> is not read. Hence, one might assume that there is no connection between the two and, hence, there is no need to mention Rosh Chodesh in the blessings. However, on the other hand, we find that when Yom Kippur falls on the Sabbath, the Sabbath is mentioned in the <i>Ne'ilah</i> service, despite the fact that the <i>Ne'ilah</i> service would otherwise not be recited on the Sabbath.<br>Though Rashi and Rav Yitzchok Alfasi interpret the passage in the same manner as the Rambam, Rabbenu Asher and Rabbenu Nissim rule that no mention should be made of Rosh Chodesh in this blessing. The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 284:2 follows the latter position.<br>[The siddurim of Rav Amram Gaon and Rav Sa'adiah Gaon include mention of Rosh Chodesh in the conclusion of the blessing as well. The text of the authoritative Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also accept this view.]</i>
How many readers [are called to the Torah]? On Sabbath morning, seven; on Yom Kippur, six; on festivals, five.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This reflects a descending order of holiness. Working on the Sabbath is punishable by execution; working on Yom Kippur by כרת (premature death by the hand of heaven); and on festivals, certain labors are permitted.</i> This number may not be reduced.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In order to distinguish each day with the measure of holiness which it is due.</i> However, it may be increased.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is quoted from the Mishnah (<i>Megillah</i> 3:2). Rabbenu Nissim explains that the license to increase the number of people called to the Torah applies only on the Sabbath, in order to clearly differentiate between the different holy days. However, Rashi (like the Rambam, here) explains that it refers to all three occasions. Since working at one's occupation is prohibited on all three occasions, there is no difficulty in adding to the number of people called to the Torah.<br>The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 282:1) states that it is customary to follow Rabbenu Nissim's opinion.</i><br>On <i>Rashei Chadashim</i> and on <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, four people [are called to read [from the Torah].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">These days are put in a category of their own, because although they are distinguished by the recitation of the <i>Musaf</i> service, work (albeit with restrictions on <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>) is permitted</i> On the Sabbath and Yom Kippur during the <i>Minchah</i> service, on Mondays and Thursdays throughout the entire year, on Chanukah and Purim in the morning service, and on fast days in the morning and <i>Minchah</i> services<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.</i> three people [are called to] read [from the Torah]. This number<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">three or four, respectively</i> may not be reduced,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">since, as stated in Halachot 1 and 3, a minimum of three people must be called to the Torah, and a distinction must be made between the days when <i>Musaf</i> is recited and when it is not recited.</i> nor may it may be increased.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">On all these occasions, with the exception of the <i>Minchah</i> services of the Sabbath and Yom Kippur, work is permitted, and the Rabbis did not want to have people lose time from work by calling extra people to the Torah. On the Sabbath no additions were made, since it was customary to hold study sessions during the afternoon. Since the reading on Yom Kippur afternoon was instituted as parallel to that of the Sabbath afternoon, no additions are made then.</i>
A woman should not read the Torah publicly, as a token of respect for the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This decision (quoted from <i>Megillah</i> 23) implies that a woman could receive an <i>aliyah</i> and recite the blessings over the Torah. However, as a gesture of respect to the community, women were not given this privilege.<br>Note the comments of the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 282:6, who states that women are obligated to hear the weekly Torah reading.</i> A minor<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a male under thirteen</i> who knows how to read<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">since, as stated below, according to the Rambam, only a person who knows how to read can be called to the Torah.</i> and is aware of the One who is being blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., understands that reciting the blessings and reading the Torah is part of the service of God. See the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Berachot</i> 7:2.</i> may be counted as one of the required number [of people<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There is a question among the commentaries if this refers only to the number of seven readers who must be called to the Torah on the Sabbath, or if this also refers to the three readers whom Ezra established as the minimum required to read from the Torah at all times. Rav Kapach relates that in the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Megillah</i> 4:6), he originally accepted a child as one of the seven, but not as one of the three. However, afterwards, he amended the text so that it appears that he accepts a child even as one of the three.</i> called to the Torah].<br>Similarly, the one who recites the <i>haftarah</i> is counted as one of the required number [of people called to the Torah], because he also reads from the Torah. [However,] if the leader of the congregation interrupted [by reciting] <i>Kaddish</i> between the conclusion of the Torah reading and the reading of the person who recites the <i>haftarah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 20.</i> [the latter] is not included as one of the required number [of people called to the Torah]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">because the <i>Kaddish</i> marks the conclusion of the required Torah reading. In such an instance, since the required number of people were not called to the Torah, the entire Torah reading must be repeated (Rav David Arameah).</i>.<br>If there is only one person in the community who knows how to read [from the Torah], he should be called to the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">However, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> (ibid.) states that a child should be called only for maftir and even that custom is not practiced in most communities at present.<br>Similarly, the one who recites the haftarah is counted as one of the required number [of people called to the Torah], because he also reads from the Torah - although, as mentioned in Halachah 13, he reads a portion that has already been read.</i> read, descend<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">to distinguish between <i>aliyot</i></i> [from the platform],<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah was read on a platform in the center of the synagogue. See Chapter 11, Halachah 3.</i> return and read again a second and a third time until he completes the number of <i>aliyot</i> designated for that day.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The<i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 141) explains that, at present, the custom is to have the <i>chazan</i> read for everyone. If only those who knew how to read from the Torah were given <i>aliyot</i>, two problems would arise:a) Many people who do not know how to read from the Torah would be embarrassed;b) People who do not know how to read correctly would claim that they do know how to read, and arguments might crop up between them and the synagogue officials.<br>Nevertheless, since there is a question whether it is acceptable for a person to recite the blessings when he does not actually read from the Torah, it is desirable that the person who recites the blessings read along with the <i>chazan</i> in an undertone. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 141:2, and commentaries.) Yemenite custom, even at present, is that each person called to the Torah reads himself.</i>
In all of these [Torah] readings, a priest reads first;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though our practice has its origins in the decrees of the Sages as explained below, there is also a Torah command (see <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, Positive Commandment 32) to give precedence to a priest regarding the Torah reading. However, according to the Torah, this commandment applies only when the two are equal in wisdom (as mentioned below).</i> after him, a Levite; and after him, an Israelite.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 5:8 states that this practice was instituted by the Sages to establish peace among the people. Originally, the first <i>aliyah</i> would be given to the sage of greatest stature in the community. However, strife and contention would frequently break out concerning the designation of the person deserving of that honor.</i> It is common custom at present<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">However, in Talmudic times, if a sage was obviously of a higher stature than the priests who were present, he was called first. Thus, <i>Megillah</i> 22a relates that Rav would receive the first <i>aliyah</i> in the presence of Shmuel, although Shmuel was a priest and Rav was not.</i> that even a priest who is a common person is given precedence and allowed to read before a wise man of great stature in Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes:<br>Know that the practice which is widely accepted everywhere, that a priest reads first in the synagogue regardless of whether he is a sage or a commoner, regardless of whether there is someone of greater stature there or not, is a matter which has no basis in the Talmud.... I wonder where this blemish came from....<br>The Rambam continues to explain that even at present, it is proper to call a sage of greater stature to the Torah before a priest of lower stature. Though the Rambam's opinion has been supported by many commentaries, in practice the commonly accepted custom has been allowed to be continued. The reason for this is quite clear. The strife and contention that existed in Talmudic times would surely return (<i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 135).</i><br>Whoever is greater than his colleague in wisdom is given precedence regarding the reading [of the Torah].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 60a states that after the priest and the Levite, the <i>aliyot</i> should be given to "Torah sages who serve as community leaders, the sons of Torah sages who serve as community leaders, the heads of the community, and then all people."</i> The last person who rolls the Torah scroll closed receives a reward equivalent to that of all the others.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 32b states that the <i>gollel</i> - the one who rolls the Torah closed - receives a reward equivalent to that of all the others who read from the Torah.<br>Apparently, the Rambam interprets this statement as referring to the person who receives the final <i>aliyah</i>, or on Sabbath and festivals, the one who reads the <i>haftarah</i>. He should also roll the Torah closed. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (147:5-6) quotes a different view, explaining that the term גולל refers to the person who lifts the Torah up (what we refer to as <i>Magbiah</i>). It is customary that the person who receives this honor need not read from the Torah beforehand.</i> Therefore, even the person of the greatest stature in the community can receive the concluding <i>aliyah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the principles mentioned above, one might think that the sage of greatest stature should receive the third (or the first) <i>aliyah</i>. However, since the reward received by the גולל is greater, the sage may be given this honor.</i>
When there are no priests present,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">or the priest is at a point in prayer where he is forbidden to make an interruption (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 135:5).</i> an Israelite is called to the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the statements of Rav Sa'adiah Gaon, who writes that the honor should be given to an Israelite and should not be given to a Levite. However, the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 135:6) states that the honor may also be given to a Levite.</i> and a Levite should not be called after him at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lest one think that the person called to the Torah before him is a priest (<i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 135).<br>Note the statements of Rav Sa'adiah Gaon, who maintains that in such circumstances, the Levite may be given one of the later <i>aliyot</i>. His opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:10). However, the Ramah maintains that a second priest or Levite should not be given one of the first seven <i>aliyot</i>.<br>When there are no Levites present, the priest who received the first <i>aliyah</i> returns and reads [from the Torah] a second time in place of the Levite. - An Israelite should not be called, for this would create the impression that the first person called to the Torah was not a priest (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 135:28).</i><br>When there are no Levites present, the priest who received the first <i>aliyah</i> returns and reads [from the Torah] a second time in place of the Levite. Another priest should not read [from the Torah] after him, lest others say that there is a blemish in the first's lineage,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">even if his father was known to be a priest, it is possible that his mother was unfit to marry a priest.</i> and, therefore, another priest was given the <i>aliyah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the situation involving two Levites mentioned below, the second priest's lineage would not be doubted, for were he not a priest, he would not be given this <i>aliyah</i> either (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 135:28).<br>Similarly, one Levite should not read [from the Torah] after another Levite, lest others say that there is a blemish in the lineage of one of them - i.e., even if his father was a Levite, it is possible that he married a women who is a ממזרת, and thus their children are also ממזרים (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 135:30).</i><br>Similarly, one Levite should not read [from the Torah] after another Levite, lest others say that there is a blemish in the lineage of one of them.
What is the order [of the service] when the Torah is read after prayer?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Having outlined the halachic principles governing the reading of the Torah, the Rambam turns to explaining the order of the prayer service on the days when the Torah is read. Thus, this and the following two halachot complete the description of the order of communal prayer begun in Chapter 9.</i> On a day when there is a <i>Musaf</i> service,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">but when the <i>haftarah</i> is not recited - i.e., Rosh Chodesh or <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i></i> after the leader of the congregation completes the morning service, he recites <i>Kaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">full <i>Kaddish</i>, to indicate the completion of the morning service</i> and takes out the Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 141:25 states that the Torah scroll should always be taken to the platform on the right side, as the reader faces the platform.</i> He calls the members of the community, one by one, and they ascend<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">to the platform on which the Torah is read. Hence, the name <i>aliyah</i> (ascent) is used to refer to a person called to the Torah.</i> and read from the Torah. When they have completed the reading, he returns the Torah scroll to its place,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in Chapter 9, Halachah 14, here the congregation recite <i>Tehillah l'David</i> and <i>U'va l'Tzion</i>.</i> recites <i>Kaddish</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">half-<i>Kaddish</i>. The Rambam's statements are based on <i>Soferim</i> 21:6. It is our practice to recite <i>Kaddish</i> after the Torah reading and then, again, as an introduction to the <i>Musaf</i> prayers.</i> and then the [congregation] recites the <i>Musaf</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">and then the [congregation] recites the <i>Musaf</i> service.</i><br> On days when the <i>haftarah</i> is read and there is a <i>Musaf</i> service,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., Sabbaths and festivals</i> it is customary to recite <i>Kaddish</i> before the person who reads the <i>haftarah</i> ascends [for his <i>aliyah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the <i>Kaddish</i> differentiates between the Torah reading, which is obligatory, and the portion read by the person who recites the <i>haftarah</i>, which was instituted only as a token of respect for the Torah (<i>Soferim</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).<br>The Rambam does not state whether the Torah scroll should be returned before the morning service or afterwards. In one of his responsa, he writes that there is no advantage to one practice over the other, and everything depends on local custom.</i> There are places where it is customary to recite <i>Kaddish</i> after the person who reads the <i>haftarah</i> [completes his Torah reading].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 17 describes the consequences that result from this difference in custom.</i>
During the <i>Minchah</i> service on the Sabbath and on Yom Kippur, after the leader of the congregation completes <i>Tehillah l'David</i> and the order of <i>Kedushah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., <i>U'va l'Tzion</i>. See Chapter 9, Halachah 13. On Yom Kippur, it is our custom to recite these prayers before the <i>Ne'ilah</i> service, and not before <i>Minchah</i>.</i> he recites the <i>Kaddish</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">a half-<i>Kaddish</i></i> and takes out a Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The congregation should stand while the Torah scroll is being taken from the ark (<i>Kiddushin</i> 33b). <i>Soferim</i> 14:14 states that the congregation should walk after the Torah scroll while it is being taken from the ark to the reading platform and when it is returned.<br>The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 149:1) mentions the custom of training children to kiss the Torah scroll as it is taken out.</i> [Those called to the Torah] ascend and read and then, [the Torah scroll] is returned [to its place.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The congregation should rise and accompany it back to the ark (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 149:1).</i> The leader of the congregation] recites <i>Kaddish</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">half-<i>Kaddish</i></i> and the [congregation] recites the <i>Minchah</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">and the [congregation] recites the <i>Minchah</i> service.</i><br> Similarly, on a fast day,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">after the recitation of<i>Tehillah l'David</i> and half-<i>Kaddish</i></i> the Torah is read [before] the <i>Minchah</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 18.</i> Afterwards, <i>Kaddish</i> is recited,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">a half-<i>Kaddish</i></i> and the <i>Minchah</i> service is recited. On festivals, it is not customary to read [the Torah] in the <i>Minchah</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halachah 1, the Torah reading on Sabbath afternoons was instituted for יושבי קרנות. According to the interpretation that this refers to idle people who sit on the street corners, Rabbenu Manoach explains that this reason would not apply on the festivals, when the people are involved in the preparation of their festive meals. Even according to the interpretation of the term as "shopkeepers," it is possible that in consideration of the time spent preparing the festive meals, the Rabbis did not institute the reading of the Torah in the <i>Minchah</i> service.</i>
[When the Torah is read on] a day when <i>Musaf</i> is not recited,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on Mondays and Thursdays, Purim, Chanukah, and fast days</i> after the morning <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> is completed,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">and the supplicatory prayers have been recited</i> [the leader of the congregation] recites the <i>Kaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">half-<i>Kaddish</i></i> and takes out a Torah scroll. [After the portion is] read from it, [the Torah scroll] is returned [to its place.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Interestingly, this is the custom in some Ashkenazic communities. However, in Sephardic communities, it is customary to return the Torah scroll to the ark after the recitation of the full <i>Kaddish</i> mentioned below (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:13).</i> The leader of the congregation] recites <i>Tehillah l'David</i> and the order of <i>Kedushah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>U'va l'Tzion</i></i> as is the practice every day.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 13.</i> [Afterwards,] he recites the <i>Kaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the full <i>Kaddish</i>, to indicate the completion of the service.</i> and the people depart.
It is not proper to read from <i>chumashim</i> in synagogues, as a token of respect for the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is proper that a community possess a complete Torah scroll. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 3:1) states that this prohibition was instituted so that the community would be upset that they were prevented from hearing the Torah readings, and therefore buy a Torah scroll. (See also Chapter 11, Halachah 1.)</i><br>A Torah scroll should not be rolled [from one portion to another portion] in the presence of the community, because of the difficulty it would cause the people, forcing them to remain standing while the Torah scroll is being rolled.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Yoma</i> 70a, offers another reason: because the congregation is forced to stand idly while the scroll is being rolled.</i> Therefore, if it is necessary to read two separate concepts, two Torah scrolls are taken out.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 18, Halachot 22-24.</i> [However,] one person should not read one concept from two Torah scrolls,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 3:10, which describes the portions read by the High Priest in the Temple on Yom Kippur. He would read one passage by heart, rather than roll the Torah scroll.<br>The <i>Merkevat HaMishneh</i> explains that if the passages read from the two scrolls deal with two different subjects, one person may read from two scrolls. See Chapter 13, Halachah 4.</i> lest people say that the first scroll was invalid and, therefore, they read from the second.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Interestingly, the Rambam does not mention at all the laws which apply when a Torah scroll is found invalid. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 143.)</i>
When a person rolls a Torah scroll [closed],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Apparently, in Talmudic times, one person would both lift the Torah up and roll it closed. (This practice is followed today among Chabad Chassidim.)</i> he should roll it from the outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 147:4) interprets this statement (a quote from <i>Megillah</i> 32b) to mean that when a Torah scroll is rolled closed, the blank side of the parchment should face the outside, and the writing should face the person holding the Torah scroll.</i> When he ties it, he should tie it from the inside.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Megillah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that if the knot is on the outside, when the Torah scroll is opened it would have to be turned over to be untied.</i> He should leave the stitching<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">between the different columns of the Torah scroll</i> [in the center], so that it will not rip.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if the Torah scroll is torn because one person pulled one <i>etz chayim</i> (one of the rods on which the Torah scroll is mounted) from the other, the scroll will tear on the stitching and not on the text.</i><br>In a place where a Torah scroll is taken [from the synagogue] to another room where it is kept, the congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 149:1) interprets the following prohibition to apply only to the community as a whole. One or two individuals are allowed to leave.</i> is not allowed to leave until the Torah scroll is taken.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur, Orach Chayim</i> 149, interprets <i>Sotah</i> 39b, the source for this prohibition, as forbidding one to leave from the same exit through which the Torah will be taken, but allowing one to leave through another exit, because it is improper for a person to walk in front of the Torah scroll.<br>The Rambam does not allow this leniency, since abandoning the Torah does not show respect. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 149:1) quotes the <i>Tur's</i> opinion.</i> They should accompany it, following it to the place where it is kept.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 39b derives this law from Deuteronomy 13:5: "Follow after God, your Lord." The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that similar practices should be followed when the Torah is kept in the ark, as is customary today. (See also the commentary on Halachah 21.)</i>

Chapter 13

The common custom throughout all Israel is to complete the [reading of] the Torah in one year. [The cycle] is begun on the Sabbath after the Sukkot festival, reading the <i>sidrah, Bereshit</i>. On the following Sabbath, [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Eleh toldot</i> [is read]; on the third, [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Vayomer Ado-nai el Avram</i>. We continue reading according to this order until the Torah is completed, during the Sukkot festival.<br>There are those who finish the Torah reading in a three-year cycle. However, this is not a widely accepted custom.
Ezra instituted the practice of having the Jews read the "curses" found in the Book of Leviticus before Shavuot, and those found in the Book of Deuteronomy before Rosh HaShanah.<br>It is common custom to read [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Bemidbar Sinai</i> before Shavuot, [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Va'etchanan</i> after Tish'ah B'Av, [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Attem nitzavim</i> before Rosh HaShanah, and [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Tzav et Aharon</i> before Pesach in an ordinary year.<br>Therefore, there are Sabbaths on which two <i>sedarim</i> are read: for example, [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Ishah ki tazria</i> and [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Vezot tih'yeh torat hametzora</i> [are often combined. Similarly, the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Im bechukotai</i> [is often combined] with [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Behar Sinai</i>, so that [the reading of the Torah] will be completed in a year, and the <i>sedarim</i> will be read at the appropriate times.
At the point [in the Torah] where the Sabbath morning [reading] was completed, the reading [is begun] on the Sabbath afternoon, on Monday, on Thursday, and on the following Sabbath.<br>What is implied? On the first Sabbath, we read [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Bereshit</i> in the morning. In the afternoon, ten or more verses from [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Eleh toldot Noach</i> are read. The same practice [is followed] on Monday and Thursday. On the coming Sabbath, we begin from <i>Eleh toldot Noach</i>, and read until the conclusion of the <i>sidrah</i>. This pattern is followed throughout the year.<br>On each Sabbath, a <i>haftarah</i> is recited that reflects the Torah reading.
On Rosh Chodesh, the first reader reads three verses from the passage (Numbers 28:1-15) <i>Tzav</i>. The second reader reads the third verse which was read by the first reader, and the following two verses, so that three verses will remain in the passage. The third reader reads the three verses that were left [unread] by the second reader, and the passage "And on the Sabbath day...." The fourth reader reads [the passage] "And on your new months...."<br>If Rosh Chodesh falls on the Sabbath, two Torah scrolls are taken out in the morning. The <i>sidrah</i> of that particular Sabbath is read from one, and the person who concludes the reading reads [the passage] "And on your new months...."<br>The person who reads the <i>haftarah</i> reads the passage concerning Rosh Chodesh, and then reads [the passage (Isaiah 66:1-24) that concludes:] "And it will be from month to month..." as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>If Rosh Chodesh Av falls on the Sabbath [the passage, Isaiah 1:14-31, beginning:] "My soul hates your new moons and your festivals" is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>If Rosh Chodesh falls on Sunday, on the preceding Sabbath [the passage (I Samuel 20:18-42), beginning:] "And Jonathan told him: 'Tomorrow is the new month...'” is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.
Whoever is called to read from the Torah should begin [his reading] with a positive matter and conclude with a positive matter.<br>However, in <i>Parashat Ha'azinu</i>, the first [person called to the Torah] reads until <i>Z'chor y'mot olam</i> (Deuteronomy 32:7 . The second begins from <i>Z'chor y'mot olam</i> [and continues] until <i>Yarkivehu</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:13). The third [reads] from <i>Yarkivehu</i> until <i>Vayar Ado-nai vayin'atz</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:19). The fourth [reads] from <i>Vayar Ado-nai vayin'atz</i> until <i>Lu chachmu</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:29). The fifth [reads] from <i>Lu chachmu</i> until <i>Ki essa el shamayim yadi</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:40). The sixth [reads] from <i>Ki essa el shamayim yadi</i> until the conclusion of the song (<i>ibid.</i>:43).<br>Why is the Torah reading ceased at these points? Because these are [verses of] rebuke, [and the intent is that] that they motivate the people to repent.
The eight verses at the conclusion of the Torah may be read in a synagogue when fewer than ten people are present. They are indeed all Torah and were related by Moses from the Almighty. However, since, on the surface, they appear to have been recited after Moses' death, the [rules governing them] are different. Therefore, it is permissible for an individual to read them.
The "curses" in Leviticus should not be interrupted. Rather, a single person should read them [in their entirety]. He should begin with the verse preceding them and conclude with the verse following them.<br>The "curses" in Deuteronomy may be interrupted if one desires. However, the people have already adopted the custom of not interrupting [this reading]. Rather, a single person reads them [in their entirety].
[The cycle of Torah readings] is interrupted for the festivals and Yom Kippur. [On these occasions,] we read [a passage that] concerns the festival and not the <i>sidrah</i> of [that] Sabbath.<br>Moses instituted [the practice that], on each festival, the Jews should read [a passage] appropriate to it. Also, it [is proper] on each festival to ask about and explain the subjects [pertinent] to that festival.<br>Which [passages] are read? On Pesach, [we read] the passage concerning the festivals in Leviticus  23:4-44). [However,] the people have already adopted the custom of reading <i>Mishchu uk'chu lachem</i> (Exodus 12:21-51) on the first day. The <i>haftarah</i> is [the description] of the Pesach celebrated in Gilgal (Joshua 5:2-15).<br>On the second day, we read <i>Shor o kesev</i> (Leviticus 22:26-23:44). The <i>haftarah</i> is [the description] of the Pesach celebrated by Josaiah (II Kings 1-9, 21-25). On the third day, we read <i>Kadesh li kol b'chor</i> (Exodus 13:1-16); on the fourth day, <i>Im kessef talveh</i> (Exodus 22:24-23:19); on the fifth day, <i>P'sol lecha</i> (Exodus 34:1-26); on the sixth day, <i>Vaya'asu Bnei Yisrael et hapesach b'mo'ado</i> (Numbers 9:1-14).<br>On the final festival, [we read] from <i>Vay'hi beshalach</i> until the conclusion of the song [sung at Red Sea] until [the verse,] <i>Ani Ado-nai rof'echa</i> (Exodus 13:17-15:26). The <i>haftarah</i> is <i>Vayedaber David</i> (II Samuel 22:1-51).<br>On the eighth day, [we read], <i>Kol hab'chor</i> (Deuteronomy 15:19-16:17). The <i>haftarah</i> is <i>Od hayom</i> (Isaiah 10:32-4, 11:1-16, 12:1-6).
On Shavuot, we read [the passage, containing the reading] <i>Shiv'ah shavuot</i> (Deuteronomy 16:9 . However, it is common custom to read [the passage,] <i>Bachodesh hash'lishi</i> (Exodus 19:1-20:23) on the first day of the festival. [The vision of God's] chariot (Ezekiel 1:1-28) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>On the second day, the passage describing the festivals, <i>Kol hab'chor</i> (Deuteronomy 15:19-16:17) is read, and [a passage from] Chabbakuk (3:1-19) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.
On Rosh HaShanah, we read [the passage including the verse]: <i>Uvachodesh hash'vi'i b'echad lachodesh</i> (Numbers 29:1 . However, it is common custom to read [the passage,] <i>VAdo-nai pakad et Sarah</i> (Genesis 21:1-33). [The passage,] <i>Vay'hi ish echad min haramatayim</i> (I Samuel 1:1-2:10) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>On the second day, [the passage,] <i>V'HaElo-him nisah et Avraham</i> (Genesis 22:1-24) is read, and [the passage including the verse] <i>Haven yakir li Efrayim</i> (Jeremiah 31:1-19) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.
On Yom Kippur, in the morning, we read [the passage,] <i>Acharei mot</i> (Leviticus 16:1-34) and read [the passage,] <i>Ki koh amar ram v'nisa</i> (Isaiah 57:14-58:14) as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>In the afternoon, [we read the passage] in <i>Acharei mot</i> that is concerned with forbidden sexual relations, in order that anyone who has violated one of these sins will remember, become embarrassed, and repent. The third person [who] reads from the Torah recites [the Book of] Yonah as the <i>haftarah</i>.
On Sukkot, on the first two days, we read the passage that concerns the festivals: <i>Shor o kesev o eyz</i> (Leviticus 22:26-23:44). The <i>haftarah</i> read on the first day is [the passage,] <i>Hiney yom ba l'Ado-nai</i> (Zechariah 14). On the second day, [the <i>haftarah</i> is the passage,] <i>Vayikahalu el hamelech Shlomo</i> (I Kings 8:2-21).<br>On the final day of the festival, we read [the passage,] <i>Kol hab'chor</i> (Deuteronomy 15:19-16:17). For the <i>haftarah</i>, we read [the passage,] <i>Vay'hi k'chalot Shlomo</i> (I Kings 8:54-66).<br>On the following day, we read [the <i>sidrah,</i>] <i>Vezot haberachah</i> (Deuteronomy 33-34). For the <i>haftarah</i>, we read [the passage,] <i>Vaya'amod Shlomo</i> (I Kings 8:22-53). There are those who read [the passage,] <i>Vay'hi acharei mot Moshe</i> (Joshua 1) as the <i>haftarah</i>.<br>On the other days of Sukkot, we read [the passages that describe] the sacrifices [offered] on the festival.
What is implied? On each of the days of <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, we read two passages. [For example,] on the third day [of the festival], which is [the first day of] <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, the priest reads [the passage,] <i>Uvayom hasheni</i>. The Levite reads [the passage,] <i>Uvayom hash'lishi</i>. The Israelite repeats [the passage,] <i>Uvayom hash'lishi,</i> and the person called for the fourth<i>aliyah</i> repeats [both passages:] <i>Uvayom hasheni</i> and <i>Uvayom hash'lishi</i>.<br>Similarly, on the fourth day [of the festival], which is the second day of <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, we read the passages <i>Uvayom hash'lishi</i> and <i>Uvayom harevi'i</i>. The same pattern is followed on all the [other] days.
In the morning [service] on each and every one of the festivals, on Yom Kippur, and during the seven days of Pesach, two [Torah] scrolls are taken out. We read the passages mentioned above from the first scroll, and from the second scroll we read the description of the sacrifices [offered on] that day, in the Book of Numbers. The person who reads the description of the sacrifices recites the <i>haftarah</i> from the prophets.
On any day when two or three [Torah] scrolls are taken out: if they are taken out one after the other, when the first scroll is returned, <i>Kaddish</i> is recited and the second scroll is taken out. When the second scroll is returned, <i>Kaddish</i> is also recited.<br>We have mentioned above that the common custom is to recite <i>Kaddish</i> after the reader concludes the reading at all times, and then to recite the <i>haftarah</i> from the prophets.
When the Sabbath falls during <i>Chol Hamo'ed</i> - whether during Pesach or during Sukkot - [the passage,] <i>R'ey Attah omer elai</i> (Exodus 33:12-34:26) is read on that Sabbath. On Pesach, [the passage describing Ezekiel's vision of] the dry bones (Chapter 37) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>. When [the Sabbath] falls in the midst of Sukkot, [the passage,] <i>B'yom bo Gog</i> (Ezekiel 38:18-39:16) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>.
On Chanukah, [the following passages are read:] On the first day, we read from the Priestly Blessing (Numbers 6:38-42) until the conclusion of the sacrifice offered on the first day (Numbers 7:17 . On the second day, we read [the passage describing] the sacrifices of the <i>Nasi</i> who brought the offering on the second day. This practice is continued until the eighth day. On the eighth day, we read [the descriptions of] all the [remaining] sacrifices until the end of the portion.<br>On the Sabbath of Chanukah, the <i>haftarah</i> is Zechariah's [vision of the Menorah (2:14-4:7)]. If two Sabbaths are celebrated during Chanukah, on the first Sabbath, Zechariah's [vision of the Menorah] is read as the <i>haftarah</i>; on the second, [the description of] Solomon's [Menorah is read as the <i>haftarah</i> (I Kings 7:40-50)]. The one who reads the [passage designated for] Chanukah is the one who recites the <i>haftarah</i> from the prophets.<br>On Purim, [the passage,] <i>Vayavo Amalek</i> (Exodus 17:8-16) [is read].
On Tish'ah B'Av, in the morning, [the passage,] <i>Ki tolid banim</i> (Deuteronomy 4:25-40) is read, and [the passage,] <i>Asof asifem, n'um Ado-nai</i> (Jeremiah 8:13-9:23) is read as the <i>haftarah</i>. During the <i>Minchah</i> service, we read [the passage,] <i>Vay'chal Moshe</i> (Exodus 32:11-14, 34:1-10), as on other fast days.<br>On the other days when we fast [to commemorate the bitter events] that occurred to our ancestors, we read the [above-mentioned passage,] in the morning and <i>Minchah</i> services [in the following manner]: The first person called to the Torah reads four verses, [beginning] <i>Vay'chal Moshe</i>. The second and the third read from <i>P'sol lecha</i> until <i>asher ani oseh imach</i>.<br>On the fasts that are declared by the community because of difficulties like famine or plague, we read blessings and curses, so that the people will repent and humble their hearts when they hear them.
It is customary on the three Sabbaths before Tish'ah B'Av to read haftarot of rebuke. On the first Sabbath, we read [the passage,] <i>Divrei Yirmiyahu</i> (Jeremiah 1:1-2:3) as the <i>haftarah</i>. On the second [Sabbath], we read [the passage,] <i>Chazon Yishayahu</i> (Isaiah 1:1-27). On the third [Sabbath], we read [the passage,] <i>Eichah hay'ta l'zonah</i> (Isaiah 1:21 .<br>Similarly, on the Sabbath after Tish'ah B'Av we read [the passage <i>Nachamu, nachamu, ami</i> (Isaiah 40:1-26) as the <i>haftarah</i>. It is the common custom in our cities to read the comforting prophecies of Isaiah as the haftarot from Tish'ah B'Av until Rosh HaShanah.<br>On the Sabbath between Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur, we read [the passage,] <i>Shuvah Yisrael</i> as the <i>haftarah</i>.
When Rosh Chodesh Adar falls on the Sabbath, we read <i>Parashat Shekalim</i> (Exodus 30:11-16), and read as the <i>haftarah</i> [the passage concerning] Yehoyada, the priest (II Kings 11:17-20, 12:1-17). If Rosh Chodesh Adar falls in the middle of the week - even if it falls on Friday - <i>Parashat Shekalim</i> is read on the previous Sabbath.<br>On the "second Sabbath," we read <i>Parashat Zachor</i> (Deuteronomy 25:17-19), and read as the <i>haftarah</i>, [the passage,] <i>Pakad'ti et asher asah Amalek</i> (I Samuel 15:1-34). What is meant by the "second Sabbath"? The Sabbath before the week in which Purim falls - even if Purim falls on Friday.<br>On the "third Sabbath," we read [the passage describing] "the red heifer" (Numbers 19:1-22), and read as the <i>haftarah</i> [the passage,] <i>V'zarakti aleichem</i> (Ezekiel 36:16-38). Which is the "third Sabbath"? The one preceding the fourth.<br>On the fourth Sabbath, we read [the passage,] <i>Hachodesh hazeh lachem</i> (Exodus 12:1-20), and read as the <i>haftarah</i> [the passage,] <i>Barishon b'echad lachodesh</i> (Ezekiel 45:16-25, 46:1-18). Which is the "fourth Sabbath"? The Sabbath of the week when Rosh Chodesh Nisan falls - even if it falls on Friday.
Thus, there will be times when there is an interruption between the first [of these] Sabbaths and the second, or between the second and the third. At times, there will be two interruptions - between the first and the second and between the second and third. However, an interruption is never made between the third and fourth [Sabbaths].
Each one of these four passages should be read from another Torah scroll, after reading the <i>sidrah</i> of that Sabbath from the scroll that was taken out first.<br>If Rosh Chodesh Adar fell on the Sabbath and the <i>sidrah</i> to be read that week was <i>V'attah tetzaveh</i>, six people read from <i>V'attah tetzaveh</i> until <i>V'asita kiyor nechoshet</i>.The seventh person reads from the second scroll and repeats the reading of <i>Ki tissa</i> until <i>V'asita kiyor nechoshet.<br></i><br>If the <i>sidrah</i> to be read that week was <i>Ki tissa</i> itself, six people read from <i>Ki tissa</i> until <i>Vayakhel</i>. The seventh person reads from the second scroll and repeats the reading of <i>Ki tissa</i> until <i>V'asita kiyor nechoshet</i>.
[When] Rosh Chodesh Adar falls on the Sabbath, three Torah scrolls are taken out. The <i>sidrah</i> of the day is read from the first scroll. The passage concerning Rosh Chodesh is read from the second scroll, and <i>Ki tissa</i> is read from the third scroll. Similarly, [when] Rosh Chodesh Nisan falls on the Sabbath, three Torah scrolls are taken out. The <i>sidrah</i> of the day is read from the first scroll, the passage concerning Rosh Chodesh is read from the second scroll, and <i>Hachodesh hazeh</i> is read from the third scroll.
[Similarly, when] Rosh Chodesh Tevet falls on the Sabbath, three Torah scrolls are taken out. The <i>sidrah</i> of the day is read from the first scroll. The passage concerning Rosh Chodesh is read from the second scroll, and the passage for Chanukah is read from the third scroll.<br>[When Rosh Chodesh Tevet] falls during the middle of the week, three people read from the passage concerning Rosh Chodesh, and the fourth person reads the passage for Chanukah.
Although a person hears the entire Torah [portion] each Sabbath [when it is read] communally, he is obligated to study on his own each week the <i>sidrah</i> of that week, reading it twice in the original and once in the Aramaic translation. [When] there is no Aramaic translation for a verse, one should read the verse three times in the original, so that one completes [the study of] one's [Torah] portions with the community.

Chapter 14

In the morning, <i>Musaf</i>, and <i>Ne'ilah</i> services,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 26), the Rambam writes that the mitzvah is for the priests to bless the people "every day." The Sages extended the scope of the requirement and obligated the priests to bless the people in all the above services (<i>Ta'anit</i> 26b).<br>The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:44) writes:<br>It is customary in these countries to recite the priestly blessing only on holidays, when people are in festive and joyous spirits...In contrast, on other days - even on Sabbaths - [the priests] are disturbed, worrying about earning a livelihood and the delay of work.Even on holidays, the priestly blessing is recited only in the <i>Musaf</i> service, directly before the people leave the synagogue and rejoice in the holiday festivities.<br>The Ramah's decision is based on the view that the priestly blessings must be recited with feelings of joy and goodwill, and if those feelings cannot be aroused (see <i>Zohar</i> III 147a), it is proper that the blessing not be recited. Though the Ashkenazic community follows his view, in Egypt and in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> the priests have always fulfilled the mitzvah of blessing the people every day. Many Ashkenazic authorities, among them Rav Shneur Zalman of Liadi and Rav Shlomo Kluger, desired to introduce this practice in the Ashkenazic community. However, they were not successful in doing so.</i> the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> states, in the name of the <i>Sefer HaCharedim</i>, that just as it is a mitzvah for the priests to bless the people, it is a mitzvah for the people to be blessed. The <i>Hafla'ah</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 24b) draws a parallel to the mitzvah of <i>Yibbum</i> which is incumbent on both the man and woman involved.</i> recite<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 278) include this as one of the Torah's 613 mitzvot.</i> the priestly blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of <i>Nesiat Kapayim</i> as "recite the priestly blessing" is not literal. The words actually mean "raise their hands," referring to the way the priests hold their hands while reciting these blessings, as mentioned in Halachah 3.</i> They do not recite the priestly blessing in the <i>Minchah</i> service, because, by the time of the <i>Minchah</i> service, all the people have eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Here, we see an example of how a Rabbinic decree can prevent the fulfillment of a Torah commandment.</i> The possibility exists that the priests would have drunken wine, and it is forbidden to recite the priestly blessing while intoxicated.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Deuteronomy 10:8 describes how God designated the tribe of Levi "to stand before God, to serve Him and offer blessing in His name," establishing a equation between service in the Temple and the recitation of the priestly blessing. Accordingly, just as a priest is forbidden to serve in the Temple while intoxicated (Leviticus 10:9), he is also forbidden to recite the priestly blessings in such a state (<i>Ta'anit</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i><br>Even on a fast day,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">when there is no suspicion that the priests are drunk</i> the priestly blessings are not recited during the <i>Minchah</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah for clarification.</i> This decree [was instituted,] lest [people fail to differentiate between] the <i>Minchah</i> service of a fast day and the <i>Minchah</i> service of an ordinary day.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ta'anit</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) records a difference of opinion on this question among the Sages. All the Sages agree that the priestly blessing is not recited during an ordinary <i>Minchah</i>. However, Rabbi Meir requires the priestly blessing to be recited on a fast day. On the other hand, Rabbi Yosse follows the view quoted by the Rambam. His position is accepted by the other Halachic authorities as well.</i>
When does the above apply? On fast days when both <i>Minchah</i> and <i>Ne'ilah</i> are recited - i.e., Yom Kippur and communal fasts [declared because of distress].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ta'anit</i> 1:4-7 and <i>Hilchot Ta'aniot</i>, Chapter 2, which describe the situations which warrant the declaration of a communal fast.</i> However, on a fast day on which <i>Ne'ilah</i> is not recited<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., Tish'ah B'Av or the seventeenth of Tammuz - i.e., fasts instituted to commemorate tragic events in our national history. These days are associated primarily with mourning. In contrast, the other fasts are days when we increase our supplication in an effort to evoke Divine mercy. For this reason, the Sages instituted the <i>Ne'ilah</i> prayer on these days alone (<i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 579).</i> - e.g., Tish'ah B'Av or the seventeenth of Tammuz - since the <i>Minchah</i> service is recited close to sunset, it resembles <i>Ne'ilah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 6, which states: "The proper time of the <i>Ne'ilah</i> prayer is such that one completes it close to sunset."</i> and will not be confused with an ordinary <i>Minchah</i> service.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">which is generally recited in the early afternoon, to afford people the opportunity to eat afterwards, since it is forbidden to eat a meal in the afternoon before reciting <i>Minchah</i> (<i>Tosafot, Ta'anit</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> Therefore, the priestly blessing is recited during it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, in Ashkenazic communities, when the priests do not recite the priestly blessings every day, the <i>chazan</i> should recite the blessing in his repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>.</i><br>If a priest transgressed and ascended to the platform during the <i>Minchah</i> service of Yom Kippur, since it is known that there is no possibility of drunkenness on that day, he may recite the priestly blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Because of this decision, in Ashkenazic communities the <i>chazan</i> recites the priestly blessings in his repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> during these days (<i>Hagahot Ma'amoniot</i>).</i> and he is not required to descend because of the suspicion [that might be aroused], so that people do not say, "He is of blemished lineage. Therefore, they forced him to descend."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is feared that the people will suspect that his mother was a divorcee, and he is thus not able to serve as a priest.</i>
How is the priestly blessing recited outside the Temple?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., in the synagogue prayer services. The recitation of the priestly blessing in the Temple is discussed in Halachah 9.</i> When the leader of the congregation reaches the blessing <i>R'tzey</i>, when he recites the word <i>R'tzey</i> all the priests in the synagogue leave their places,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 38b states: "Any priest who does not ascend during the blessing <i>R'tzey</i> may not ascend afterwards. Later, the Talmud qualifies this statement to mean that a priest must leave his place in the synagogue during the blessing <i>R'tzey</i>.</i> proceed forward,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">reciting the short prayer mentioned in Halachah 12.</i> and ascend the <i>duchan</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>duchan</i> refers to the steps before the <i>heichal</i>. (See the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Berachot</i> 5:4.) This term has become associated with the recitation of the priestly blessing to the extent that reciting the blessing is often popularly referred to as <i>duchaning</i>.<br>In a synagogue where there are no steps before the ark, the priests still recite the blessings while standing before the ark.</i><br>They stand there, facing the <i>heichal</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the permanent ark; Chapter 11, Halachah 2.</i> with their backs to the congregation. They hold their fingers closed, against their palms,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> explains that the priests are not required to close their hands. The Rambam is merely clarifying that, at this point, they are not obligated to spread their hands, as they do when they bless the people.</i> until the leader of the congregation completes the blessing <i>Modim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The priestly blessing is recited at this point in the repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, as the preface to the blessing praising God for granting peace to our people.</i> [Then,]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">they recite the blessing mentioned in Halachah 12, and</i> they turn<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">turning to the right (see Halachah 13).</i> their faces to the people,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">so the blessing will be recited face to face, as required by Halachah 11.</i> spread out their fingers,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">On the verse (Song of Songs 2:9), "peeking through the windows," <i>Shir HaShirim Rabbah</i> comments that the Divine Presence peeks through the windows between the priests' fingers.
