In particular, the Sages note that the word "the windows" (החרכים) can be broken up as follows: ה חרכים - "five windows," alluding to the unique manner in which the priests hold their hands while reciting the priestly blessing.</i> lift up their hands<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 38a derives the obligation of the priests to raise their hands from Leviticus 9:22: "Aharon lifted his hands toward the people and blessed them."</i> shoulder high,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Compare to the practice in the Temple mentioned in Halachah 9.</i> and begin reciting, <i>Y'varechecha</i>....<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This statement has raised questions among the commentaries. The <i>Kiryat Sefer</i> explains that the priests recite <i>Y'varechecha</i> - the first word of the priestly blessings - directly after concluding the blessing (see Halachah 12) recited before blessing the people, without being prompted by the reader. They take the initiative, so that an interruption is not made between the recitation of the blessing before the performance of a mitzvah and its actual performance.<br>Study of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) shows that the Rambam himself had questions about this matter. In the first manuscripts, the Rambam writes that "the leader of the congregation recites <i>Y'varechecha</i>, and the priests read after him." This is also the reading in the popularly published edition of that text. However, in the later manuscripts of the Commentary on the Mishnah (see Rav Kapach's edition of that text), the Rambam changes his mind and states that the priests begin reciting <i>y'varechecha</i>.<br>In his <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128), Rav Yosef Karo mentions these different views, and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (128:13) rules that the priests begin on their own initiative. The Ramah differs and states that it is Ashkenazic practice that this word is also recited in response to the <i>chazan</i>.</i><br>The leader of the congregation reads [the blessing] to them, word for word, and they respond after him [as can be inferred<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Sifre</i> on the verse below.</i> from Numbers 6:23: "This is how you should bless the children of Israel:] 'Say to them...;’” [i.e., the priests do not bless until one] "says to<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the priests merely repeat the blessing emphasizes the concept (Chapter 14, Halachah 7) that the blessing is God's, and the priests do no more than convey that blessing to the people (<i>Kinat Eliyahu</i>).<br>When [the priests] conclude the first verse, all the people answer "Amen." - The <i>Kiryat Sefer</i> states that responding "Amen" to the priestly blessings is an obligation from the Torah.<br>Note the difference between this law and the ruling when the <i>chazan</i> recites the priestly blessings in the absence of any priests (Chapter 15, Halachah 10). Note also the contrast to the people's response to the priestly blessings in the Temple, (Halachah 9).<br><b>The leader of the congregation reads [the priests] the second verse, word for word, and they respond after him until they complete the second verse. -</b> The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>:45) mentions the custom of the priests chanting during the recitation of the blessings. This practice is followed only on holidays. In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the priests do not chant when the priestly blessing is recited on other days.<br><b>The people respond "Amen." The same applies regarding the third verse. -</b> <i>Berachot</i> 55b states that a person who has a dream which requires explanation should stand before the priests when they bless the people. Accordingly, it is customary to recite a prayer regarding dreams during the priestly blessing. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> <i>loc. cit.</i>; 130:1 and commentaries.)</i> them."<br>When [the priests] conclude the first verse, all the people answer "Amen." The leader of the congregation reads [the priests] the second verse, word for word, and they respond after him until they complete the second verse. The people respond "Amen." The same applies regarding the third verse.
When the priests conclude the recitation of [these] three verses, the leader of the congregation begins the final blessing of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh, Sim shalom</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">After the priests promise that God will bless the people, it is appropriate that the <i>chazan</i> begin the blessing requesting peace, alluding to Psalms 29:11: "God will bless His people with peace" (<i>Megillah</i> 18a).</i> The priests turn<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">to the right (see Halachah 13), turning</i> their faces to the ark<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6.</i> and close their fingers.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The priests are not allowed to close their fingers until they turn to face the ark (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 128:16). At this time, they recite a short prayer, as mentioned in Halachah 12.</i> They remain standing on the <i>duchan</i> until the leader of the congregation concludes the blessing, [and then] return to their places.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6.</i>
The person who calls the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 3, based on Numbers 6:23: "This is how you should bless the children of Israel: 'Say to them...,'” our Sages explained that before the priests bless the people, someone must "Say to them" - i.e., invite them to recite the blessing.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128) states that the obligation to bless the people is not incumbent upon the priests until someone requests that they do so.</i> is not permitted to call to the priests until the Amen of the community is no longer heard.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the standard printed text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, which reads לכהנים. Other texts read כהנים. This version translates as "The person who calls the priests is not permitted to call out, '<i>Kohanim</i>'..."<br>These textual differences reflect a difference of interpretation. The standard text relates that the <i>chazan</i> should not begin reading the blessing to the priests until one no longer hears the Amen which the congregation recited in response to the blessing recited by the priests (Halachah 12) before blessing the people.<br>According to the texts which read כהנים, the halachah teaches us that the <i>chazan</i> should not call the priests until the Amen recited by the congregation in response to the blessing <i>Modim</i> can no longer be heard. Both opinions are accepted as halachah by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and commentaries (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:18).</i> The priests are not permitted to begin reciting the blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">either the blessing recited before blessing the people, or the priestly blessing itself, depending on the above interpretations.</i> until the statement of the person who calls the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">either <i>Y'varechecha</i> or <i>Kohanim</i>, according to the respective interpretations.</i> is no longer heard.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In order that each of the statements and blessings can be clearly heard.<br>The congregation should not respond "Amen" until the blessing of the priests - This refers to the blessing recited before blessing the people, and also each of the priestly blessings.</i><br>The congregation should not respond "Amen" until the blessing of the priests is no longer heard. The priests may not begin another<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">one of the three priestly...</i> blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">s - until the Amen of the community - recited in response to the previous blessing...</i> until the Amen of the community is no longer heard.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">However, if a few individuals extend their pronunciation of "Amen" exceedingly, the recitation of the priestly blessings need not be delayed.</i><br>The leader of the congregation is not allowed to recite Amen to the priests' blessings like the rest of the people, lest he become confused and not realize which blessing to recite to them, whether the second blessing or the third blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The recitation of "Amen" is not considered to be an interruption of his recitation of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. Nevertheless, he should not recite "Amen," because he may become confused and begin reading the wrong blessing to the priests.<br>Note that in Chapter 15, Halachah 10, the Rambam writes that if the <i>chazan</i> is the only priest present in a community, he should not bless the people, lest he become confused and err in his prayers. However, if he is sure that he will not make a mistake, he may bless the people. In the present halachah, the Rambam does not make such an allowance.<br>Perhaps the difference is that in Chapter 15, there is the possibility that the recitation of the priestly blessing will be nullified entirely. Hence, greater leniency is shown. In this halachah, the congregation as a whole will not suffer if the <i>chazan</i> does not respond "Amen" to the priests' blessings. Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i> 128:19) quotes the Rambam's decision, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (128:31) and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (128:71) state that particularly at present, when the blessings are recited from a siddur, the <i>chazan</i> should respond Amen.</i>
The priests are not permitted to turn their faces away from the congregation until the leader of the congregation begins [the blessing] <i>Sim shalom</i>. Neither may the priests leave their places<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in front of the <i>heichal<br></i><br>until the leader of the congregation concludes [the blessing] Sim shalom - nor should the priests speak among themselves.</i> until the leader of the congregation concludes [the blessing] <i>Sim shalom</i>, nor may they close their fingers<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">from the outstretched position in which they are held while the priestly blessings are being recited.</i> until they turn their faces from the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">All these three statements are quoted from <i>Sotah</i> 39b. However, the Rambam changes from the order in which these statements are found in the Talmud. Interestingly, the three statements are also quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:16) in still a different order.</i><br>One of the measures ordained by Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This version is found in the standard printed text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The commentaries maintain that the text is in error, noting that <i>Sotah</i> 40a ascribes this decree to Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai.</i> is that the priests should not ascend to the <i>duchan</i> wearing sandals.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This decree was instituted lest the sandal strap of one of the priests snap and he descend to fix it. Someone watching them might think that he was forced to descend because someone exposed a blemish in his lineage that prevented him from serving as a priest (<i>Sotah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> Rather, they should stand barefoot.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is not included in <i>Sotah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), and appears to have been added to negate the view of certain authorities, who allow a priest to recite the priestly blessings wearing boots. Nevertheless, even the Rambam allows the priests to wear socks (Rav Kapach). (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>loc. cit.</i> and 128:5.)</i>
When the priests bless the people, they should not look at them<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">individually. A priest must concentrate his thoughts on blessing the people. Looking at any individual (or group) face to face might cause him to divert his thoughts from that intent.</i> or divert their attention.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">by thinking of other matters. See also <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:21), which states that the priests should not sing different chants while reciting the blessings for this same reason.</i> Rather, their eyes should be directed towards the earth like one standing in prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 4.<br>The <i>Levush</i> develops this concept, explaining that implicit in the priests' recitation of the blessing is the prayer that God will truly bless the people.</i><br>A person should not look at the priests' faces while they are blessing the people, lest they divert their attention.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements are based on the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Megillah</i> 4:8. The Babylonian Talmud (<i>Chaggigah</i> 16a) states that a person who looks at the priests while they are reciting the priestly blessing in the Temple will lose his eyesight from gazing at the Divine Presence, which rests between the priests' fingers.</i> Rather, all the people should listen attentively to the blessing;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, while the priests are reciting the blessing, the people should not recite any Biblical verses or prayers (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i> 128:25). See <i>Sotah</i> 40a, which states, "Is there a servant who will not listen while he is being blessed?" Nevertheless, the Ramah allows verses to be recited while the priests are chanting.</i> they should [stand] face to face with the priests,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11.</i> without looking at their faces.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:36, which explains that, at present, it is customary for both the priests and the congregation to pull their <i>tallitot</i> over their heads so that their attention will not be disturbed while the blessing is being recited. Nevertheless, the priests should extend their hands beyond their <i>tallitot</i>, so that there will be no separation between them and the people.</i>
If only one priest is blessing the people, he should begin reciting the blessing alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 5, based on Numbers 6:23: "This is how you should bless the children of Israel: 'Say to them...,'” our Sages explained that before the priests bless the people, someone must "Say to them" - i.e., invite them to recite the blessing. However, since the verse mentions "them," <i>Sotah</i> 38a teaches that this invitation is not extended to a single priest.</i> [Afterwards,] the leader of the congregation reads [the blessings] to him, word for word, as mentioned.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in Halachah 3.</i><br>If there are two or more [priests blessing the people],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Then the teaching mentioned above applies and</i> they do not begin reciting the blessing until the leader of the congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Tam protests against this statement, explaining that since the leader of the congregation is in the middle of the recitation of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, calling the priests - but not reading the blessings to them - would be considered an interruption, and therefore forbidden. Rather, another member of the congregation should call the priests.<br>The Rambam addresses himself to this question in one of his responsa and explains that there is nothing wrong with another member of the congregation calling the priests. However, there is no obligation to have this done. Since calling the priests is a necessary element of the repetition of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, it is not considered an interruption to the <i>chazan's</i> prayers. The<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:10) quotes the Rambam's view. Nevertheless, the Ramah suggests that the <i>chazan</i> should recite silently the paragraph beginning <i>Elo-heinu, v'Elo-hei avoteinu</i>, and in that way ensure that his call to the priests is not an interruption.</i> calls them, saying "<i>Kohanim</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Ashkenazic custom is that the <i>chazan</i> then continues, <i>Am Kedoshecha ka'amur</i> - "Your consecrated people, as it is said:...," and then recites the blessing, word for word, for the priests (Ramah).</i> They answer and respond <i>Y'varechecha</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the opinion mentioned in Halachah 3, that the <i>chazan</i> does not read the word <i>Y'varechecha</i> to the priests.</i> and then he reads [the blessings] to them, word for word, in the manner described above.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">in Halachah 3.</i>
How is the priestly blessing recited in the Temple? The priests ascend to the <i>duchan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slightly problematic statement. The Mishnah (<i>Middot</i> 2:6) and the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:3) describe the <i>duchan</i> as three steps separating the Courtyard of the Israelites from the Priestly Courtyard. There, the Levites would stand and accompany the Temple service with songs and music. No mention is made of the priests standing there. Also, in Halachah 14, the Rambam mentions that the priests would bless the people while standing on the steps of the Temple building.<br>The Radbaz (Vol. II, <i>Leshonot HaRambam</i>) offers a resolution to this difficulty, noting that priests with disqualifying physical deformities may not stand on the Temple steps (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 7:20). Thus, the majority of priests would stand on the Temple steps, and those with deformities would stand on the <i>duchan</i>.<br>The <i>Tiferet Yisrael</i> (<i>Middot</i> 2:4) states that most of the priests would stand on the Temple steps. If there was no room there, they would stand on the <i>duchan</i>.</i> after the priests have completed the service associated with the morning sacrifice offered daily.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Following the pattern established by Aharon, who blessed the people after completing the sacrificial offerings (Leviticus, Chapter 9).</i> They lift their hands above their heads<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as an act of deference to the <i>Shechinah</i>, which rests between their fingers (Rashi, <i>Sotah</i> 38a). Note the contrast to the practice outside the Temple, as described in Halachah 3.</i> with their fingers extended,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">holding them in the same manner as described in Halachah 3.</i> except the High Priest. He does not lift his hands above the <i>tzitz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>tzitz</i> refers to the golden plate worn by the High Priest on his forehead on which God's name is written (Exodus 28:37 . Accordingly, it is not proper for the High Priest to lift his hands above it (Rashi, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i><br>One person reads [the blessings] to them, word for word, in the same manner as outside the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3.</i> until they complete the three verses. The people do not respond ["Amen"] after each verse.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as is done outside the Temple.</i> Instead, in the Temple, [the priestly blessings] are read as a single blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is improper to recite "Amen" in the Temple, there is no need to make an interruption between verses (<i>Sotah</i> 40b).</i> When [the priests] conclude,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the recitation of the entire blessing</i> all the people respond, "Blessed be God, the Lord, the Lord of Israel to all eternity."<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Based on Nechemiah 9:5, this refrain was recited after each blessing recited in the Temple (<i>Sotah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>). The <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i> emphasizes that usually the person who recites a blessing in the Temple would add this refrain himself, and the listeners would respond: "Blessed be the name of His glorious kingdom forever and ever." However, since the priests themselves are forbidden to add to the priestly blessings (see Halachah 12), "Blessed be God..." would be recited by the people, and not by the priests.<br>Alternatively, because the people had already responded "Blessed be the name..." in response to each of the three recitations of God's name in the priestly blessings, then, when the blessings are concluded, they recite "Blessed be God..." (<i>Rishon Letzion, Berachot</i>).</i>
They<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the priests, when reciting the priestly blessing</i> recite [God's] name<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the name י-ה-ו-ה - as it is written. This is what is referred to as the "explicit name" in all sources. - As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i> 6:2, there are seven names of God which must be treated with reverence. However, the name י-ה-ו-ה is granted a greater degree of respect than the six others.<br>In the Guide to the Perplexed (Vol. I, Chapter 61), the Rambam explains that all the other names for God, refer to Him as He manifests Himself in a particular quality and thus, have parallels in human terms. In contrast, the name, י-ה-ו-ה, refers to Him, as He stands above any relation to human terms.</i> - i.e., the name י-ה-ו-ה , as it is written. This is what is referred to as the "explicit name" in all sources. In the country,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., any place outside the Temple premises. In certain contexts - see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 4:1 - this refers to any place outside Jerusalem.</i> it<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the name י-ה-ו-ה</i> is read [using another one of God's names]:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 71a states: "I am not referred to as [My name] is written. My name is written י-ה-ו-ה and it is pronounced אדני."<br>Exodus 3:15 states: "This is My name forever." The word "forever" (לעולם) can be interpreted as לעלם - "to hide." God's essential name is to remain hidden from man.</i> אדני, for only in the Temple is this name [of God] recited as it is written.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 38a offers the following interpretation of Exodus 20:21: "Wherever I allow My name to be mentioned, I will come to you and bless you": "In the place where I will come and bless you," - i.e., the Temple, the source of Divine blessing for the entire world - "I will allow My name to be mentioned" - the name י-ה-ו-ה, may be pronounced.<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sotah</i> 7:4), the Rambam writes: "It is forbidden...to recite God's explicit name or to speak about it at all..., except in the Temple."</i><br>After Shimon HaTzaddik<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Shimon HaTzaddik was the last surviving member of the <i>Anshei HaK'nesset HaGedolah</i>, the body of Sages who, under the direction of Ezra, laid the spiritual foundation for the return to Zion after the Babylonian exile.</i> died,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 39b relates that Shimon HaTzaddik's death represented a turning point in the history of the second Temple. After his passing, five miracles that had reflected the manifestation of God's presence in the Temple ceased.</i> the priests ceased reciting the [priestly] blessing using God's explicit name even in the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">and would recite a twelve-letter name of God instead (The Guide to the Perplexed, Vol. I, Chapter 62).</i> lest it be learned by a person lacking proper stature and moral conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the Guide to the Perplexed (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes that knowledge of this name would destroy these individuals' faith.<br>Rashi (<i>Kiddushin</i> 71a) and Rabbenu Manoach state that these individuals would use the mystic power of the name for improper purposes.</i> The Sages of the early generations would teach [this name]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the Guide to the Perplexed (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes:<br>[The name, י-ה-ו-ה]: It was not known to all men how to pronounce it, and which way each of the letters should be vocalized, whether any of the letters would be pronounced with a <i>dagesh</i>, and which one would be...I think that when it says that the Sages would teach the four-letter name to their sons and disciples, this does not mean the pronunciation of the name alone..., but also its uniqueness and Divine secret.</i> once in seven years,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) mentions another opinion, which states that this name would be taught twice in two years, but favors the opinion quoted by the Rambam, since ultimate discretion is appropriate for this great mystic secret.</i> only to their students and sons<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Our text of <i>Kiddushin</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) does not mention "sons." However, it is possible that the Rambam had a different text of that Talmudic passage.</i> [who had proven] their moral conduct. All this is in reverence for His great and awesome name.
Wherever the priestly blessing is recited, it is recited only in the holy tongue,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">quoting the blessing as it is recorded in the Torah</i> as [implied by Numbers 6:23]: "This is how you should bless the children of Israel."<br>We have learned the following [instructions]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (128:50) states that all these instructions are absolute requirements. A priest who cannot fulfill them - e.g., an aged priest who cannot stand unsupported - should leave the synagogue before the priests are called to recite the blessings.</i> from the tradition [passed on] from Moses, our teacher, may he rest in peace:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, the Rambam's use of the term <i>mipi hashmu’ah</i>, refers to <i>Halachah LeMoshe MiSinai</i> - laws learned as part of the oral tradition given to Moses on Mount Sinai (<i>Yad Malachi</i>).<br>This definition is somewhat difficult to accept in the present instance. A <i>halachah LeMoshe MiSinai</i> is not based on any specific verse in the Torah, while all the instructions in this halachah are derived by <i>Sotah</i> 38a based on different verses.</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - while standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> <i>loc. cit.</i>, explains that the recitation of the priestly blessing is considered to be equivalent to service in the Temple. Just as the Temple service was conducted while standing, the priestly blessing should also be recited while standing.<br>The <i>Eshkol</i> states that the congregation should also stand. Though this is the common custom, it is not an absolute requirement (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:23, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 51).</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - raising your hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as Aharon did when he blessed the people (Leviticus 9:23).</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - in the holy tongue.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The expression "This is how" implies that the priests should recite the exact words mentioned in the verse (<i>Sotah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - face to face.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as a sign of closeness and affection.</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - in a loud voice.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">From the words "Speak to them" (Numbers, <i>loc. cit.</i>), <i>Sotah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, derives the law that the priestly blessing should be recited in a conversational tone, as a person speaks to a colleague. From this statement, the <i>Sifre</i> states that the blessing should be recited in a tone which is audible to the entire congregation. However, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:23 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:53 emphasize that the priests should not shout or chant in an overly loud tone.</i><br>"This is how you should bless" - mentioning [God's] explicit name;<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the name י-ה-ו-ה</i> the latter<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the responsum of the <i>Nodah BiYhudah</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i>, Vol. I, 5). Note the <i>Sh'vut Ya'akov</i> (Vol. II, 1), who maintains that all these instructions are obligatory only in the Temple.</i> [applying only] when one is in the Temple, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">in the previous halachah.</i>
Wherever [they recite the blessing],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">whether in the Temple or outside its premises</i> the priests are not permitted to add other blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., "May God, Lord of your fathers, increase your numbers a thousandfold" (Deuteronomy 11:11) - in addition to the three verses [of the priestly blessing]. - <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 28b states that a priest should not think: "Since the Torah has given me permission to bless Israel, I will add a blessing of my own."</i> - e.g., "May God, Lord of your fathers, increase your numbers a thousandfold" (Deuteronomy 11:11)  - in addition to the three verses [of the priestly blessing]. [These additions may not be made] either silently or out loud, as [Deuteronomy 4:2] states: "Do not add to the matter."<br>While<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general commandment, prohibiting making additions to any of the mitzvot of the Torah.</i> a priest is ascending to the <i>duchan</i>, he recites [the following prayer]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This prayer, the blessing recited before blessing the people, and the prayer recited afterwards, are quoted (with minimal differences) from <i>Sotah</i> 39a-b. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:9) states that the priests should prolong the recitation of this prayer until the congregation concludes the recitation of "Amen" after the blessing <i>Modim</i>. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> states that the priests should conclude their prayer at the same time the <i>chazan</i> completes the blessing, so that the response "Amen" will also apply to their prayer.</i> when he leaves his place to<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:9) states that Ashkenazic custom is to begin reciting this prayer when one stands before the <i>heichal</i>.</i> ascend:<br>May it be Your will, God, our Lord and Lord of our fathers, that this blessing which You have commanded us to bless Your people, Israel, be a perfect blessing, that it not be marred by obstacles or iniquity,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the priests pray that their lack of proper intention should not spoil the blessing.</i> from now until eternity.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">These words are not found in our text of <i>Sotah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i></i><br>Before he turns to bless the community,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>, 128:11) and most later halachic authorities require the blessing to be recited after the priests turn to the people.</i> [a priest] should recite the blessing:<br>Blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since blessing the people fulfills a positive commandment, it is proper to recite a blessing beforehand, as we do before fulfilling most mitzvot.</i> are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has sanctified us with the holiness of Aharon,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since this mitzvah can be fulfilled only by the priests, the blessing differs from the blessing recited before fulfilling other mitzvot.</i> and commanded us to bless His people Israel with love.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The addition of the words "with love" is associated with the <i>Zohar's</i> (Vol. III, 147b) teaching: "Any priest who does not love [God's] people or is not beloved by His people should not bless the people."</i><br>Afterwards, he turns his face to the community and begins reciting the priestly blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 3.</i> When he turns his face from the community after completing [the recitation] of the blessings,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 4.</i> he recites [the following]:<br>We<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:15, states that the priests should prolong their recitation of this prayer so that they will conclude together with the<i>chazan's</i> conclusion of the blessing.</i> have carried out that which You have decreed upon us.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since the blessing is dependent on God (see Chapter 12, Halachah 7), the priests' actions are considered to be the fulfillment of a Divine decree (Maharsha, <i>Sotah</i> 39a).</i> Deal with us as You have promised us:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">i.e.,..<br><b>Look down from Your abode, from the heavens, and bless Your people, Israel -</b> Deuteronomy 26:15. In many communities, it is customary to add the last words of that verse, "a land flowing with milk and honey."<br>The recitation of this prayer is not considered to be an addition to the priestly blessings, because the priests have already lowered their hands from the position in which the blessing is recited (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:40).</i> Look down from Your abode, from the heavens, and bless Your people, Israel.
When the priests turn their faces to the community to bless them,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3.</i> and when they turn their faces from the community after blessing [them],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4.</i> they should turn only to the right.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if the synagogue faces east, the priests should turn to the south and then to the west, and then recite the blessing. Afterwards, they should turn to the north and back to the west (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:26, Mishnah Berurah 128:61).</i> Similarly, any turns which a person makes<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">in the Temple or prayer service</i> should always be to the right.
In the Temple, the priestly blessing would be recited once a day, after the [offering of] the morning sacrifice. [The priests] come and stand on the steps to the <i>Ulam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Ulam</i> refers to the Entrance Hall to the Temple building. There were twelve steps leading to it. (See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:4.)<br>Note the apparent contradiction between the Rambam's statements in this halachah and in Halachah 9, as explained in the commentary on Halachah 9. In <i>Hilchot Temidim U</i>Musafim 6:5, the Rambam also states that the priestly blessing was recited on the steps to the <i>Ulam</i>.</i> and recite the blessing, as mentioned above.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in Halachah 9.</i> However, outside the Temple, it is recited after every prayer service, except <i>Minchah</i>, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">in Halachah 1.</i><br>In all places,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">whether in the Temple or outside its premises</i> an effort is made<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 5:4) states: "The <i>chazan</i> should be an Israelite," the Rambam does not consider this to be a binding obligation.</i> that the person who reads the blessing to the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3, which relates that the priests recite the words of the blessing after someone else who reads them.</i> should be an Israelite, as [implied by Numbers 6:23]: "Say to them." This implies that the one who reads the blessing to them is not one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:22) states that if there is no alternative and a priest serves as the <i>chazan</i>, an Israelite should read the blessings to the priests, and the <i>chazan</i> should remain silent in his place or recite the priestly blessings, as explained in Chapter 15, Halachah 10.<br>If there is no Israelite who can read the blessings to the priests, some authorities maintain that the priests should recite the blessings without having another person read them to them. However, other opinions maintain that the <i>chazan</i> should read the blessings to the priests even though he is himself a priest (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:34).</i>

Chapter 15

There are six factors that prevent [a priest] from reciting the priestly blessings: [an inability] to pronounce [the blessings properly],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in this halachah</i> physical deformities,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 2</i> transgressions,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 3</i> [lack of] maturity,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 4</i> intoxication,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 4</i> and the ritual impurity of [the priest's] hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 5</i><br>[An inability] to pronounce [the blessings properly]:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Note the discussion of this difficulty with regard to the choice of a <i>chazan</i>, Chapter 8, Halachah 12.</i> What is implied? Those who cannot articulate the letters properly<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., those who read an aleph as an ayin and an ayin as an aleph - if the first word of the second priestly blessing, יאר is read with an ע instead of an א, the phrase את פניו אליך יאר ה' becomes a curse rather than a blessing.</i> - e.g., those who read an <i>aleph</i> as an <i>ayin</i> and an <i>ayin</i> as an <i>aleph</i>, or who pronounce <i>shibbolet</i> as <i>sibbolet</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., reading a <i>shin</i> as a <i>sin</i>. See Judges 12:6.</i> and the like<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., who read a <i>chet</i> like a <i>hay<br></i><br>should not recite the priestly blessings. - The later authorities explain that, if, as in many communities of the present day, the overwhelming majority of the people do not know how to differentiate between an ע and an א, a priest should not be disqualified because of this factor, since the meaning of the blessing is not changed (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 128:46). The <i>Turei Zahav</i> (128:90) states that even if a speech fault is common, but not overwhelmingly common - e.g., the substitution of a <i>sin</i> for a <i>shin</i> - a priest should not be disqualified, because such an error will not arouse the attention of the listeners. Nevertheless, this position is not accepted by all authorities.</i> - should not recite the priestly blessings.<br>Similarly, a stutterer or one who speaks unclearly, whose words cannot be understood by everyone,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">included in the category are people with other speech defects - e.g., a person who lisps</i> should not recite the priestly blessing.
Physical deformities: What is implied? A priest should not recite the priestly blessings if he has blemishes on his face, hands, or feet<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for all of these can be seen be the people while the priests recite the blessings. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:30-31) states that in places where the priests cover their faces and hands with their <i>tallitot</i> and wear socks while reciting the blessings, even these blemishes do not disqualify a priest from reciting the blessings.</i> - for example, his fingers are bent over, crooked, or covered with white spots<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">for they will attract the people's attention - and distract their concentration on the blessings.<br>A person whose spittle always dribbles when he speaks, and also a person who is blind in one eye should not recite the priestly blessings - for the same reasons as mentioned above. In places where the priests cover their faces, a priest with such a difficulty may also bless the people.</i> - for they will attract the people's attention.<br>A person whose spittle always dribbles when he speaks, and also a person who is blind in one eye should not recite the priestly blessings. However, if such a person was well known in his city and everyone was familiar<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, this refers to a person who lives within a city for at least thirty days (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i> 128:30).</i> with the person who was blind in one eye or whose spittle dribbled, he may recite the priestly blessing, for he will not attract their attention.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">with the person who was blind in one eye or whose spittle dribbled, he may recite the priestly blessing, for he will not attract their attention.</i><br> Similarly,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., for the same reasons</i> a person whose hands were colored purple or scarlet should not recite the priestly blessings. If the majority of the city's population is involved in such a profession,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">or if the people of the city are familiar with him (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, 128:32).</i> he is permitted, for this does not attract the people's attention.
Transgressions: What is implied? A priest who killed someone should never recite the priestly blessings, even if he repents,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:35) allows a priest who repents after committing such a sin to bless the people, so that "the door will not be closed to those who repent."</i> as [implied by Isaiah 1:15 which] states: "Your hands are full of blood,"<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the killing of a colleague</i> and states:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The order of these phrases in the Bible is the opposite of the order in which they are quoted by the Rambam.<br>"When you spread out your hands, I will hide My eyes from you." - Once a person has taken a life, God will not let him serve as a medium to convey blessing on the people.<br>A priest who served false gods, even if he was compelled to do so - See <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i> 5:2-4, which states that a person should sacrifice his life rather than submit to pressure to worship false gods. Nevertheless, if he fails to make this sacrifice, he is not punished by an earthly court for his sin.</i> "When you spread out your hands, I will hide My eyes from you."<br>A priest who served false gods, even if he was compelled to do so or did so inadvertently<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">or without knowing that the worship of this god was forbidden</i> - though he has repented<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">may never recite the priestly blessing, as [can be inferred from II Kings 23:9, which] states: "However, the priests of the high places shall not ascend [to God's altar in Jerusalem]." - This verse describes the efforts of King Josaiah to cleanse Judah from the pagan practices introduced by his father and grandfather.</i> - may never recite the priestly blessing, as [can be inferred from II Kings 23:9, which] states: "However, the priests of the high places shall not ascend [to God's altar in Jerusalem]." [The recitation of the priestly] blessings is equated to service [in the Temple],<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Rambam's comments, <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:13:<br>Any priest who served false gods, whether willingly or inadvertently - even though he sincerely repents - should never serve in the Temple.... If he transgressed and offered a sacrifice, his sacrifice is not a "pleasing fragrance."</i> as [Deuteronomy 10:8] states: "to serve Him and to bless in His<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Many of the later authorities maintain that this association is only a point of Rabbinic Law, and the mention of Biblical verses is only an <i>asmachta</i> (allusion). However, there is no indication of such a concept in the Rambam's words.</i> name."<br>Similarly, a priest who converted<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">without actually serving the false gods</i> to the worship of false gods<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 128:54 states that even someone who converts to Islam - which does not involve idol worship - is not allowed to recite the priestly blessings.</i> - even though he repents<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, as well, the Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>:37) allows a priest to bless the people if he repents.</i> - may never recite the priestly blessing. Other transgressions<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:26 notes that a person who desecrates the sanctity of the Sabbath is considered as one who adopted paganism, and, therefore, should not be allowed to recite the priestly blessing.<br>Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Iggerot Moshe, Orach Chayim</i> I, 33) states that present circumstances differ from those during the <i>Mishnah Berurah's</i> era, and, at present, priests who violate the Sabbath laws are not judged as severely and may bless the people. Nevertheless, he agrees that if restricting such individuals from reciting the priestly blessings may motivate them to increase their Sabbath observance, they may be prevented from blessing the people.</i> do not prevent [a priest from blessing the people].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 6 and 7.</i>
[Lack of] maturity: What is implied? A young priest should not recite the priestly blessings until he grows a full beard.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Chapter 8, Halachah 11, the expression "grows a full beard" is a primarily measure of age, whether the person actually grows a beard or not.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:34), based on <i>Tosafot, Chulin</i> 24b, states:<br>A minor who has not manifested signs of physical maturity should not recite the priestly blessing alone. However, he may recite the blessing together with his brethren, the priests, to learn and become educated.If he has manifested signs of physical maturity, he may recite the priestly blessing even while alone. However, he should do so only as a temporary measure, and not as a fixed practice, until he grows a full beard.<br>Intoxication: - The Hebrew יין literally means wine. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 128:55 notes that the Rambam speaks only about wine and does not mention other alcoholic beverages. Accordingly, he explains that a person who becomes drunk from other alcoholic beverages may recite the priestly blessing, unless he is so drunk that he has no control of himself.<br>It is significant that in Chapter 4, Halachah 17, when speaking about the prohibition against an intoxicated person praying, the Rambam states: "A person who is drunk should not pray.... When is a person considered drunk? When he cannot speak before a king." See also <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 1:1-2, where the Rambam describes the prohibition against serving in the Temple while intoxicated and mentions, albeit with differences between them, both a person who drank wine and one who became intoxicated from other alcoholic beverages.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham's</i> decision is not accepted by all authorities. (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:141.)</i><br>Intoxication: What is implied? A [priest] who drank a <i>revi'it</i> of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the minimum measure of wine that is considered to be able to influence a person's behavior.</i> at one time should not recite the priestly blessings until the effects of the wine wear off.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 1:5 states that if a person drank only a <i>revi'it</i> of wine, it is assumed that the wine's effects have worn off if he sleeps a little or walks a <i>mil</i>.</i> [This restriction was imposed] because an association was established between [reciting the priestly] blessing and service [in the Temple].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in the previous halachah.</i><br>Should [a priest] drink<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">only</i> a <i>revi'it</i> of wine on two different occasions<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., interrupting slightly between drinking the entire <i>revi'it</i></i> or mix a small amount of water in it, he is permitted [to recite the priestly blessings].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and serve in the Temple (<i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 1:1).</i> If he drank more than a <i>revi'it</i>, even though it was mixed with water or even though he drank it intermittently, he should not recite the priestly blessings until the effects of the wine wear off.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 1:5 states that sleeping or walking a <i>mil</i> is not sufficient to remove the effects of the wine, and one must wait until no signs of intoxication remain.<br>Because of the prohibition against reciting the priestly blessing while intoxicated, it is customary on Simchat Torah in Ashkenazic communities to recite the priestly blessing in the morning service and not during <i>Musaf</i>.</i><br>How much is a <i>revi'it</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Interestingly, although the Rambam also mentions a <i>revi'it</i> in Chapter 2, Halachah 17, he chooses to define its volume here.</i> [The volume of an area] two fingerbreadths by two fingerbreadths and two and seven tenths of a fingerbreadth high.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In modern measure, a <i>revi'it</i> is 86.4 milliliters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 149.3 milliliters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> Whenever the term "finger" is mentioned as a measure throughout the entire Torah, it refers to a thumbbreadth.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> 9:9. In modern measure, a thumbbreadth is 2 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 2.4 centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> The thumb is called <i>bohen yad</i> [in the Torah].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 8:23, 14:14.</i>
The ritual impurity of [the priest's] hands: What is implied? A priest who did not wash his hands should not recite the priestly blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo notes that in contrast to Chapter 4, Halachah 2, which mentions "the purification of the hands," here, the Rambam refers to "ritual impurity." He maintains that this choice of language was intended to imply that if a priest washed his hands in the morning, he need not wash them a second time unless they have become ritually impure. He also quotes a responsum of Rav Avraham, the Rambam's son, who explicitly states that a priest may rely on his morning washing.<br>Nevertheless, in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:6), Rav Yosef Karo quotes the opinion of Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Sotah</i> 39a), who require the priests to have their hands washed a second time as an additional measure of holiness.</i> Rather, he should wash his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. III, 146b) states that the Levites should wash the priests' hands. Since the Levites are themselves holy (Numbers 8:18), it is proper that they be the ones who convey this added holiness upon the priests. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) also mentions this practice.</i> to the wrist,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note our commentary Chapter 4, Halachah 2.</i> as is done when sanctifying the hands for the service in the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i>, Chapter 5.</i> as [Psalms 134:2] states: "Raise up your hands [in] holiness and bless God."<br>A<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Targum</i> to this verse also stresses its connection to the recitation of the priestly blessing.</i> <i>challal<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">a person born from relations between a priesta person born from relations between a priest and a divorcee or any other woman who he may not marry (see Leviticus 21:7), and, according to Rabbinic Law, a person born from a marriage between a priest and a woman who has undergone <i>chalitzah</i>. and a divorcee or any other woman who he may not marry (see Leviticus 21:7), and, according to Rabbinic Law, a person born from a marriage between a priest and a woman who has undergone <i>chalitzah</i>.</i></i> does not recite the priestly blessing, for he is not a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 19:14, where the Rambam says: "A <i>challal</i> is just like any other non-priest." The <i>Pri Chadash</i> writes that if a <i>challal</i> ascends to bless the people, he should be forced to descend.</i>
A priest who does not have any of the factors which hinder the recitation of the priestly blessings mentioned above<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in the previous five halachot.</i> should recite the priestly blessing, even though he is not a wise man or careful in his observance of the mitzvot. [This applies] even though the people spread unwholesome gossip about him,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., he is suspected of sinning.</i> or his business dealings are not ethical.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Bechorot</i> 45a, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128:40,41) adds that a priest who violates the specific mitzvot associated with the priesthood (the forbidden sexual relations mentioned in Leviticus 21:7 and the prohibitions against contracting impurity stemming from a corpse) may not recite the priestly blessings.<br>He should not be prevented from [reciting the priestly blessings] - even though he has not repented for his transgressions</i><br>He should not be prevented from [reciting the priestly blessings] because [reciting these blessings] is a positive mitzvah incumbent on each priest who is fit to recite them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's statements, it appears that the disqualifying factors mentioned above remove the mitzvah entirely from a priest.</i> We do not tell a wicked person: Increase your wickedness [by] failing to perform mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In other places, as well, we see the Rambam urging people to encourage the nonobservant to perform mitzvot. Note the conclusion of <i>Iggeret HaShmad</i>:<br>It is not fitting to push away or despise those who violate the Sabbath. Rather one should draw them close and encourage them to perform mitzvot.... Even if a person willingly sins, when he comes to the synagogue to pray, he should be accepted and not treated with disrespect.The Rabbis have based [this approach on the interpretation of] Solomon's words (Proverbs 6:30  "Do not scorn the thief when he steals" - i.e., do not scorn the sinners of Israel when they come discreetly to steal mitzvot.</i>
Do not wonder: "What good will come from the blessing of this simple person?" for the reception of the blessings is not dependent on the priests, but on the Holy One, blessed be He,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Gittin</i> 5:9 relates:<br>Do not say: "So and so is an adulterer... how can he bless me?"God replies: "Is it he that is blessing you? I'm the one who is blessing you."</i> as [Numbers 6:27] states: "And they shall set My name upon the children of Israel, and I shall bless them."<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the Torah, the Rashbam emphasizes that the text of priestly blessing itself express this point, stating, "May God bless you..., May God shine..., May God turn..."</i> The priests perform the mitzvah with which they were commanded,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">reciting the blessings</i> and God, in His mercies, will bless Israel as He desires.
The people standing behind the priests are not included in the blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">By standing behind the priests, they show that the blessing is not important to them. Hence, they are not included (Rashi, <i>Sotah</i> 38b). Also, as mentioned in Chapter 14, Halachah 11, the priestly blessing must be recited while the priests are standing face to face with those being blessed (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:37).</i> Those standing at their sides<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">even those who are standing parallel to the place of the priests</i> are included in the blessing. [Even] if there is a partition<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">even an iron wall - <i>Sotah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states: "Even an iron divider cannot separate between Israel and their Father in heaven."</i> - even an iron wall - between the priests and the people who are being blessed, since they are facing the priests,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Be'ur Halachah</i> explains that the people who are standing to the sides of the priests, but before them, should face the <i>heichal</i>. In principle, those who are standing parallel to the priests should turn to the side and face the priests. However, since it is not proper that people standing next to each other in the synagogue should face different directions, they need not shift the positions of their feet. It is sufficient for them to tilt their heads slightly towards the priests.</i> they are included in the blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam mentions in the following halachah, even people who do not attend the synagogue can be included in the priestly blessing.</i>
The priestly blessing is recited [only] when ten people [are present].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The priestly blessing is included among "the holy matters" that require a <i>minyan</i>. See Chapter 8, Halachot 4-6.</i> The priests can be included in that number.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even if there will not be ten people to respond "Amen," the priestly blessing can be recited.</i><br>If [the congregation in a particular] synagogue are all priests, they should all<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even though there will no one to read the words of the blessings to them, as mentioned in Chapter 14, Halachah 3. The question of whether the <i>chazan</i> should also recite the priestly blessing is discussed in the following halachah.</i> recite the priestly blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Unless there are a <i>minyan</i> of priests to respond "Amen," it is preferable that they all recite the blessings and none respond.<br>Who should they bless? Their brethren in the north and the south - i.e., those outside the synagogue. Even though they were unable to attend the synagogue, since they were prevented by forces beyond their control, they are included within the blessing.<br>The Rambam's statements are taken from <i>Sotah</i> 38b and the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Berachot</i> 5:4. However, the Rambam's choice of phraseology is more restrictive, mentioning only "the people in the north and the south," while these sources state, "their brethren in the fields."<br>According to his grandson, Rav Yitzchak HaNagid, this restriction was intentional. Since in the Rambam's time the Jews lived mostly to the east or west of Jerusalem, in most synagogues the <i>heichal</i> would be pointed in that direction, and thus, depending on the location of the synagogue, the people standing in one of these directions would be standing behind the priests. Therefore, they would not be included in the blessing.<br>Other authorities (e.g., the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 128:25) quote the text in the Babylonian Talmud without making any restrictions.</i> Who should they bless? Their brethren in the north and the south. Who will respond "Amen" to their [blessings]? The women and the children.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:99 states that even if there are no women or children present to answer "Amen," the blessing may be recited.</i> If more than ten priests remain besides those who ascend to the <i>duchan</i>, these ten [priests] respond "Amen"<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if there are twelve priests, two recite the blessings and ten respond "Amen," so that there will be a <i>minyan</i> responding "Amen."</i> and the remainder of the priests recite the blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:33 states that in such an instance, the <i>chazan</i> should not call out "<i>Kohanim</i>," since, according to many opinions, if the priests are not called to recite the blessings, they are not obligated to do so. Thus, the priests who did not recite the blessings will not be considered negligent in their fulfillment of the mitzvah.</i>
When there is no priest in the community other than the leader of the congregation,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 128:20, states that if other priests are present, a priest serving as the <i>chazan</i> should never recite the priestly blessing. The <i>Pri Chadash</i> takes issue with this decision, and allows him to recite the priestly blessings if he is confident that he will not err.</i> he should not recite the priestly blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">lest he become confused after completing the priestly blessing and be unable to complete the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 34b).</i> If he is sure that he can recite the priestly blessings and return to his prayers [without becoming confused],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:32 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:76, which state that at present, since the <i>chazan</i> prays from a siddur, he need not worry about being confused and may recite the priestly blessing.</i> he may [recite the priestly blessing].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that in such a case, the <i>chazan</i> should move from his place slightly during the blessing <i>R'tzey</i> (see the following halachah) and after concluding the blessing <i>Modim</i>, ascend to the <i>duchan</i> and recite the priestly blessing.<br>If there are no priests present at all - or according to Ashkenazic custom, in all services when the priestly blessing would be recited other than the <i>Musaf</i> service of the festivals.</i><br>If there are no priests present at all, when the leader of the congregation reaches [the blessing] <i>Sim shalom</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See the Commentary on Chapter 14, Halachah 4, for an explanation of the connection between the blessing <i>Sim shalom</i> and the priestly blessings.</i> he recites [the following prayer]:<br>Our<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">to commemorate the recitation of these blessings.</i> God and God of our fathers, bless us with the threefold blessing written in the Torah by Moses, Your servant, and recited by Aharon and his sons, the priests, Your consecrated people,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 127:8, which explains that the intent is that the priests themselves are a "consecrated people."</i> as it is<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Numbers 6:24-27</i> said:<br>May God bless you and keep you.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">May God bless you and keep you. May God shine His countenance upon you and be gracious to you. May God turn His countenance to you and grant you peace.</i><br>May God shine His countenance upon you and be gracious to you.<br>May God turn His countenance to you and grant you peace.<br>And they shall set My name upon the children of Israel and I will bless them.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> explain that the recitation of this verse parallels the prayer recited by the priests after completing the blessings (Chapter 14, Halachah 12). It is Ashkenazic custom not to recite this final verse (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 127:2).</i><br>The people do not respond "Amen" to these blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">for it is proper to recite "Amen" only after the blessings recited by the priests themselves. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 127:2 suggests reciting <i>ken yehi ratzon</i> - "So may it be Your will." Nevertheless, there are some communities which recite "Amen," based on the <i>Tanya Rabbati</i> 334 and a letter from Rav Hai Gaon.</i> He [resumes his recitation of the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>], beginning the recitation of [the blessing] <i>Sim shalom</i>.
A priest who recited the priestly blessings and went to another synagogue and found the congregation in the midst of prayer, before the [recitation of] the priestly blessings, should bless them.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> emphasizes that reciting the priestly blessings a second time is not considered to be a transgression of the prohibition of adding to the Torah's commandments. That prohibition is violated when one adds to the blessings themselves (see Chapter 14, Halachah 12), but not when one fulfills the mitzvah a number of times.</i> [He may recite the priestly blessings] several times during the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This statement has raised questions among the commentaries. The <i>Kiryat Sefer</i> explains that the priests recite <i>Y'varechecha</i> - the first word of the priestly blessings - directly after concluding the blessing (see Halachah 12) recited before blessing the people, without being prompted by the reader. They take the initiative, so that an interruption is not made between the recitation of the blessing before the performance of a mitzvah and its actual performance.<br>Study of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) shows that the Rambam himself had questions about this matter. In the first manuscripts, the Rambam writes that "the leader of the congregation recites <i>Y'varechecha</i>, and the priests read after him." This is also the reading in the popularly published edition of that text. However, in the later manuscripts of the Commentary on the Mishnah (see Rav Kapach's edition of that text), the Rambam changes his mind and states that the priests begin reciting <i>y'varechecha</i>.<br>In his <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 128), Rav Yosef Karo mentions these different views, and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (128:13) rules that the priests begin on their own initiative. The Ramah differs and states that it is Ashkenazic practice that this word is also recited in response to the <i>chazan</i>.</i><br>A priest who does not move from his place to ascend to the <i>duchan</i> when the leader of the congregation recites [the blessing] <i>R'tzey<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 14, Halachah 3.</i></i> should not ascend [to the <i>duchan</i>] during that prayer service.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if he was prevented from leaving his place by forces beyond his control (Radbaz, <i>Magen Avraham</i>).</i> However, if he moved [from his place], even though he did not reach the <i>duchan</i> until after the [conclusion of the blessing <i>R'tzey</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 128:13 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:27 state that even if he reaches the <i>duchan</i> after the <i>chazan</i> completes <i>R'tzey</i>, as long as he reaches there before the priests begin reciting the blessing, he may join them.</i> he may<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">but he is not obligated to (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 128:106).</i> ascend [the <i>duchan</i>] and bless [the people].
Any priest who does not ascend to the <i>duchan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even though he neglects [the performance] of [only] one commandment - is considered as if he violated three positive commandments, as Numbers 6:23-27 states: “This is how you shall bless the children of Israel,” “Say to them,” “And you shall set My name...” - The Rambam's statements are based on <i>Sotah</i> 38b. There, the Talmud states that one “violates three positive commandments.” The Rambam amends that statement, explaining that although there is only one commandment for the priests to bless the people, the Torah mentioned the commandment in three different ways to emphasize the importance of its fulfillment. Thus, the failure to bless the people is considered as nullifying the observance of three commands.<br>In <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (<i>Shoresh</i> 9), the Rambam cites this teaching as a classic example of a fundamental principle regarding the reckoning of the 613 mitzvot. Though the Talmud often states that many mitzvot are involved in the performance or transgression of a particular commandment, this does not mean that the mitzvah should be counted as more than one mitzvah when calculating the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. Rather, the Talmud means to say that the performance of this mitzvah is considered as important as if many mitzvot were involved.</i> - even though he neglects [the performance] of [only] one commandment - is considered as if he violated three positive commandments, as [Numbers 6:23-27] states: "This is how you shall bless the children of Israel," "Say to them," "And you shall set My name..."<br>Any priest who does not recite the priestly blessing will not be blessed, and any priest who blesses [the people] will be blessed, as [Genesis 12:3] states: "And I will bless those who bless you."<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chulin</i> 49a quotes a difference of opinion between Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Yishmael. Rabbi Akiva's statements are quoted by the Rambam, while Rabbi Yishmael explains that the blessing for the priests is derived from Numbers 6:27: “And you will set My name upon the children of Israel and I will bless them.” “The priests bless the Jews and God blesses the priests,... together with the Jews” (Rashi).<br>By quoting the verse from Genesis, “and I will bless those who bless you,” Rabbi Akiva emphasizes that the blessings bestowed upon the priests - as well as the blessing conveyed by the priests - stem from God's infinite goodness (<i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 10).<br>Blessed be the Merciful One who grants assistance.</i>
